10111-8{_}484{_}He read more books than they cared to study with him; was alone in the midst of them many a time, and passed nights over labours, useless perhaps, but in which they could not join him.
10111-8{_}485{_}His dear mistress divined his thoughts with her usual jealous watchfulness of affection; began to forebode a time when he would escape from his home nest; and at his eager protestations to the contrary, would only sigh and shake her head, knowing that some day her predictions would come true.
10111-8{_}486{_}Meanwhile evil fortune came upon the inmates of Castlewood Hall; brought thither by no other than Harry himself.
10111-8{_}487{_}In those early days, before Lady Mary Wortley Montague brought home the custom of inoculation from Turkey, smallpox was considered, as indeed it was, the most dreadful scourge of the world.
10111-8{_}488{_}The pestilence would enter a village and destroy half its inhabitants.
10111-8{_}489{_}At its approach not only the beautiful, but the strongest were alarmed, and those fled who could.
10111-8{_}490{_}One day in the year 1694 Dr. Tusher ran into Castlewood House with a face of consternation, saying that the malady had made its appearance in the village, that a child at the Inn was down with the smallpox.
10111-8{_}491{_}Now there was a pretty girl at this Inn, Nancy Sievewright, the blacksmith's daughter, a bouncing, fresh-looking lass, with whom Harry Esmond in his walks and rambles often happened to fall in; or, failing to meet her, he would discover some errand to be done at the blacksmith's, or would go to the Inn to find her.
10111-8{_}492{_}When Dr. Tusher brought the news that smallpox was at the Inn, Henry Esmond's first thought was of alarm for poor Nancy, and then of disquiet for the Castlewood family, lest he might have brought this infection to them; for the truth is, that Mr. Harry had been sitting that day for an hour with Nancy Sievewright, holding her little brother, who had complained of headache, on his knee; and had also since then been drawing pictures and telling stories to little Frank Castlewood, who had occupied his knee for an hour after dinner, and was never tired of Henry's tales of soldiers and horses.
10111-8{_}493{_}As luck would have it, Beatrix had not that evening taken her usual place, which generally she was glad enough to take, upon her tutor's lap.
10111-8{_}494{_}For Beatrix, from the earliest time, was jealous of every caress which was given to her little brother Frank.
10111-8{_}495{_}She would fling away even from her mother's arms if she saw Frank had been there before her; she would turn pale and red with rage if she caught signs of affection between Frank and his mother; would sit apart and not speak for a whole night, if she thought the boy had a better fruit or a larger cake than hers; would fling away a ribbon if he had one too; and from the earliest age, sitting up in her little chair by the great fireplace opposite to the corner where Lady Castlewood commonly sat at her embroidery, would utter childish sarcasm about the favour shown to her brother.
10111-8{_}496{_}These, if spoken in the presence of Lord Castlewood, tickled and amused his humour; he would pretend to love Frank best, and dandle and kiss him, and roar with laughter at Beatrix's jealousy.
10111-8{_}497{_}So it chanced that upon this very day, when poor Harry Esmond had had the blacksmith's son, and the peer's son, alike upon his knee, little Beatrix had refused to take that place, seeing it had been occupied by her brother, and, luckily for her, had sat at the further end of the room away from him, playing with a spaniel dog which she had--for which by fits and starts she would take a great affection--and talking at Harry Esmond over her shoulder, as she pretended to caress the dog, saying that Fido would love her, and she would love Fido and no one but Fido all the rest of her life.
10111-8{_}498{_}When, then, Dr. Tusher brought the news that the little boy at the Inn was ill with the smallpox, poor Harry Esmond felt a shock of alarm, not so much for himself as for little Frank, whom he might have brought into peril.
10111-8{_}499{_}Beatrix, who had by this time pouted sufficiently (and who, whenever a stranger appeared, began from infancy almost to play off little graces to catch his attention), her brother being now gone to bed, was for taking her place upon Esmond's knee: for though the Doctor was very attentive to her, she did not like him because he had thick boots and dirty hands (the pert young miss said), and because she hated learning the catechism.
10111-8{_}500{_}But as she advanced toward Esmond, he started back, and placed the great chair on which he was sitting between him and her--saying in French to Lady Castlewood, "Madam, the child must not approach me; I must tell you that I was at the blacksmith's to-day, and had his little boy upon my lap."
10111-8{_}501{_}"Where you took my son afterwards!"
10111-8{_}502{_}Lady Castlewood cried, very angry, and turning red.
10111-8{_}503{_}"I thank you, sir, for giving him such company.
10111-8{_}504{_}Beatrix," she continued in English, "I forbid you to touch Mr. Esmond.
10111-8{_}505{_}Come away, child--come to your room.
10111-8{_}506{_}Come to your room--I wish your reverence good-night"--this to Dr. Tusher--adding to Harry: "and you, sir, had not you better go back to your friends at the Inn?"
10111-8{_}507{_}Her eyes, ordinarily so kind, darted flashes of anger as she spoke; and she tossed up her head with the mien of a Princess, adding such words of reproach and indignation that Harry Esmond, to whom she had never once before uttered a syllable of unkindness, stood for some moments bewildered with grief and rage at the injustice of her reproaches.
10111-8{_}508{_}He turned quite white from red, and answered her in a low voice, ending his little speech with these words, addressed to Lord Castlewood: "Heaven bless you and yours for your goodness to me.
10111-8{_}509{_}I have tired her ladyship's kindness out, and I will go;" and sinking down on his knee, took the rough hand of his benefactor and kissed it.
10111-8{_}510{_}Here my lady burst into a flood of tears, and quitted the room, as my lord raised up Harry Esmond from his kneeling posture, put his broad hand on the lad's shoulder, and spoke kindly to him.
10111-8{_}511{_}Then, suddenly remembering that Harry might have brought the infection with him, he stepped back suddenly, saying, "Keep off, Harry, my boy; there is no good in running into the wolf's jaws, you know!"
10111-8{_}512{_}My lady, who had now returned to the room, said: "There is no use, my lord.
10111-8{_}513{_}Frank was on his knee as he was making pictures, and was running constantly from Henry to me.
10111-8{_}514{_}The evil is done, if any."
10111-8{_}515{_}"Not with me!"
10111-8{_}516{_}cried my lord.
10111-8{_}517{_}"I've been smoking, and it keeps off infection, and as the disease is in the village, plague take it, I would have you leave it.
10111-8{_}518{_}We'll go to-morrow to Wolcott."
10111-8{_}519{_}"I have no fear, my lord," said my lady; "it broke out in our house when I was an infant, and when four of my sisters had it at home, two years before our marriage, I escaped it."
10111-8{_}520{_}"I won't run the risk," said my lord; "I am as bold as any man, but I'll not bear that."
10111-8{_}521{_}"Take Beatrix with you and go," said my lady.
10111-8{_}522{_}"For us the mischief is done."
10111-8{_}523{_}Then my lord, calling away Tusher, bade him come to the oak parlour and have a pipe.
10111-8{_}524{_}When my lady and Harry Esmond were alone there was a silence of some moments, after which her ladyship spoke in a hard, dry voice of her objections to his intimacy with the blacksmith's daughter, and she added, "Under all the circumstances I shall beg my lord to despatch you from this house as quick as possible; and will go on with Frank's learning as well as I can.
10111-8{_}525{_}I owe my father thanks for a little grounding, and you, I am sure, for much that you have taught me.
10111-8{_}526{_}And--I wish you a good-night."
10111-8{_}527{_}And with this she dropped a stately curtsy, and, taking her candle, went away through the tapestry door which led to her apartments.
10111-8{_}528{_}Esmond stood by the fireplace, blankly staring after her.
10111-8{_}529{_}Indeed, he scarce seemed to see until she was gone; and then her image was impressed upon him, and remained forever fixed upon his memory.
10111-8{_}530{_}He saw her retreating, the taper lighting up her marble face, her scarlet lip quivering, and her shining golden hair.
10111-8{_}531{_}He went to his own room, and to bed, where he tried to read, as his custom was; but he never knew what he was reading.
10111-8{_}532{_}And he could not get to sleep until daylight, and woke with a violent headache, and quite unrefreshed.
10111-8{_}533{_}He had brought the contagion with him from the Inn, sure enough, and was presently laid up with the smallpox, which spared the Hall no more than it did the cottage.
10111-8{_}534{_}When Harry Esmond passed through the crisis of that malady, and returned to health again, he found that little Frank Esmond had also suffered and rallied after the disease, and that Lady Castlewood was down with it, with a couple more of the household.
10111-8{_}535{_}"It was a Providence, for which we all ought to be thankful," Dr. Tusher said, "that my lady and her son were spared, while death carried off the poor domestics of the house;" and he rebuked Harry for asking in his simply way, for which we ought to be thankful; that the servants were killed or the gentlefolk were saved?
10111-8{_}536{_}Nor could young Esmond agree with the Doctor that the malady had not in the least impaired my lady's charms, for Harry thought that her ladyship's beauty was very much injured by the smallpox.
10111-8{_}537{_}When the marks of the disease cleared away, they did not, it is true, leave scars on her face, except one on her forehead, but the delicacy of her complexion was gone, her eyes had lost their brilliancy and her face looked older.
10111-8{_}538{_}When Tusher vowed and protested that this was not so, in the presence of my lady, the lad broke out impulsively, and said, "It is true; my mistress is not near so handsome as she was!"
10111-8{_}539{_}On which poor Lady Castlewood gave a rueful smile, and a look into a little glass she had, which showed her, I suppose, that what the stupid boy said was only too true, for she turned away from the glass, and her eyes filled with tears.
10111-8{_}540{_}The sight of these on the face of the lady whom he loved best filled Esmond's heart with a soft of rage of pity, and the young blunderer sank down on his knees and besought her to pardon him, saying that he was a fool and an idiot, that he was a brute to make such a speech, he, who caused her malady; and Dr. Tusher told him that he was a bear indeed, and a bear he would remain, after which speech poor young Esmond was so dumb-stricken that he did not even growl.
10111-8{_}541{_}"He is my bear, and I will not have him baited, Doctor," my lady said, patting her hand kindly on the boy's head, as he was still kneeling at her feet.
10111-8{_}542{_}"How your hair has come off!--and mine, too," she added, with another sigh.
10111-8{_}543{_}"Madam, you have the dearest, and kindest, and sweetest face in the world, I think," the lad said.
10111-8{_}544{_}"Will my lord think so when he comes back?"
10111-8{_}545{_}the lady asked with a sigh, and another look at her glass.
10111-8{_}546{_}Then turning to her young son she said, "Come, Frank, come, my child.
10111-8{_}547{_}You are well, praised be Heaven.
10111-8{_}548{_}_Your_ locks are not thinned by this dreadful smallpox; nor your poor face scarred--is it, my angel?"
10111-8{_}549{_}Frank began to shout and whimper at the idea of such a misfortune, for from the very earliest time the young lord had been taught by his mother to admire his own beauty; and esteemed it very highly.
10111-8{_}550{_}At length, when the danger was quite over, it was announced that my lord and Beatrix would return.
10111-8{_}551{_}Esmond well remembered the day.
10111-8{_}552{_}My lady was in a flurry of fear.
10111-8{_}553{_}Before my lord came she went into her room, and returned from it with reddened cheeks.
10111-8{_}554{_}Her fate was about to be decided.
10111-8{_}555{_}Would my lord--who cared so much for physical perfection--find hers gone, too?
10111-8{_}556{_}A minute would say.
10111-8{_}557{_}She saw him come riding over the bridge, clad in scarlet, and mounted on his grey hackney, his little daughter beside him, in a bright riding dress of blue, on a shining chestnut horse.
10111-8{_}558{_}My lady put her handkerchief to her eyes, and withdrew it, laughing hysterically.
10111-8{_}559{_}She ran to her room again, and came back with pale cheeks and red eyes, her son beside her, just as my lord entered, accompanied by young Esmond, who had gone out to meet his protector, and to hold his stirrup as he descended from horseback.
10111-8{_}560{_}"What, Harry boy!"
10111-8{_}561{_}he exclaimed good-naturedly, "you look as gaunt as a greyhound.
10111-8{_}562{_}The smallpox hasn't improved your beauty, and you never had too much of it--ho!"
10111-8{_}563{_}And he laughed and sprang to the ground, looking handsome and red, with a jolly face and brown hair.
10111-8{_}564{_}Esmond, kneeling again, as soon as his patron had descended, performed his homage, and then went to help the little Beatrix from her horse.
10111-8{_}565{_}"Fie!
10111-8{_}566{_}how yellow you look," she said; "and there are one, two red holes in your face;" which indeed was very true, Harry Esmond's harsh countenance bearing as long as he lived the marks of the disease.
10111-8{_}567{_}My lord laughed again, in high good-humour, exclaiming with one of his usual oaths, "The little minx sees everything.
10111-8{_}568{_}She saw the dowager's paint t'other day, and asked her why she wore that red stuff--didn't you, Trix?
10111-8{_}569{_}And the Tower; and St. James's; and the play; and the Prince George; and the Princess Ann--didn't you, Trix?"
10111-8{_}570{_}"They are both very fat, and smelt of brandy," the child said.
10111-8{_}571{_}Papa roared with laughing.
10111-8{_}572{_}"Brandy!"
10111-8{_}573{_}he said.
10111-8{_}574{_}"And how do you know, Miss Pert?"
10111-8{_}575{_}"Because your lordship smells of it after supper, when I kiss you before I go to bed," said the young lady, who indeed was as pert as her father said, and looked as beautiful a little gipsy as eyes ever gazed on.
10111-8{_}576{_}"And now for my lady," said my lord, going up the stairs, and passing alone under the tapestry curtain that hung before the drawing-room door.
10111-8{_}577{_}Esmond always remembered that noble figure, handsomely arrayed in scarlet.
10111-8{_}578{_}Within the last few months he himself had grown from a boy to be a man, and with his figure his thoughts had shot up, and grown manly.
10111-8{_}579{_}After her lord's return, Harry Esmond watched my lady's countenance with solicitous affection, and noting its sad, depressed look realised that there was a marked change in her.
10111-8{_}580{_}In her eagerness to please her husband she practised a hundred arts which had formerly pleased him, charmed him, but in vain.
10111-8{_}581{_}Her songs did not amuse him, and she hushed them and the children when in his presence.
10111-8{_}582{_}Her silence annoyed him as much as her speech; and it seemed as if nothing she could do or say could please him.
10111-8{_}583{_}But for Harry Esmond his benefactress' sweet face had lost none of its charms.
10111-8{_}584{_}It had always the kindest of looks and smiles for him; not so gay and artless perhaps as those which Lady Castlewood had formerly worn, but out of her griefs and cares, as will happen when trials fall upon a kindly heart, grew up a number of thoughts and virtues which had never come into existence, had not her sorrow given birth to them.
10111-8{_}585{_}When Lady Castlewood found that she had lost the freshness of her husband's admiration, she turned all her thoughts to the welfare of her children, learning that she might teach them, and improving her many natural gifts and accomplishments that she might impart them.
10111-8{_}586{_}She made herself a good scholar of French, Italian, and Latin.
10111-8{_}587{_}Young Esmond was house-tutor under her or over her, as it might happen, no more having been said of his leaving Castlewood since the night before he came down with the smallpox.
10111-8{_}588{_}During my lord's many absences these school days would go on uninterruptedly: the mother and daughter learning with surprising quickness, the latter by fits and starts only, as suited her wayward humour.
10111-8{_}589{_}As for the little lord, it must be owned that he took after his father in the matter of learning, liked marbles and play and sport best, and enjoyed marshalling the village boys, of whom he had a little court; already flogging them, and domineering over them with a fine imperious spirit that made his father laugh when he beheld it, and his mother fondly warn him.
10111-8{_}590{_}Dr. Tusher said he was a young nobleman of gallant spirit; and Harry Esmond, who was eight years his little lordship's senior, had hard work sometimes to keep his own temper, and hold his authority over his rebellious little chief.
10111-8{_}591{_}Indeed, "Mr. Tutor," as my lady called Esmond, had now business enough on his hands in Castlewood house.
10111-8{_}592{_}He had his pupils, besides writing my lord's letters, and arranging his accounts for him, when these could be got from his indolent patron.
10111-8{_}593{_}Of the pupils the two young people were but lazy scholars, and as my lady would admit no discipline such as was then in use, my lord's son only learned what he liked, which was but little, and never to his life's end could be got to construe more than six lines of Virgil.
10111-8{_}594{_}Mistress Beatrix chattered French prettily, from a very early age; and sang sweetly, but this was from her mother's teaching, not Harry Esmond's, who could scarce distinguish one air from another, although he had no greater delight in life than to hear the ladies sing.
10111-8{_}595{_}He never forgot them as they used to sit together of the summer evenings, the two golden heads over the page, the child's little hand, and the mother's, beating the time with their voices rising and falling in unison.
10111-8{_}596{_}But these happy days were to end soon, and it was by Lady Castlewood's own decree that they were brought to a conclusion.
10111-8{_}597{_}It happened about Christmas time, Harry Esmond being now past sixteen years of age, that his old comrade, Tom Tusher, returned from school in London, a fair, well-grown and sturdy lad, who was about to enter college, with good marks from his school, and a prospect of after-promotion in the church.
10111-8{_}598{_}Tom Tusher's talk was of nothing but Cambridge now; and the boys examined each other eagerly about their progress in books.
10111-8{_}599{_}Tom had learned some Greek and Hebrew, besides Latin, in which he was pretty well skilled, and also had given himself to mathematical study under his father's guidance.
10111-8{_}600{_}Harry Esmond could not write Latin as well as Tom, though he could talk it better, having been taught by his dear friend the Jesuit Father, for whose memory the lad ever retained the warmest affection, reading his books, and keeping his swords clean.
10111-8{_}601{_}Often of a night sitting in the Chaplain's room, over his books, his verses, his rubbish, with which the lad occupied himself, he would look up at the window, wishing it might open and let in the good father.
10111-8{_}602{_}He had come and passed away like a dream; but for the swords and books Harry might almost think he was an imagination of his mind--and for two letters which had come from him, one from abroad, full of advice and affection, another soon after Harry had been confirmed by the Bishop of Hexton, in which Father Holt deplored his falling away from the true faith.
10111-8{_}603{_}But it would have taken greater persuasion than his to induce the boy to worship other than with his beloved mistress, and under her kind eyes he read many volumes of the works of the famous British divines of the last age.
10111-8{_}604{_}His mistress never tired of pursuing their texts with fond comments, or to urge those points which her fancy dwelt on most, or her reason deemed most important.
10111-8{_}605{_}In later life, at the University, Esmond pursued the subject in a very different manner, as was suitable for one who was to become a clergyman.
10111-8{_}606{_}But his heart was never much inclined towards this calling.
10111-8{_}607{_}He made up his mind to wear the cassock and bands as another man does to wear a breastplate and jack-boots, or to mount a merchant's desk for a livelihood--from obedience and necessity, rather than from choice.
10111-8{_}608{_}When Thomas Tusher was gone, a feeling of no small depression and disquiet fell upon young Esmond, of which, though he did not complain, his kind mistress must have guessed the cause: for, soon after, she showed not only that she understood the reason of Harry's melancholy, but could provide a remedy for it.
10111-8{_}609{_}All the notice, however, which she seemed to take of his melancholy, was by a gaiety unusual to her, attempting to dispel his gloom.
10111-8{_}610{_}She made his scholars more cheerful than ever they had been before, and more obedient, too, learning and reading much more than they had been accustomed to do.
10111-8{_}611{_}"For who knows," said the lady, "what may happen, and whether we may be able to keep such a learned tutor long?"
10111-8{_}612{_}Frank Esmond said he for his part did not want to learn any more, and cousin Harry might shut up his book whenever he liked, if he would come out a-fishing; and little Beatrix declared she would send for Tom Tusher, and _he_ would be glad enough to come to Castlewood, if Harry chose to go away.
10111-8{_}613{_}At last came a messenger from Winchester one day, bearer of a letter with a great black seal, from the Dean there, to say that his sister was dead, and had left her fortune among her six nieces, of which Lady Castlewood was one.
10111-8{_}614{_}When my lord heard of the news, he made no pretence of grieving.
10111-8{_}615{_}"The money will come very handy to furnish the music-room and the cellar, which is getting low, and buy your ladyship a coat, and a couple of new horses.
10111-8{_}616{_}And, Beatrix, you shall have a spinnet; and, Frank, you shall have a little horse from Hexton Fair; and, Harry, you shall have five pounds to buy some books," said my lord, who was generous with his own, and indeed with other folk's money.
10111-8{_}617{_}"I wish your aunt would die once a year, Rachel; we could spend your money, and all your sisters', too."
10111-8{_}618{_}"I have but one aunt--and--and I have another use for the money, my lord," said my lady.
10111-8{_}619{_}"Another use, my dear; and what do you know about money?"
10111-8{_}620{_}said my lord.
10111-8{_}621{_}"And what the devil is there that I don't give you which you want?"
10111-8{_}622{_}"I intend this money for Harry Esmond to go to college," says my lady.
10111-8{_}623{_}"You mustn't stay longer in this dull place, but make a name for yourself, and for us, too, Harry."
10111-8{_}624{_}"Is Harry going away?
10111-8{_}625{_}You don't mean to say you will go away?"
10111-8{_}626{_}cried out Frank and Beatrix in one breath.
10111-8{_}627{_}"But he will come back; and this will always be his home," cried my lady, with blue eyes looking a celestial kindness.
10111-8{_}628{_}"And his scholars will always love him, won't they?"
10111-8{_}629{_}"Rachel, you're a good woman!"
10111-8{_}630{_}exclaimed my lord, with an oath, seizing my lady's hand.
10111-8{_}631{_}"I wish you joy!"
10111-8{_}632{_}he continued, giving Harry Esmond a hearty slap on the shoulder.
10111-8{_}633{_}"I won't balk your luck.
10111-8{_}634{_}Go to Cambridge, boy, and when Tusher dies you shall have the living here, if you are not better provided by that time.
10111-8{_}635{_}We'll furnish the dining-room and buy the horses another year.
10111-8{_}636{_}I'll give thee a nag out of the stables; take any one except my hack and the bay gelding and the coach horses; and God speed thee, my boy!"
10111-8{_}637{_}"Have the sorrel, Harry; 'tis a good one.
10111-8{_}638{_}Father says 'tis the best in the stable," said little Frank, clapping his hands and jumping up.
10111-8{_}639{_}"Let's come and see him in the stable."
10111-8{_}640{_}And Harry Esmond in his delight and eagerness was for leaving the room that instant to arrange about his journey.
10111-8{_}641{_}The Lady Castlewood looked after him with sad penetrating glances.
10111-8{_}642{_}"He wishes to be gone already, my lord," said she to her husband.
10111-8{_}643{_}The young man hung back abashed.
10111-8{_}644{_}"Indeed, I would stay forever if your ladyship bade me," he said.
10111-8{_}645{_}"And thou wouldst be a fool for thy pains," said my lord.
10111-8{_}646{_}"Tut, tut, man.
10111-8{_}647{_}Go and see the world.
10111-8{_}648{_}Sow thy wild oats; and take the best luck that fate sends thee.
10111-8{_}649{_}I wish I were a boy again, that I might go to college and taste the Thumpington ale."
10111-8{_}650{_}"Indeed, you are best away," said my lady, laughing, as she put her hand on the boy's head for a moment.
10111-8{_}651{_}"You shall stay in no such dull place.
10111-8{_}652{_}You shall go to college and distinguish yourself as becomes your name.
10111-8{_}653{_}That is how you shall please me best; and--and if my children want you, or I want you, you shall come to us; and I know we may count on you."
10111-8{_}654{_}"May Heaven forsake me if you may not!"
10111-8{_}655{_}Harry said, getting up from his knee.
10111-8{_}656{_}"And my knight longs for a dragon this instant that he may fight," said my lady, laughing; which speech made Harry Esmond start, and turn red; for indeed the very thought was in his mind, that he would like that some chance should immediately happen whereby he might show his devotion.
10111-8{_}657{_}And it pleased him to think that his lady had called him "her knight," and often and often he recalled this to his mind, and prayed that he might be her true knight, too.
10111-8{_}658{_}My lady's bed-chamber window looked out over the country, and you could see from it the purple hills beyond Castlewood village, the green common betwixt that and the Hall, and the old bridge which crossed over the river.
10111-8{_}659{_}When Harry Esmond went away to Cambridge, little Frank ran alongside his horse as far as the bridge, and there Harry stopped for a moment, and looked back at the house where the best part of his life had been passed.
10111-8{_}660{_}It lay before him with its grey familiar towers, a pinnacle or two shining in the sun, the buttresses and terrace walls casting great blue shades on the grass.
10111-8{_}661{_}And Harry remembered all his life after how he saw his mistress at the window looking out on him in a white robe, the little Beatrix's chestnut curls resting at her mother's side.
10111-8{_}662{_}Both waved a farewell to him, and little Frank sobbed to leave him.
10111-8{_}663{_}Yes, he _would_ be his lady's true knight, he vowed in his heart; he waved her an adieu with his hat.
10111-8{_}664{_}The village people had good-bye to say to him, too.
10111-8{_}665{_}All knew that Master Harry was going to college, and most of them had a kind word and a look of farewell.
10111-8{_}666{_}I do not stop to say what adventures he began to imagine, or what career to devise for himself before he had ridden three miles from home.
10111-8{_}667{_}He had not read the Arabian tales as yet; but be sure that there are other folks who build castles in the air, and have fine hopes, and kick them down, too, besides honest Alnaschar.
10111-8{_}668{_}This change in his life was a very fine thing indeed for Harry, who rode away in company of my lord, who said he should like to revisit the old haunts of his youth, and so accompanied Harry to Cambridge.
10111-8{_}669{_}Their road lay through London, where my Lord Viscount would have Harry stay a few days to see the pleasures of the town before he entered upon his university studies, and whilst here Harry's patron conducted the young man to my lady dowager's house near London.
10111-8{_}670{_}Lady Isabella received them cordially, and asked Harry what his profession was to be.
10111-8{_}671{_}Upon hearing that the lad was to take orders, and to have the living of Castlewood when old Dr. Tusher vacated it, she seemed glad that the youth should be so provided for.
10111-8{_}672{_}She bade Harry Esmond pay her a visit whenever he passed through London, and carried her graciousness so far as to send a purse with twenty guineas for him to the tavern where he and his lord were staying, and with this welcome gift sent also a little doll for Beatrix, who, however, was growing beyond the age of dolls by this time, and was almost as tall as Lady Isabella.
10111-8{_}673{_}After seeing the town, and going to the plays, my Lord Castlewood and Esmond rode together to Cambridge, spending two pleasant days upon the journey.
10111-8{_}674{_}Those rapid new coaches that performed the journey in a single day were not yet established, but the road was pleasant and short enough to Harry Esmond, and he always gratefully remembered that happy holiday which his kind patron gave him.
10111-8{_}675{_}Henry Esmond was entered at Trinity College, Cambridge, to which famous college my lord had also in his youth belonged.
10111-8{_}676{_}My Lord Viscount was received with great politeness by the head master, as well as by Mr. Bridge, who was appointed to be Harry's tutor.
10111-8{_}677{_}Tom Tusher, who was by this time a junior Soph, came to take Harry under his protection; and comfortable rooms being provided for him, Harry's patron took leave of him with many kind words and blessings, and an admonition to have to behave better at the University than my lord himself had ever done.
10111-8{_}678{_}Thus began Harry Esmond's college career, which was in no wise different from that of a hundred other young gentlemen of that day.
10111-8{_}679{_}Meanwhile, while he was becoming used to the manners and customs of his new life and enjoying it thoroughly in his quiet way; at Castlewood Hall life was not so cheerful as it had been when he was there to note his mistress' sorrow or joy and act according to her need.
10111-8{_}680{_}Coming home to his dear Castlewood in the third year of his academic course, Harry was overjoyed to see again the kind blue eyes of his mistress, when she and the children came to greet him.
10111-8{_}681{_}He found Frank shooting up to be like his gallant father in looks and in tastes.
10111-8{_}682{_}He had his hawks, and his spaniel dog, his little horse, and his beagles; had learned to ride and to shoot flying, and had a small court made up of the sons of the huntsmen and woodsmen, over whom he ruled as imperiously as became the heir-apparent.
10111-8{_}683{_}As for Beatrix, Esmond found her grown to be taller than her mother, a slim and lovely young girl, with cheeks mantling with health and roses; with eyes like stars shining out of azure, with waving bronze hair clustered about the fairest young forehead ever seen; and a mien and shape haughty and beautiful, such as that of the famous antique statue of the huntress Diana.
10111-8{_}684{_}This bright creature was the darling and torment of father and mother.
10111-8{_}685{_}She intrigued with each secretly, and bestowed her fondness and withdrew it, plied them with tears, smiles, kisses, caresses; when the mother was angry, flew to the father; when both were displeased, transferred her caresses to the domestics, or watched until she could win back her parents' good graces, either by surprising them into laughter and good-humour, or appeasing them by submissive and an artful humility.
10111-8{_}686{_}She had been a coquette from her earliest days; had long learned the value of her bright eyes, and tried experiments in coquetry upon rustics and country 'squires until she should have opportunity to conquer a larger world in later years.
10111-8{_}687{_}When, then, Harry Esmond came home to Castlewood for his last vacation he found his old pupil shot up into this capricious beauty; her brother, a handsome, high-spirited, brave lad, generous and frank and kind to everybody, save perhaps Beatrix, with whom he was perpetually at war, and not from his, but her, fault; adoring his mother, whose joy he was.
10111-8{_}688{_}And Lady Castlewood was no whit less gracious and attractive to Harry than in the old days when as a lad he had first kissed her fair, protecting hand.
10111-8{_}689{_}Such was the group who welcomed Henry Esmond on his return from college.
10111-8{_}690{_}Not anticipating the future, not looking ahead, let us leave beautiful Beatrix, imperious young Frank, sweet Lady Castlewood, giving a glad welcome to their old friend and tutor.
10111-8{_}691{_}Truly we carry away a pretty picture as we finish this chapter of Esmond's youth.
10111-8{_}692{_}THE VIRGINIANS   [Illustration: WARRINGTON AND GEORGE WASHINGTON.]
10111-8{_}693{_}Henry Esmond, Esq., an officer who had served with the rank of Colonel during the wars of Queen Anne's reign, found himself at its close involved in certain complications, both political and private.
10111-8{_}694{_}For this reason Mr. Esmond thought best to establish himself in Virginia, where he took possession of a large estate conferred by King Charles I. upon his ancestor.
10111-8{_}695{_}Mr. Esmond previously to this had married Rachel, widow of the late Francis Castlewood, Baronet, by whom he had one daughter, afterwards Madame Warrington, whose twin sons, George and Henry Warrington, were known as the Virginians.
10111-8{_}696{_}Mr. Esmond called his American house Castlewood, from the family estate in England.
10111-8{_}697{_}The whole customs of Virginia, indeed, were fondly modelled after the English customs.
10111-8{_}698{_}The Virginians boasted that King Charles II.
10111-8{_}699{_}had been king in Virginia before he had been king in England.
10111-8{_}700{_}The resident gentry were connected with good English families and lived on their great lands after a fashion almost patriarchal.
10111-8{_}701{_}For its rough cultivation, each estate had a multitude of hands, who were subject to the command of the master.
10111-8{_}702{_}The land yielded their food, live stock and game.
10111-8{_}703{_}The great rivers swarmed with fish for the taking.
10111-8{_}704{_}Their ships took the tobacco off their private wharves on the banks of the Potomac or the James River, and carried it to London or Bristol, bringing back English goods and articles of home manufacture in return for the only produce which the Virginian gentry chose to cultivate.
10111-8{_}705{_}Their hospitality was boundless.
10111-8{_}706{_}No stranger was ever sent away from their gates.
10111-8{_}707{_}The question of slavery was not born at the time of which we write.
10111-8{_}708{_}To be the proprietor of black servants shocked the feelings of no Virginian gentleman; nor, in truth, was the despotism exercised over the negro race generally a savage one.
10111-8{_}709{_}The food was plenty; the poor black people lazy and not unhappy.
10111-8{_}710{_}You might have preached negro-emancipation to Madame Esmond of Castlewood as you might have told her to let the horses run loose out of the stables; she had no doubt but that the whip and the corn-bag were good for both.
10111-8{_}711{_}Having lost his wife, his daughter took the management of the Colonel and his estate, and managed both with the spirit and determination which governed her management of every person and thing which came within her jurisdiction.
10111-8{_}712{_}After fifteen years' residence upon his great Virginian estate the Colonel agreed in his daughter's desire to replace the wooden house in which they lived, with a nobler mansion which would be more fitting for his heirs to inherit.
10111-8{_}713{_}His daughter had a very high opinion indeed of her ancestry, and her father, growing exquisitely calm and good-natured in his serene declining years, humoured his child's peculiarities and interests in an easy bantering way.
10111-8{_}714{_}Truth to tell, there were few families in England with nobler connections than the Esmonds.
10111-8{_}715{_}The Virginians, Madame Rachel Warrington's sons, inherited the finest blood and traditions, and the rightful king of England had not two more faithful little subjects than the young twins of Castlewood.
10111-8{_}716{_}At Colonel Esmond's death, Madame Esmond, as she was thereafter called, proclaimed her eldest son, George, heir of the estate; and Harry, George's younger brother by half an hour, was instructed to respect his senior.
10111-8{_}717{_}All the household was also instructed to pay him honour, and in the whole family of servants there was only one rebel, Harry's foster-mother, a faithful negro woman who never could be made to understand why her child should not be first, who was handsomer and stronger and cleverer than his brother, as she vowed; though in truth, there was not much difference in the beauty, strength, or stature of the twins.
10111-8{_}718{_}In disposition, they were in many points exceedingly unlike; but in feature they resembled each other so closely that, but for the colour of their hair, it had been difficult to distinguish them.
10111-8{_}719{_}In their beds, and when their heads were covered with those vast ribboned nightcaps which our great and little ancestors wore, it was scarcely possible for any but a nurse or a mother to tell the one from the other child.
10111-8{_}720{_}Howbeit, alike in form, we have said that they differed in temper.
10111-8{_}721{_}The elder was peaceful, studious and silent; the younger was warlike and noisy.
10111-8{_}722{_}He was quick at learning when he began, but very slow at beginning.
10111-8{_}723{_}No threats of the ferule would provoke Harry to learn in an idle fit, or would prevent George from helping his brother in his lesson.
10111-8{_}724{_}Harry was of a strong military turn, drilled the little negroes on the estate, and caned them like a corporal, having many good boxing-matches with them, and never bearing malice if he was worsted; whereas George was sparing of blows, and gentle with all about him.
10111-8{_}725{_}As the custom in all families was, each of the boys had a special little servant assigned him; and it was a known fact that George, finding his little wretch of a blackamoor asleep on his master's bed, sat down beside it and brushed the flies off the child with a feather-fan, to the horror of old Gumbo, the child's father, who found his young master so engaged, and to the indignation of Madame Esmond, who ordered the young negro off to the proper officer for a whipping.
10111-8{_}726{_}In vain George implored and entreated, burst into passionate tears and besought a remission of the sentence.
10111-8{_}727{_}His mother was inflexible regarding the young rebel's punishment, and the little negro went off beseeching his young master not to cry.
10111-8{_}728{_}A fierce quarrel between mother and son ensued out of this event.
10111-8{_}729{_}Her son would not be pacified.
10111-8{_}730{_}He said the punishment was a shame--a shame; that he was the master of the boy, and no one--no, not his mother--had a right to touch him; that she might order _him_ to be corrected, and that he would suffer the punishment, as he and Harry often had, but no one should lay a hand on his boy.
10111-8{_}731{_}Trembling with passionate rebellion against what he conceived the injustice of the procedure, he vowed that on the day he came of age he would set young Gumbo free; went to visit the child in the slaves' quarters, and gave him one of his own toys.
10111-8{_}732{_}The black martyr was an impudent, lazy, saucy little personage, who would be none the worse for a whipping, as the Colonel, who was then living, no doubt thought; for he acquiesced in the child's punishment when Madame Esmond insisted upon it, and only laughed in his good-natured way when his indignant grandson called out:  "You let mamma rule you in everything, grandpapa."
10111-8{_}733{_}"Why so I do," says grandpapa.
10111-8{_}734{_}"Rachel, my love, the way in which I am petticoat-ridden is so evident that even this baby has found it out."
10111-8{_}735{_}"Then why don't you stand up like a man?"
10111-8{_}736{_}says little Harry, who always was ready to abet his brother.
10111-8{_}737{_}Grandpapa looked queerly.
10111-8{_}738{_}"Because I like sitting down best, my dear," he said.
10111-8{_}739{_}"I am an old gentleman, and standing fatigues me."
10111-8{_}740{_}On account of a certain apish drollery and humour which exhibited itself in the lad, and a liking for some of the old man's pursuits, the first of the twins was the grandfather's favourite and companion, and would laugh and talk out all his infantine heart to the old gentleman, to whom the younger had seldom a word to say.
10111-8{_}741{_}George was a demure, studious boy, and his senses seemed to brighten up in the library, where his brother was so gloomy.
10111-8{_}742{_}He knew the books before he could well-nigh carry them, and read in them long before he could understand them.
10111-8{_}743{_}Harry, on the other hand, was all alive in the stables or in the wood, eager for all parties of hunting and fishing, and promised to be a good sportsman from a very early age.
10111-8{_}744{_}The grandfather's ship was sailing for Europe once when the boys were children, and they were asked what present Captain Franks would bring them back?
10111-8{_}745{_}George was divided between books and a fiddle; Harry instantly declared for a little gun; and Madame Warrington (as she then was called) was hurt that her elder boy should have low tastes, and applauded the younger's choice as more worthy of his name and lineage.
10111-8{_}746{_}"Books, papa, I can fancy to be a good choice," she replied to her father, who tried to convince her that George had a right to his opinion, "though I am sure you must have pretty nigh all the books in the world already.
10111-8{_}747{_}But I never can desire--I may be wrong--but I never can desire, that my son, and the grandson of the Marquis of Esmond, should be a fiddler."
10111-8{_}748{_}"Should be a fiddlestick, my dear," the old Colonel answered.
10111-8{_}749{_}"Remember that Heaven's ways are not ours, and that each creature born has a little kingdom of thought of his own, which it is a sin in us to invade.
10111-8{_}750{_}Suppose George loves music?
10111-8{_}751{_}You can no more stop him than you can order a rose not to smell sweet, or a bird not to sing."
10111-8{_}752{_}"A bird!
10111-8{_}753{_}A bird sings from nature; George did not come into the world with a fiddle in his hand," says Mrs. Warrington, with a toss of her head.
10111-8{_}754{_}"I am sure I hated the harpsichord when a chit at Kensington school, and only learned it to please my mamma.
10111-8{_}755{_}Say what you will, I cannot believe that this fiddling is work for persons of fashion."
10111-8{_}756{_}"And King David who played the harp, my dear?"
10111-8{_}757{_}"I wish my papa would read him more, and not speak about him in that way," said Mrs. Warrington.
10111-8{_}758{_}"Nay, my dear, it was but by way of illustration," the father replied gently.
10111-8{_}759{_}It was Colonel's Esmond's nature always to be led by a woman, and he spoiled his daughter; laughing at her caprices, but humouring them; making a joke of her prejudices, but letting them have their way; indulging, and perhaps increasing, her natural imperiousness of character, which asserted itself to an unusual degree after her father's death.
10111-8{_}760{_}The Colonel's funeral was the most sumptuous one ever seen in the country.
10111-8{_}761{_}The little lads of Castlewood, almost smothered in black trains and hat bands, headed the procession, followed by Madame Esmond Warrington (as she called herself after her father's death), by my Lord Fairfax, by his Excellency the Governor of Virginia, by the Randolphs, the Careys, the Harrisons, the Washingtons, and many others, for the whole county esteemed the departed gentleman whose goodness, whose high talents, whose unobtrusive benevolence had earned for him the just respect of his neighbours.
10111-8{_}762{_}The management of the house of Castlewood had been in the hands of his daughter long before the Colonel slept the sleep of the just, for the truth is little Madame Esmond never came near man or woman but she tried to domineer over them.
10111-8{_}763{_}If people obeyed, she was their very good friend; if they resisted, she fought and fought until she or they gave in, and without her father's influence to restrain her she was now more despotic than ever.
10111-8{_}764{_}She exercised a rigid supervision over the estate; dismissed Colonel Esmond's English factor and employed a new one; built, improved, planted, grew tobacco, appointed a new overseer, and imported a new tutor for her boys.
10111-8{_}765{_}The little queen domineered over her little dominion, and over the princes her sons as well, thereby falling out frequently with her neighbours, with her relatives, and with her sons also.
10111-8{_}766{_}A very early difference which occurred between the queen and crown prince arose out of the dismissal of the lad's tutor, Mr. Dempster, who had also been the late Colonel's secretary.
10111-8{_}767{_}Upon his retirement George vowed he never would forsake his old tutor, and kept his promise.
10111-8{_}768{_}Another cause of dispute between George and his mother presently ensued.
10111-8{_}769{_}By the death of an aunt, the heirs of Mr. George Warrington became entitled to a sum of six thousand pounds, of which their mother was one of the trustees.
10111-8{_}770{_}She never could be made to understand that she was not the proprietor, but merely the trustee of this money; and was furious with the London lawyer who refused to send it over at her order.
10111-8{_}771{_}"Is not all I have my sons'?"
10111-8{_}772{_}she cried, "and would I not cut myself into little pieces to serve them?
10111-8{_}773{_}With the six thousand pounds I would have bought Mr. Boulter's estate and negroes, which would have given us a good thousand pounds a year, and made a handsome provision for my Harry."
10111-8{_}774{_}Her young friend and neighbour, Mr. Washington of Mount Vernon, could not convince her that the London agent was right, and must not give up his trust except to those for whom he held it.
10111-8{_}775{_}George Esmond, when this little matter was referred to him, and his mother vehemently insisted that he should declare himself, was of the opinion of Mr. Washington and Mr. Draper, the London lawyer.
10111-8{_}776{_}The boy said he could not help himself.
10111-8{_}777{_}He did not want the money; he would be very glad to give the money to his mother if he had the power.
10111-8{_}778{_}But Madame Esmond would not hear of these reasons.
10111-8{_}779{_}Here was a chance of making Harry's fortune--dear Harry, who was left with such a slender younger brother's pittance--and the wretches in London would not help him; his own brother, who inherited all his papa's estate, would not help him.
10111-8{_}780{_}To think of a child of hers being so mean at _fourteen years of age_!
10111-8{_}781{_}Into this state of mind the incident plunged Madame Warrington, and no amount of reasoning could bring her out of it.
10111-8{_}782{_}On account of the occurrence she at once set to work saving for her younger son, for whom she was eager to make a fortune.
10111-8{_}783{_}The fine buildings were stopped as well as the fine fittings which had been ordered for the interior of the new home.
10111-8{_}784{_}No more books were bought; the agent had orders to discontinue sending wine.
10111-8{_}785{_}Madame Esmond deeply regretted the expense of a fine carriage which she had from England, and only rode in it to church, crying out to the sons sitting opposite to her, "Harry, Harry!
10111-8{_}786{_}I wish I had put by the money for thee, my poor portionless child; three hundred and eighty guineas of ready money to Messieurs Hatchett!"
10111-8{_}787{_}"You will give me plenty while you live, and George will give me plenty when you die," says Harry gaily.
10111-8{_}788{_}"Not until he changes in _spirit_, my dear," says the lady grimly, glancing at her elder boy.
10111-8{_}789{_}"Not unless Heaven softens his heart and teaches him _charity_, for which I pray day and night; as Mountain knows; do you not, Mountain?"
10111-8{_}790{_}Mrs. Mountain, Ensign Mountain's widow, who had been a friend of Rachel Esmond in her school days, and since her widowhood had been Madame Esmond's companion in Castlewood house, serving to enliven many dull hours for that lady and enjoying thoroughly the home which Castlewood afforded her and her child.
10111-8{_}791{_}Mrs. Mountain, I say, who was occupying the fourth seat in the family coach, said, "Humph!
10111-8{_}792{_}humph!
10111-8{_}793{_}I know you are always disturbing yourself about this legacy, and I don't see that there is any need."
10111-8{_}794{_}"Oh, no!
10111-8{_}795{_}no need!"
10111-8{_}796{_}cries the widow, rustling in her silks; "of course I have no need to be disturbed, because my eldest born is _a disobedient son and an unkind brother;_ because he has an estate, and my poor Harry, bless him, but a _mess of pottage_."
10111-8{_}797{_}George looked despairingly at his mother until he could see her no more for eyes welled up with tears.
10111-8{_}798{_}"I wish you would bless me, too, O my mother!"
10111-8{_}799{_}he said, and burst into a passionate fit of weeping.
10111-8{_}800{_}Harry's arms were in a moment round his brother's neck, and he kissed George a score of times.
10111-8{_}801{_}"Never mind, George.
10111-8{_}802{_}I know whether you are a good brother or not.
10111-8{_}803{_}Don't mind what she says.
10111-8{_}804{_}She don't mean it."
10111-8{_}805{_}"I do mean it, child," cries the mother.
10111-8{_}806{_}"Would to Heaven--"  "_Hold your tongue, I say_!"
10111-8{_}807{_}roars out Harry.
10111-8{_}808{_}"It's a shame to speak so to him, ma'am."
10111-8{_}809{_}"And so it is, Harry," says Mrs. Mountain, shaking his hand.
10111-8{_}810{_}"You never said a truer word in your life."
10111-8{_}811{_}"Mrs. Mountain, do you dare to set my children against me?"
10111-8{_}812{_}cries the widow.
10111-8{_}813{_}"From this very day, madam--"  "Turn me and my child into the street?
10111-8{_}814{_}Do," says Mrs. Mountain.
10111-8{_}815{_}"That will be a fine revenge because the English lawyer won't give you the boy's money.
10111-8{_}816{_}Find another companion who will tell you black is white, and flatter you; it is not my way, madam.
10111-8{_}817{_}When shall I go?
10111-8{_}818{_}I shan't be long a-packing.
10111-8{_}819{_}I did not bring much into Castlewood house, and I shall not take much out."
10111-8{_}820{_}"Hush!
10111-8{_}821{_}the bells are ringing for church, Mountain.
10111-8{_}822{_}Let us try, if you please, and compose ourselves," said the widow, and she looked with eyes of extreme affection, certainly at one, perhaps at both, of her children.
10111-8{_}823{_}George kept his head down, and Harry, who was near, got quite close to him during the sermon, and sat with his arm round his brother's neck.
10111-8{_}824{_}From these incidents it may be clearly seen that Madame Esmond besides being a brisk little woman at business and ruling like a little queen in Castlewood was also a victim of many freaks and oddities, among them one of the most prominent being a great desire for flattery.
10111-8{_}825{_}There was no amount of compliment which she could not graciously receive and take as her due, and it was her greatest delight to receive attention from suitors of every degree.
10111-8{_}826{_}Her elder boy saw this peculiarity of his mother's disposition and chafed privately under it.
10111-8{_}827{_}From a very early day he revolted when compliments were paid to the little lady, and strove to expose them with his youthful satire; so that his mother would say gravely, "the Esmonds were always of a jealous disposition, and my poor boy takes after my father and mother in this."
10111-8{_}828{_}One winter after their first tutor had been dismissed Madame Esmond took them to Williamsburg for such education as the schools and colleges there afforded, and there they listened to the preaching and became acquainted with the famous Mr. Whitfield, who, at Madame Esmond's request, procured a tutor for the boys, by name Mr. Ward.
10111-8{_}829{_}For weeks Madame Esmond was never tired of hearing Mr. Ward's utterances of a religious character, and according to her wont she insisted that her neighbours should come and listen to him and ordered them to be converted to the faith which he represented.
10111-8{_}830{_}Her young favourite, Mr. George Washington, she was especially anxious to influence; and again and again pressed him to come and stay at Castlewood and benefit by the spiritual advantages there to be obtained.
10111-8{_}831{_}But that young gentleman found he had particular business which called him home or away from home, and always ordered his horse of evenings when the time was coming for Mr. Ward's exercises.
10111-8{_}832{_}And--what boys are just towards their pedagogue?--the twins grew speedily tired and even rebellious under their new teacher.
10111-8{_}833{_}They found him a bad scholar, a dull fellow, and ill-bred to boot.
10111-8{_}834{_}George knew much more Latin and Greek than his master; Harry, who could take much greater liberties than were allowed to his elder brother, mimicked Ward's manner of eating and talking, so that Mrs. Mountain and even Madame Esmond were forced to laugh, and little Fanny Mountain would crow with delight.
10111-8{_}835{_}Madame Esmond would have found the fellow out for a vulgar quack but for her son's opposition, which she, on her part, opposed with her own indomitable will.
10111-8{_}836{_}George now began to give way to a sarcastic method, took up Ward's pompous remarks and made jokes of them so that that young divine chafed and almost choked over his great meals.
10111-8{_}837{_}He made Madame Esmond angry, and doubly so when he sent off Harry into fits of laughter.
10111-8{_}838{_}Her authority was defied, her officer scorned and insulted, her youngest child perverted by the obstinate elder brother.
10111-8{_}839{_}She made a desperate and unhappy attempt to maintain her power.
10111-8{_}840{_}The boys were fourteen years of age, Harry being now taller and more advanced than his brother, who was delicate and as yet almost childlike in stature and appearance.
10111-8{_}841{_}The flogging method was quite a common mode of argument in these days.
10111-8{_}842{_}Our little boys had been horsed many a day by Mr. Dempster, their Scotch tutor, in their grandfather's time; and Harry, especially, had got to be quite accustomed to the practice, and made very light of it.
10111-8{_}843{_}But since Colonel Esmond's death, the cane had been laid aside, and the young gentlemen at Castlewood had been allowed to have their own way.
10111-8{_}844{_}Her own and her lieutenant's authority being now spurned by the youthful rebels, the unfortunate mother thought of restoring it by means of coercion.
10111-8{_}845{_}She took counsel of Mr. Ward.
10111-8{_}846{_}That athletic young pedagogue could easily find chapter and verse to warrant the course he wished to pursue,--in fact, there was no doubt about the wholesomeness of the practice in those days.
10111-8{_}847{_}He had begun by flattering the boys, finding a good berth and snug quarters at Castlewood, and hoping to remain there.
10111-8{_}848{_}But they laughed at his flattery, they scorned his bad manners, they yawned soon at his sermons; the more their mother favoured him, the more they disliked him; and so the tutor and the pupils cordially hated each other.
10111-8{_}849{_}Mrs. Mountain warned the lads to be prudent, and that some conspiracy was hatching against them; saying, "You must be on your guard, my poor boys.
10111-8{_}850{_}You must learn your lessons and not anger your tutor.
10111-8{_}851{_}Your mamma was talking about you to Mr. Washington the other day when I came into the room.
10111-8{_}852{_}I don't like that Major Washington, you know I don't.
10111-8{_}853{_}He is very handsome and tall, and he may be very good, but show me his wild oats I say--not a grain!
10111-8{_}854{_}Well, I happened to step in last Tuesday when he was here with your mamma, and I am sure they were talking about you, for he said, 'Discipline is discipline, and must be preserved.
10111-8{_}855{_}There can be but one command in a house, ma'am, and you must be the mistress of yours.'"
10111-8{_}856{_}"The very words he used to me," cries Harry.
10111-8{_}857{_}"He told me that he did not like to meddle with other folks' affairs, but that our mother was very angry, and he begged me to obey Mr. Ward, and to press George to do so."
10111-8{_}858{_}"Let him manage his own house, not mine," says George very haughtily.
10111-8{_}859{_}And the caution, far from benefiting him, only made the lad more scornful and rebellious.
10111-8{_}860{_}On the next day the storm broke.
10111-8{_}861{_}Words were passed between George and Mr. Ward during the morning study.
10111-8{_}862{_}The boy was quite disobedient and unjust.
10111-8{_}863{_}Even his faithful brother cried out, and owned that he was in the wrong.
10111-8{_}864{_}Mr. Ward bottled up his temper until the family met at dinner, when he requested Madame Esmond to stay, and laid the subject of discussion before her.
10111-8{_}865{_}He asked Master Harry to confirm what he had said; and poor Harry was obliged to admit all his statements.
10111-8{_}866{_}George, standing under his grandfather's portrait by the chimney, said haughtily that what Mr. Ward had said was perfectly correct.
10111-8{_}867{_}"To be a tutor to such a pupil is absurd," said Mr. Ward, making a long speech containing many scripture phrases, at each of which young George smiled scornfully; and at length Ward ended by asking her honour's leave to retire.
10111-8{_}868{_}"Not before you have punished this wicked and disobedient child," said Madame Esmond.
10111-8{_}869{_}"Punish!"
10111-8{_}870{_}exclaimed George.
10111-8{_}871{_}"Yes, sir, punish!
10111-8{_}872{_}If means of love and entreaty fail, other means must be found to bring you to obedience.
10111-8{_}873{_}I punish you now, rebellious boy, to guard you from greater punishment hereafter.
10111-8{_}874{_}The discipline of this family must be maintained.
10111-8{_}875{_}There can be but one command in a house, and I must be the mistress of mine.
10111-8{_}876{_}You will punish this refractory boy, Mr. Ward, as we have agreed, and if there is the least resistance on his part my overseer and servants will lend you aid."
10111-8{_}877{_}In the midst of his mother's speech George Esmond felt that he had been wronged.
10111-8{_}878{_}"There can be but one command in the house and you must be mistress.
10111-8{_}879{_}I know who said those words before you," George said slowly, and looking very white, "and--and I know, mother, that I have acted wrongly to Mr.
10111-8{_}880{_}Ward."
10111-8{_}881{_}"He owns it!
10111-8{_}882{_}He asks pardon!"
10111-8{_}883{_}cries Harry.
10111-8{_}884{_}"That's right, George!
10111-8{_}885{_}That's enough, isn't it?"
10111-8{_}886{_}"No, it is _not_ enough!
10111-8{_}887{_}I know that he who spares the rod spoils the child, ungrateful boy!"
10111-8{_}888{_}says Madame Esmond, with more references of the same nature, which George heard, looking very pale and desperate.
10111-8{_}889{_}Upon the mantelpiece stood a china cup, by which the widow set great store, as her father had always been accustomed to drink from it.
10111-8{_}890{_}George suddenly took it, and a strange smile passed over his pale face.
10111-8{_}891{_}"Stay one minute.
10111-8{_}892{_}Don't go away yet," he cried to his mother, who was leaving the room.
10111-8{_}893{_}"You are very fond of this cup, mother?"
10111-8{_}894{_}and Harry looked at him wondering.
10111-8{_}895{_}"If I broke it, it could never be mended, could it?
10111-8{_}896{_}My dear old grandpapa's cup!
10111-8{_}897{_}I have been wrong.
10111-8{_}898{_}Mr. Ward, I ask pardon.
10111-8{_}899{_}I will try and amend."
10111-8{_}900{_}The widow looked at her son indignantly.
10111-8{_}901{_}"I thought," she said, "I thought an Esmond had been more of a man than to be afraid, and--" Here she gave a little scream, as Harry uttered an exclamation and dashed forward with his hands stretched out towards his brother.
10111-8{_}902{_}George, after looking at the cup, raised it, opened his hand and let it fall on the marble slab before him.
10111-8{_}903{_}Harry had tried in vain to catch it.
10111-8{_}904{_}"It is too late, Hal," George said.
10111-8{_}905{_}"You will never mend that again--never.
10111-8{_}906{_}Now, mother, I am ready, as it is your wish.
10111-8{_}907{_}Will you come and see whether I am afraid?
10111-8{_}908{_}Mr. Ward, I am your servant.
10111-8{_}909{_}Your servant?
10111-8{_}910{_}Your slave!
10111-8{_}911{_}And the next time I meet Mr. Washington, Madame, I will thank him for the advice which he gave you."
10111-8{_}912{_}"I say, do your duty, sir!"
10111-8{_}913{_}cried Mrs. Esmond, stamping her little foot.
10111-8{_}914{_}And George, making a low bow to Mr. Ward, begged him to go first out of the room to the study.
10111-8{_}915{_}"Stop!
10111-8{_}916{_}For God's sake, mother, stop!"
10111-8{_}917{_}cried poor Hal.
10111-8{_}918{_}But passion was boiling in the little woman's heart, and she would not hear the boy's petition.
10111-8{_}919{_}"You only abet him, sir!"
10111-8{_}920{_}she cried.
10111-8{_}921{_}"If I had to do it myself, it should be done!"
10111-8{_}922{_}And Harry, with sadness and wrath in his countenance, left the room by the door through which Mr. Ward and his brother had just issued.
10111-8{_}923{_}The widow sank down in a great chair near it, and sat a while vacantly looking at the fragments of the broken cup.
10111-8{_}924{_}Then she inclined her head towards the door.
10111-8{_}925{_}For a while there was silence; then a loud outcry, which made the poor mother start.
10111-8{_}926{_}Mr. Ward came out bleeding from a great wound on his head, and behind him Harry, with flaring eyes, and brandishing a little ruler of his grandfather, which hung, with others of the Colonel's weapons, on the library wall.
10111-8{_}927{_}"I don't care.
10111-8{_}928{_}I did it," says Harry.
10111-8{_}929{_}"I couldn't see this fellow strike my brother; and as he lifted his hand, I flung the great ruler at him.
10111-8{_}930{_}I couldn't help it.
10111-8{_}931{_}I won't bear it; and if one lifts a hand to me or my brother, I'll have his life," shouts Harry, brandishing the hanger.
10111-8{_}932{_}The widow gave a great gasp and a sigh as she looked at the young champion and his victim.
10111-8{_}933{_}She must have suffered terribly during the few minutes of the boys' absence; and the stripes which she imagined had been inflicted on the elder had smitten her own heart.
10111-8{_}934{_}She longed to take both boys to it.
10111-8{_}935{_}She was not angry now.
10111-8{_}936{_}Very likely she was delighted with the thought of the younger's prowess and generosity.
10111-8{_}937{_}"You are a very naughty, disobedient child," she said in an exceedingly peaceable voice.
10111-8{_}938{_}"My poor Mr. Ward!
10111-8{_}939{_}What a rebel to strike you!
10111-8{_}940{_}Let me bathe your wound, my good Mr. Ward, and thank Heaven it was no worse.
10111-8{_}941{_}Mountain!
10111-8{_}942{_}Go fetch me some court-plaster.
10111-8{_}943{_}Here comes George.
10111-8{_}944{_}Put on your coat and waistcoat, child!
10111-8{_}945{_}You were going to take your punishment, sir, and that is sufficient.
10111-8{_}946{_}Ask pardon, Harry, of good Mr. Ward, for your wicked, rebellious spirit.
10111-8{_}947{_}I do, with all my heart, I am sure.
10111-8{_}948{_}And guard against your passionate nature, child, and pray to be forgiven.
10111-8{_}949{_}My son, oh my son!"
10111-8{_}950{_}Here with a burst of tears which she could no longer control the little woman threw herself on the neck of her first born, whilst Harry went up very feebly to Mr. Ward, and said, "Indeed, I ask your pardon, sir.
10111-8{_}951{_}I couldn't help it; on my honour, I couldn't; nor bear to see my brother struck."
10111-8{_}952{_}The widow was scared, as after her embrace she looked up at George's pale face.
10111-8{_}953{_}In reply to her eager caresses, he coldly kissed her on the forehead, and separated from her.
10111-8{_}954{_}"You meant for the best, mother," he said, "and I was in the wrong.
10111-8{_}955{_}But the cup is broken; and all the king's horses and all the king's men cannot mend it.
10111-8{_}956{_}There--put the fair side outwards on the mantelpiece, and the wound will not show."
10111-8{_}957{_}Then George went up to Mr. Ward, who was still piteously bathing his eye and forehead in the water.
10111-8{_}958{_}"I ask pardon for Hal's violence, sir," he said in great state.
10111-8{_}959{_}"You see, though we are very young, we are gentlemen, and cannot brook an insult from strangers.
10111-8{_}960{_}I should have submitted, as it was mamma's desire; but I am glad she no longer entertains it."
10111-8{_}961{_}"And pray, sir, who is to compensate me?"
10111-8{_}962{_}says Mr. Ward; "who is to repair the insult done to _me_?"
10111-8{_}963{_}"We are very young," says George, with another of his old-fashioned bows.
10111-8{_}964{_}"We shall be fifteen soon.
10111-8{_}965{_}Any compensation that is usual amongst gentlemen--"  "This, sir, to a minister of the Word!"
10111-8{_}966{_}bawls out Ward, starting up, and who knew perfectly well the lad's skill in fence, having a score of times been foiled by the pair of them.
10111-8{_}967{_}"You are not a clergyman yet.
10111-8{_}968{_}We thought you might like to be considered as a gentleman.
10111-8{_}969{_}We did not know."
10111-8{_}970{_}"A gentleman!
10111-8{_}971{_}I am a Christian, sir!"
10111-8{_}972{_}says Ward, glaring furiously, and clenching his great fists.
10111-8{_}973{_}"Well, well, if you won't fight, why don't you forgive?"
10111-8{_}974{_}says Harry.
10111-8{_}975{_}"If you won't forgive, why don't you fight?
10111-8{_}976{_}That's what I call the horns of a dilemma."
10111-8{_}977{_}And he laughed his jolly laugh.
10111-8{_}978{_}But this was nothing to the laugh a few days afterwards, when, the quarrel having been patched up along with poor Mr. Ward's eye, the unlucky tutor was holding forth according to his custom, but in vain.
10111-8{_}979{_}The widow wept no more at his harangues, was no longer excited by his eloquence.
10111-8{_}980{_}Nay, she pleaded headache, and would absent herself of an evening, on which occasions the remainder of the little congregation were very cold indeed.
10111-8{_}981{_}One day Ward, still making desperate efforts to get back his despised authority, was preaching on the necessity of obeying our spiritual and temporal rulers.
10111-8{_}982{_}"For why, my dear friends," he asked, "why are the governors appointed, but that we should be governed?
10111-8{_}983{_}Why are tutors engaged, but that children should be taught?"
10111-8{_}984{_}(Here a look at the boys.)
10111-8{_}985{_}"Why are rulers--" Here he paused, looking with a sad, puzzled face at the young gentlemen.
10111-8{_}986{_}He saw in their countenances the double meaning of the unlucky word he had uttered, and stammered and thumped the table with his fist.
10111-8{_}987{_}"Why, I say are rulers--rulers--"  "_Rulers_," says George, looking at Harry.
10111-8{_}988{_}"Rulers!"
10111-8{_}989{_}says Hal, putting his hand to his eye, where the poor tutor still bore marks of the late scuffle.
10111-8{_}990{_}"Rulers, o-ho!"
10111-8{_}991{_}It was too much.
10111-8{_}992{_}The boys burst out in an explosion of laughter.
10111-8{_}993{_}Mrs. Mountain, who was full of fun, could not help joining in the chorus; and little Fanny Mountain, who had always behaved very demurely and silently at these ceremonies, crowed again, and clapped her little hands at the others laughing, not in the least knowing the reason why.
10111-8{_}994{_}This could not be borne.
10111-8{_}995{_}Ward shut down the book before him; in a few angry but eloquent and manly words said he would speak no more in that place; and left Castlewood not in the least regretted by Madame Esmond, who had doted on him three months before.
10111-8{_}996{_}After the departure of her unfortunate spiritual adviser and chaplain, Madame Esmond and her son seemed to be quite reconciled: but although George never spoke of the quarrel with his mother, it must have weighed upon the boy's mind very painfully, for he had a fever soon after the last recounted domestic occurrences, during which illness his brain once or twice wandered, when he shrieked out, "Broken!
10111-8{_}997{_}Broken!
10111-8{_}998{_}It never, never, can be mended!"
10111-8{_}999{_}to the silent terror of his mother, who sat watching the poor child as he tossed wakeful upon his midnight bed.
10111-8{_}1000{_}That night, and for some days afterwards, it seemed very likely that poor Harry would become heir of Castlewood; but by Mr. Dempster's skilful treatment the fever was got over, the intermittent attacks diminished in intensity, and George was restored almost to health again.
10111-8{_}1001{_}A change of air, a voyage even to England, was recommended, but the widow had quarrelled with her children's relatives there, which made that trip impossible.
10111-8{_}1002{_}A journey to the north and east was determined upon, and the two young gentleman, with Mr. Dempster reinstated as their tutor, and a couple of servants to attend them, took a voyage to New York, and thence up the beautiful Hudson River to Albany, where they were received by the first gentry of the province; and thence into the French provinces, where they were hospitably entertained by the French gentry.
10111-8{_}1003{_}Harry camped with the Indians and took furs and shot bears.
10111-8{_}1004{_}George, who never cared for field sports, and whose health was still delicate, was a special favourite with the French ladies, who were accustomed to see very few young English gentlemen speaking the French language so readily as our young gentleman.
10111-8{_}1005{_}He danced the minuet elegantly.
10111-8{_}1006{_}He learned the latest imported French catches and songs and played them beautifully on his violin; and to the envy of poor Harry, who was absent on a bear-hunt, he even had an affair of honour with a young ensign, whom he pinked on the shoulder, and with whom he afterwards swore an eternal friendship.
10111-8{_}1007{_}When the lads returned home at the end of ten delightful months, their mother was surprised at their growth and improvement.
10111-8{_}1008{_}George especially was so grown as to come up to his younger-born brother.
10111-8{_}1009{_}The boys could hardly be distinguished one from another, especially when their hair was powdered; but that ceremony being too cumbrous for country-life, each of the lads commonly wore his own hair, George his raven black, and Harry his light locks, tied with a ribbon.
10111-8{_}1010{_}Now Mrs. Mountain had a great turn for match-making, and fancied that everybody had a design to marry everybody else.
10111-8{_}1011{_}As a consequence of this weakness she was able to persuade George Warrington that Mr. Washington was laying siege to Madame Esmond's heart, which idea was anything but agreeable to George's jealous disposition.
10111-8{_}1012{_}"I beg you to keep this quiet, Mountain," said George, with great dignity.
10111-8{_}1013{_}"Or you and I shall quarrel, too.
10111-8{_}1014{_}Never to any one must you mention such an absurd suspicion."
10111-8{_}1015{_}"Absurd!
10111-8{_}1016{_}Why absurd?
10111-8{_}1017{_}Mr. Washington is constantly with the widow.
10111-8{_}1018{_}She never tires of pointing out his virtues as an example to her sons.
10111-8{_}1019{_}She consults him on every question respecting her estate and its management.
10111-8{_}1020{_}There is a room at Castlewood regularly called Mr. Washington's room.
10111-8{_}1021{_}He actually leaves his clothes here, and his portmanteau when he goes away.
10111-8{_}1022{_}Ah, George, George!
10111-8{_}1023{_}The day will come when he won't go away!"
10111-8{_}1024{_}groaned Mrs. Mountain, and in consequence of the suspicions which her words aroused in him Mr. George adopted toward his mother's favourite a frigid courtesy, at which the honest gentleman chafed but did not care to remonstrate; or a stinging sarcasm which he would break through as he would burst through so many brambles on those hunting excursions in which he and Harry Warrington rode so constantly together; while George, retreating to his tents, read mathematics and French and Latin, or sulked in his book-room.
10111-8{_}1025{_}Harry was away from home with some other sporting friends when Mr. Washington came to pay a visit at Castlewood.
10111-8{_}1026{_}He was so peculiarly tender and kind to the mistress there, and received by her with such special cordiality, that George Warrington's jealousy had well-nigh broken out into open rupture.
10111-8{_}1027{_}But the visit was one of adieu, as it appeared.
10111-8{_}1028{_}Major Washington was going on a long and dangerous journey, quite to the western Virginia frontier and beyond it.
10111-8{_}1029{_}The French had been for some time past making inroads into our territory.
10111-8{_}1030{_}The government at home, as well as those of Virginia and Pennsylvania, were alarmed at this aggressive spirit of the lords of Canada and Louisiana.
10111-8{_}1031{_}Some of our settlers had already been driven from their holdings by Frenchmen in arms, and the governors of the British provinces were desirous of stopping their incursions, or at any rate to protest against their invasion.
10111-8{_}1032{_}We chose to hold our American colonies by a law that was at least convenient for its framers.
10111-8{_}1033{_}The maxim was, that whoever possessed the coast had a right to all the territory in hand as far as the Pacific; so that the British charters only laid down the limits of the colonies from north to south, leaving them quite free from east to west.
10111-8{_}1034{_}The French, meanwhile, had their colonies to the north and south, and aimed at connecting them by the Mississippi and the St. Lawrence, and the great intermediate lakes and waters lying to the westward of the British possessions.
10111-8{_}1035{_}In the year 1748, though peace was signed between the two European kingdoms, the colonial question remained unsettled, to be opened again when either party should be strong enough to urge it.
10111-8{_}1036{_}In the year 1753 it came to an issue on the Ohio River where the British and French settlers met.
10111-8{_}1037{_}A company called the Ohio Company, having grants from the Virginia government of lands along that river, found themselves invaded in their settlement's by French military detachments, who roughly ejected the Britons from their holdings.
10111-8{_}1038{_}These latter applied for protection to Mr. Dinwiddie, lieutenant governor of Virginia, who determined upon sending an ambassador to the French commanding officer on the Ohio demanding that the French should desist from their inroads upon the territories of his Majesty King George.
10111-8{_}1039{_}Young Mr. Washington jumped eagerly at the chance of distinction which this service afforded him, and volunteered to leave his home and his rural and professional pursuits in Virginia, to carry the governor's message to the French officer.
10111-8{_}1040{_}Taking a guide, an interpreter, and a few attendants, and following the Indian tracks, in the fall of the year 1753 the intrepid young envoy made his way from Williamsburg almost to the shores of Lake Erie, and found the French commander at Fort Le Boeuf.
10111-8{_}1041{_}That officer's reply was brief; his orders were to hold the place and drive all the English from it.
10111-8{_}1042{_}The French avowed their intention of taking possession of the Ohio.
10111-8{_}1043{_}And with this rough answer the messenger from Virginia had to return through danger and difficulty, across lonely forest and frozen river, shaping his course by the compass, and camping at night in the snow by the forest fires.
10111-8{_}1044{_}On his return from this expedition, which he had conducted with an heroic energy and simplicity, Major Washington was a greater favourite than ever with the lady of Castlewood.
10111-8{_}1045{_}She pointed him out as a model to both of her sons.
10111-8{_}1046{_}"Ah, Harry!"
10111-8{_}1047{_}she would say, "think of you, with your cock-fighting and your racing matches, and the Major away there in the wilderness, watching the French, and battling with the frozen rivers!
10111-8{_}1048{_}Ah, George!
10111-8{_}1049{_}learning may be a very good thing, but I wish my elder son were doing something in the service of his country!"
10111-8{_}1050{_}Mr. Washington on his return home began at once raising such a regiment as, with the scanty pay and patronage of the Virginian government, he could get together, and proposed with the help of these men-of-war to put a more peremptory veto upon the French invaders than the solitary ambassador had been enabled to lay.
10111-8{_}1051{_}A small force under another officer, Colonel Trent, had already been despatched to the west, with orders to fortify themselves so as to be able to resist any attack of the enemy.
10111-8{_}1052{_}The French troops greatly outnumbering ours, came up with the English outposts, who were fortifying themselves at a place on the confines of Pennsylvania where the great city of Pittsburg now stands.
10111-8{_}1053{_}A Virginian officer with but forty men was in no condition to resist twenty times that number of Canadians who appeared before his incomplete works.
10111-8{_}1054{_}He was suffered to draw back without molestation; and the French, taking possession of his fort, strengthened it and christened it by the name of the Canadian governor, Du Quesne.
10111-8{_}1055{_}Up to this time no actual blow of war had been struck.
10111-8{_}1056{_}It was strange that in a savage forest of Pennsylvania a young Virginian officer should fire a shot and waken up a war which was to last for sixty years, which was to cover his own country and pass into Europe, to cost France her American colonies, to sever ours from us, and create the great Western Republic; to rage over the old world when extinguished in the new; and of all the myriads engaged in the vast contest, to leave the prize of the greatest fame with him who struck the first blow!
10111-8{_}1057{_}He little knew of the fate in store for him.
10111-8{_}1058{_}A simple gentleman, anxious to serve his king and do his duty, he volunteered for the first service, and executed it with admirable fidelity.
10111-8{_}1059{_}In the ensuing year he took the command of the small body of provincial troops with which he marched to repel the Frenchmen.
10111-8{_}1060{_}He came up with their advanced guard and fired upon them, killing their leader.
