10111-8{_}1061{_}After this he had himself to fall back with his troops, and was compelled to capitulate to the superior French force.
10111-8{_}1062{_}On the 4th of July, 1754, the Colonel marched out with his troops from the little fort where he had hastily entrenched himself, and which they called Fort Necessity, gave up the place to the conqueror, and took his way home.
10111-8{_}1063{_}His command was over, his regiment disbanded after the fruitless, inglorious march and defeat.
10111-8{_}1064{_}Saddened and humbled in spirit, the young officer presented himself after a while to his old friends at Castlewood.
10111-8{_}1065{_}But surely no man can have better claims to sympathy than bravery, youth, good looks, and misfortune.
10111-8{_}1066{_}Mr. Washington's room at Castlewood was more than ever Mr. Washington's room now.
10111-8{_}1067{_}Madame Esmond raved about him and praised him in all her companies.
10111-8{_}1068{_}She more than ever pointed out his excellences to her sons, contrasting his sterling qualities with Harry's love of pleasure and George's listless musing over his books.
10111-8{_}1069{_}George was not disposed to like Mr. Washington any better for his mother's extravagant praises.
10111-8{_}1070{_}He coaxed the jealous demon within him until he must have become a perfect pest to himself and all his friends round about him.
10111-8{_}1071{_}He uttered jokes so deep that his simple mother did not know their meaning, but sat bewildered at his sarcasms.
10111-8{_}1072{_}Meanwhile the quarrel between the French and English North Americans, from being a provincial, had grown to be a national quarrel.
10111-8{_}1073{_}Reinforcements from France had already arrived in Canada, and English troops were expected in Virginia.
10111-8{_}1074{_}It was resolved to wrest from the French all the conquests they had made upon British dominion.
10111-8{_}1075{_}A couple of regiments were raised and paid by the king in America, and a fleet with a couple more was despatched from home under an experienced commander.
10111-8{_}1076{_}In February, 1755, Commodore Keppel, in the famous ship "Centurion," anchored in Hampton Roads with two ships of war under his command, and having on board General Braddock, his staff, and a part of his troops.
10111-8{_}1077{_}Mr. Braddock was appointed by the Duke.
10111-8{_}1078{_}A fleet of transports speedily followed him bringing stores, and men and money in plenty.
10111-8{_}1079{_}The arrival of the General and his little army caused a mighty excitement all through the provinces, and nowhere greater than at Castlewood.
10111-8{_}1080{_}Harry was off forthwith to see the troops under canvas at Alexandria.
10111-8{_}1081{_}The sight of their lines delighted him, and the inspiring music of their fifes and drums.
10111-8{_}1082{_}He speedily made acquaintance with the officers of both regiments; he longed to join in the expedition upon which they were bound, and was a welcome guest at their mess.
10111-8{_}1083{_}We may be sure that the arrival of the army and the approaching campaign formed the subject of continued conversation in the Castlewood family.
10111-8{_}1084{_}To make the campaign was the dearest wish of Harry's life.
10111-8{_}1085{_}He dreamed only of war and battle; he was forever with the officers at Williamsburg; he scoured and cleaned and polished all the guns and swords in the house; he renewed the amusements of his childhood and had the negroes under arms, but eager as he was to be a soldier, he scarcely dared touch on the subject with George, for he saw to his infinite terror how George, too, was occupied with military matters, and having a feudal attachment for his elder brother, and worshipping him with an extravagant regard, he gave way in all things to him as the chief, and felt that should George wish to make the campaign he would submit.
10111-8{_}1086{_}He took note that George had all the military books of his grandfather brought down from his book-shelves, and that he and Dempster were practising with the foils again; and he soon found that his fears were true.
10111-8{_}1087{_}Mr. Franklin of Philadelphia, having heard that Madame Esmond had beeves and horses and stores in plenty, which might be useful to General Braddock, recommended the General to conciliate her by inviting her sons to dinner, which he at once did.
10111-8{_}1088{_}The General and the gentlemen of his family made much of them, and they returned home delighted with their entertainment; and so pleased was their mother at the civility shown them that she at once penned a billet thanking his Excellency for his politeness, and begging him to fix the time when she might have the honour of receiving him at Castlewood.
10111-8{_}1089{_}Madame Esmond made her boys bearers of the letter in reply to his Excellency's message, accompanying her note with handsome presents for the General's staff and officers, which they were delighted to accept.
10111-8{_}1090{_}"Would not one of the young gentlemen like to see the campaign?"
10111-8{_}1091{_}the General asked.
10111-8{_}1092{_}"A friend of theirs, who often spoke of them--Mr. Washington, who had been unlucky in the affair of last year--had already promised to join him as aide-de-camp, and his Excellency would gladly take another young Virginian gentleman into his family."
10111-8{_}1093{_}Harry's eyes brightened and his face flushed at this offer.
10111-8{_}1094{_}He would like with all his heart to go, he cried out.
10111-8{_}1095{_}George said, looking hard at his younger brother, that one of them would be proud to attend his Excellency, whilst it would be the other's duty to take care of their mother at home.
10111-8{_}1096{_}Harry allowed his senior to speak.
10111-8{_}1097{_}However much he desired to go, he would not pronounce until George had declared himself.
10111-8{_}1098{_}He longed so for the campaign that the actual wish made him timid.
10111-8{_}1099{_}He dared not speak on the matter as he went home with George.
10111-8{_}1100{_}They rode for miles in silence, or strove to talk upon indifferent subjects, each knowing what was passing in the other's mind, and afraid to bring the awful question to an issue.
10111-8{_}1101{_}On their arrival at home the boys told their mother of General Braddock's offer.
10111-8{_}1102{_}"I know it must happen," she said; "at such a crisis in the country our family must come forward.
10111-8{_}1103{_}Have you--have you settled yet which of you is to leave me?"
10111-8{_}1104{_}and she looked anxiously from one to another, dreading to hear either name.
10111-8{_}1105{_}"The youngest ought to go, mother; of course I ought to go!"
10111-8{_}1106{_}cries Harry, turning very red.
10111-8{_}1107{_}"Of course, he ought," said Mrs. Mountain, who was present at their talk.
10111-8{_}1108{_}"The head of the family ought to go, mother," says George, adding: "You would make the best soldier, I know that, dearest Hal.
10111-8{_}1109{_}You and George Washington are great friends, and could travel well together, and he does not care for me, nor I for him, however much he is admired in the family.
10111-8{_}1110{_}But, you see, 'tis the law of honour, my Harry.
10111-8{_}1111{_}I must go.
10111-8{_}1112{_}Had fate given you the benefit of that extra half hour of life which I have had before you, it would have been your lot, and you would have claimed your right to go first, you know you would."
10111-8{_}1113{_}"Yes, George," said poor Harry; "I own I should."
10111-8{_}1114{_}"You will stay at home, and take care of Castlewood and our mother.
10111-8{_}1115{_}If anything happens to me, you are here to fill my place.
10111-8{_}1116{_}I should like to give way, my dear, as you, I know, would lay down your life to serve me.
10111-8{_}1117{_}But each of us must do his duty.
10111-8{_}1118{_}What would our grandfather say if he were here?"
10111-8{_}1119{_}The mother looked proudly at her two sons.
10111-8{_}1120{_}"My papa would say that his boys were gentlemen," faltered Madame Esmond, and left the young men, not choosing perhaps to show the emotion which was filling her heart.
10111-8{_}1121{_}It was speedily known amongst the servants that Mr. George was going on the campaign.
10111-8{_}1122{_}Dinah, George's foster-mother, was loud in her lamentations at losing him; Phillis, Harry's old nurse, was as noisy, because Master George, as usual, was preferred over Master Harry.
10111-8{_}1123{_}Sady, George's servant, made preparations to follow his master, bragging incessantly of the deeds which he would do; while Gumbo, Harry's boy, pretended to whimper at being left behind, though at home Gumbo was anything but a fire-eater.
10111-8{_}1124{_}But of all in the house Mrs. Mountain was the most angry at George's determination to go on the campaign.
10111-8{_}1125{_}She begged, implored, insisted that he should alter his determination; voted that nothing but mischief would come from his departure; and finally suggested that it was his duty to remain at home to protect his mother from the advances of Colonel Washington, whom she assured him she believed to desire a rich wife, and that if George would go away he would come back to find George Washington master of Castlewood.
10111-8{_}1126{_}As a proof of what she said she produced part of a letter written by Colonel Washington to his brother, in which his words seemed to the romantic Mrs. Mountain to bear out her belief.
10111-8{_}1127{_}This fragment, which she had found in the Colonel's room and with none too much honesty appropriated, she now showed to George, who after gazing at the document gave her a frightful look, saying, "I--I will return this paper to Mr.
10111-8{_}1128{_}Washington."
10111-8{_}1129{_}Mrs. Mountain was thoroughly scared then at what she had done and said, but it could not be taken back, so she was obliged to adjust herself to taking in good part whatever consequences might come of her dishonest act.
10111-8{_}1130{_}On the day set for Madame Esmond's entertainment to General Braddock the House of Castlewood was set out with the greatest splendour; and Madame Esmond arrayed herself in a much more magnificent dress than she was accustomed to wear, while the boys were dressed alike in gold-corded frocks, braided waistcoats, silver-hilted sword, and wore each a solitaire.
10111-8{_}1131{_}The General's new aide-de-camp was the first guest to arrive, and he and his hostess paced the gallery for some time.
10111-8{_}1132{_}She had much to say to him, and also to hear from him a confirmation of his appointment as aide-de-camp to General Braddock, and to speak of her son's approaching departure.
10111-8{_}1133{_}At length they descended the steps down to the rough lawn in front of the house, and presently the little lady re-entered her mansion, leaning upon Mr. Washington's arm.
10111-8{_}1134{_}Here they were joined by George, who came to them accurately powdered and richly attired, saluting his parent and his friend alike with respectful bows, according to the fashion of that time.
10111-8{_}1135{_}But George, though he made the lowest possible bow to Mr. Washington and his mother, was by no means in good humour with either of them, and in all his further conversation that day with Colonel Washington showed a bitter sarcasm and a depth of innuendo which the Colonel was at a loss to understand.
10111-8{_}1136{_}A short time after George's entrance into the Colonel's presence Harry answered back a remark of George's to the effect that he hated sporting by saying, "I say one thing, George."
10111-8{_}1137{_}"Say twenty things, Don Enrico," cries the other.
10111-8{_}1138{_}"If you are not fond of sporting and that, being cleverer than me, why shouldst thou not stop at home and be quiet, and let me go out with Colonel George and Mr. Braddock?
10111-8{_}1139{_}That's what I say," says Harry, flushing with excitement.
10111-8{_}1140{_}"One of our family must go because honour obliges it, and my name being number one, number one must go first," says George, adding, "One must stay, or who is to look after mother at home?
10111-8{_}1141{_}We cannot afford to be both scalped by Indians or fricasseed by French."
10111-8{_}1142{_}"Fricasseed by French," cries Harry; "the best troops of the world are Englishmen.
10111-8{_}1143{_}I should like to see them fricasseed by the French!
10111-8{_}1144{_}what a mortal thrashing you will give them!"
10111-8{_}1145{_}and the brave lad sighed to think he should not be present at the combat.
10111-8{_}1146{_}George sat down to the harpsichord and was playing when the Colonel re-entered, saying that his Excellency's coach would be here almost immediately, and asking leave to retire to his apartment, to put himself in a fit condition to appear before her ladyship's company.
10111-8{_}1147{_}As the widow was conducting Mr. Washington to his chamber, George gave way to a fit of wrath, ending in an explanation to his astonished brother of the reason of it, and telling him of Mrs. Mountain's suspicions concerning the Colonel's attitude towards their mother, which he confirmed by showing Harry the letter of Colonel Washington's which Mrs. Mountain had found and preserved.
10111-8{_}1148{_}But to go back to Madame Esmond's feast for his Excellency; all the birds of the Virginia air, and all the fish of the sea in season, and all the most famous dishes for which Madame Esmond was famous, and the best wine which her cellar boasted, were laid on the little widow's board to feed her distinguished guest and the other gentlemen who accompanied him.
10111-8{_}1149{_}The kind mistress of Castlewood looked so gay and handsome and spoke with such cheerfulness and courage to all her company that the few ladies who were present could not but congratulate Madame Esmond upon the elegance of the feast and upon her manner of presiding at it.
10111-8{_}1150{_}But they were scarcely in the drawing-room, when her artificial courage failed her, and she burst into tears, exclaiming, "Ah, it may be an honour to have Mr. Braddock in my house, but he comes to take one of my sons away from me.
10111-8{_}1151{_}Who knows whether my boy will return, or how?
10111-8{_}1152{_}I dreamed of him last night as wounded, with blood streaming from his side."
10111-8{_}1153{_}Meanwhile Mr. Washington was pondering deeply upon George's peculiar behaviour towards him.
10111-8{_}1154{_}The tone of freedom and almost impertinence which young George had adopted of late towards Mr. Washington had very deeply vexed and annoyed that gentleman.
10111-8{_}1155{_}There was scarce half a dozen years' difference of age between him and the Castlewood twins; but Mr. Washington had always been remarked for a discretion and sobriety much beyond his time of life, whilst the boys of Castlewood seemed younger than theirs.
10111-8{_}1156{_}They had always been till now under their mother's anxious tutelage, and had looked up to their neighbour of Mount Vernon as their guide, director, friend, as, indeed, almost everybody seemed to do who came in contact with the simple and upright young man.
10111-8{_}1157{_}Himself of the most scrupulous gravity and good-breeding, in his communication with other folks he appeared to exact, or, at any rate, to occasion, the same behaviour.
10111-8{_}1158{_}His nature was above levity and jokes: they seemed out of place when addressed to him.
10111-8{_}1159{_}He was slow of comprehending them: and they slunk as it were abashed out of his society.
10111-8{_}1160{_}"He always seemed great to me," says Harry Warrington, in one of his letters many years after the date of which we are writing; "and I never thought of him otherwise than as a hero.
10111-8{_}1161{_}When he came over to Castlewood and taught us boys surveying, to see him riding to hounds was as if he was charging an army.
10111-8{_}1162{_}If he fired a shot, I thought the bird must come down, and if he flung a net, the largest fish in the river were sure to be in it.
10111-8{_}1163{_}His words were always few, but they were always wise; they were not idle, as our words are; they were grave, sober and strong, and ready on occasion to do their duty.
10111-8{_}1164{_}In spite of his antipathy to him, my brother respected and admired the General as much as I did--that is to say, more than any mortal man."
10111-8{_}1165{_}Mr. Washington was the first to leave the jovial party which were doing so much honour to Madame Esmond's hospitality.
10111-8{_}1166{_}Young George Esmond, who had taken his mother's place when she left the dining-room, had been free with the glass and with the tongue.
10111-8{_}1167{_}He had said a score of things to his guest which wounded and chafed the latter, and to which Mr. Washington could give no reply.
10111-8{_}1168{_}Angry beyond all endurance, he left the table at length, and walked away through the open windows into the broad veranda or porch which belonged to Castlewood as to all Virginian houses.
10111-8{_}1169{_}Here Madame Esmond caught sight of her friend's tall frame as it strode up and down before the windows; and gave up her cards to one of the other ladies, and joined her good neighbour out of doors.
10111-8{_}1170{_}He tried to compose his countenance as well as he could, but found it so difficult that presently she asked, "Why do you look so grave?"
10111-8{_}1171{_}"Indeed, to be frank with you, I do not know what has come over George," says Mr. Washington.
10111-8{_}1172{_}"He has some grievance against me which I do not understand, and of which I don't care to ask the reason.
10111-8{_}1173{_}He spoke to me before the gentlemen in a way which scarcely became him.
10111-8{_}1174{_}We are going to the campaign together, and 'tis a pity we begin such ill friends."
10111-8{_}1175{_}"He has been ill.
10111-8{_}1176{_}He is always wild and wayward and hard to understand, but he has the most affectionate heart in the world.
10111-8{_}1177{_}You will bear with him, you will protect him.
10111-8{_}1178{_}Promise you will."
10111-8{_}1179{_}"Dear lady, I will do so with my life," Mr. Washington said heartily.
10111-8{_}1180{_}"You know I would lay it down cheerfully for you or any you love."
10111-8{_}1181{_}"And my father's blessing and mine go with you, dear friend!"
10111-8{_}1182{_}cried the widow.
10111-8{_}1183{_}As they talked, they had quitted the porch and were pacing a walk before the house.
10111-8{_}1184{_}Young George Warrington, from his place at the head of the table in the dining-room, could see them, and after listening in a very distracted manner for some time to the remarks of the gentlemen around him, he jumped up and pulled his brother Harry by the sleeve, turning him so that he, too, could see his mother and the Colonel.
10111-8{_}1185{_}Somewhat later, when General Braddock and the other guests had retired to their apartments, the boys went to their own room, and there poured out to one another their opinions respecting the great event of the day.
10111-8{_}1186{_}They would not bear such a marriage--No.
10111-8{_}1187{_}Was the representative of the Marquis of Esmond to marry the younger son of a colonial family, who had been bred up as a land surveyor--Castlewood and the boys at nineteen years of age handed over to the tender mercies of a step-father of three and twenty?
10111-8{_}1188{_}Oh, it was monstrous!
10111-8{_}1189{_}Harry was for going straightway to his mother, protesting against the odious match, and announcing that they would leave her forever if the marriage took place.
10111-8{_}1190{_}George had another plan for preventing it, which he explained to his admiring brother.
10111-8{_}1191{_}"Our mother," he said, "can't marry a man with whom one or both of us has been out on the field, and who has wounded us or killed us, or whom we have wounded or killed.
10111-8{_}1192{_}We must have him out, Harry."
10111-8{_}1193{_}Harry saw the profound truth conveyed in George's statement, and admired his brother's immense sagacity.
10111-8{_}1194{_}"No, George," says he, "you are right.
10111-8{_}1195{_}Mother can't marry our murderer; she won't be as bad as that.
10111-8{_}1196{_}And if we pink him, he is done for.
10111-8{_}1197{_}Shall I send my boy with a challenge to Colonel George now?"
10111-8{_}1198{_}"We can't insult a gentleman in our own house," said George with great majesty; "the laws of honour forbid such inhospitable treatment.
10111-8{_}1199{_}But, sir, we can ride out with him, and, as soon as the park gates are closed, we can tell him our mind."
10111-8{_}1200{_}"That we can, by George!"
10111-8{_}1201{_}cries Harry, grasping his brother's hand, "and that we will, too.
10111-8{_}1202{_}I say, Georgie--" Here the lad's face became very red, and his brother asked him what he would say.
10111-8{_}1203{_}"This is _my_ turn, brother," Harry pleaded.
10111-8{_}1204{_}"If you go to the campaign, I ought to have the other affair.
10111-8{_}1205{_}Indeed, indeed, I ought."
10111-8{_}1206{_}And he prayed for this bit of promotion.
10111-8{_}1207{_}"Again the head of the house must take the lead, my dear," George said with a superb air.
10111-8{_}1208{_}"If I fall, my Harry will avenge me.
10111-8{_}1209{_}But I must fight George Washington, Hal; and 'tis best I should; for, indeed, I hate him the worst.
10111-8{_}1210{_}Was it not he who counselled my mother to order that wretch, Ward, to lay hands on me?"
10111-8{_}1211{_}"Colonel Washington is my enemy especially.
10111-8{_}1212{_}He has advised one wrong against me, and he meditates a greater.
10111-8{_}1213{_}I tell you, brother, we must punish him."
10111-8{_}1214{_}The grandsire's old Bordeaux had set George's ordinarily pale countenance into a flame.
10111-8{_}1215{_}Harry, his brother's fondest worshipper, could not but admire George's haughty bearing and rapid declamation, and prepared himself, with his usual docility, to follow his chief.
10111-8{_}1216{_}So the boys went to their beds, the elder conveying special injunctions to his junior to be civil to all the guests so long as they remained under the maternal roof on the morrow.
10111-8{_}1217{_}The widow, occupied as she had been with the cares of a great dinner, followed by a great breakfast on the morning ensuing, had scarce leisure to remark the behaviour of her sons very closely, but at least saw that George was scrupulously polite to her favourite, Colonel Washington, as to all the other guests of the house.
10111-8{_}1218{_}Before Mr. Braddock took his leave he had a private audience with Madame Esmond, in which his Excellency formally arranged to take her son into his family; after which the jolly General good-naturedly shook hands with George, and bade George welcome and to be in attendance at Frederick three days hence; shortly after which time the expedition would set forth.
10111-8{_}1219{_}And now the great coach was again called into requisition, the General's escort pranced round it, the other guests and their servants went to horse.
10111-8{_}1220{_}As the boys went up the steps, there was the Colonel once more taking leave of their mother.
10111-8{_}1221{_}No doubt she had been once more recommending George to his namesake's care; for Colonel Washington said: "With my life.
10111-8{_}1222{_}You may depend on me," as the lads returned to their mother and the few guests still remained in the porch.
10111-8{_}1223{_}The Colonel was booted and ready to depart.
10111-8{_}1224{_}"Farewell, my dear Harry," he said.
10111-8{_}1225{_}"With you, George, 'tis no adieu.
10111-8{_}1226{_}We shall meet in three days at the camp."
10111-8{_}1227{_}George Warrington watched his mother's emotion, and interpreted it with a pang of malignant scorn.
10111-8{_}1228{_}"Stay yet a moment, and console our mamma," he said with a steady countenance, "only the time to get ourselves booted, and my brother and I will ride with you a little way, George."
10111-8{_}1229{_}George Warrington had already ordered his horses.
10111-8{_}1230{_}The three young men were speedily under way, their negro grooms behind them, and Mrs. Mountain, who knew she had made mischief between them and trembled for the result, felt a vast relief that Mr. Washington was gone without a quarrel with the brothers, without, at any rate, an open declaration of love to their mother.
10111-8{_}1231{_}No man could be more courteous in demeanour than George Warrington to his neighbour and name-sake, the Colonel, who was pleased and surprised at his young friend's altered behaviour.
10111-8{_}1232{_}The community of danger, the necessity of future fellowship, the softening influence of the long friendship which bound him to the Esmond family, the tender adieux which had just passed between him and the mistress of Castlewood, inclined the Colonel to forget the unpleasantness of the past days, and made him more than usually friendly with his young companion.
10111-8{_}1233{_}George was quite gay and easy: it was Harry who was melancholy now; he rode silently and wistfully by his brother, keeping away from Colonel Washington, to whose side he used always to press eagerly before.
10111-8{_}1234{_}If the honest Colonel remarked his young friend's conduct, no doubt he attributed it to Harry's known affection for his brother, and his natural anxiety to be with George now the day of their parting was so near.
10111-8{_}1235{_}They talked further about the war, and the probable end of the campaign; none of the three doubted its successful termination.
10111-8{_}1236{_}Two thousand veteran British troops with their commander must get the better of any force the French could bring against them.
10111-8{_}1237{_}The ardent young Virginian soldier had an immense respect for the experienced valour and tactics of the regular troops.
10111-8{_}1238{_}King George II.
10111-8{_}1239{_}had no more loyal subject than Mr. Braddock's new aide-de-camp.
10111-8{_}1240{_}So the party rode amicably together, until they reached a certain rude log-house, called Benson's, where they found a rough meal prepared for such as were disposed to partake.
10111-8{_}1241{_}A couple of Halkett's officers, whom our young gentlemen knew, were sitting under the porch, with the Virginian toddy bowl before them, and the boys joined them and sent for glasses and more toddy, in a very grown-up manner.
10111-8{_}1242{_}George called out to Colonel Washington, who was at the porch, to join his friends and drink, with the intention of drawing Mr. Washington into some kind of a disagreement.
10111-8{_}1243{_}The lad's tone was offensive, and resembled the manner lately adopted by him, which had so much chafed Mr. Washington.
10111-8{_}1244{_}He bowed, and said he was not thirsty.
10111-8{_}1245{_}"Nay, the liquor is paid for," says George; "never fear, Colonel."
10111-8{_}1246{_}"I said I was not thirsty.
10111-8{_}1247{_}I did not say the liquor was not paid for," said the young Colonel, drumming with his foot.
10111-8{_}1248{_}"When the King's health is proposed, an officer can hardly say no.
10111-8{_}1249{_}I drink the health of his Majesty, gentlemen," cried George.
10111-8{_}1250{_}"Colonel Washington can drink it or leave it.
10111-8{_}1251{_}The King!"
10111-8{_}1252{_}This was a point of military honour.
10111-8{_}1253{_}The two British officers of Halkett's, Captain Grace and Mr. Waring, both drank "The King."
10111-8{_}1254{_}Harry Warrington drank "The King."
10111-8{_}1255{_}Colonel Washington, with glaring eyes, gulped, too, a slight draught from the bowl.
10111-8{_}1256{_}Then Captain Grace proposed "The Duke and the Army," which toast there was likewise no gainsaying.
10111-8{_}1257{_}Colonel Washington had to swallow "The Duke and the Army."
10111-8{_}1258{_}"You don't seem to stomach the toast, Colonel," said George.
10111-8{_}1259{_}"I tell you again, I don't want to drink," replied the Colonel.
10111-8{_}1260{_}"It seems to me the Duke and the Army would be served all the better if their healths were not drunk so often."
10111-8{_}1261{_}"A British officer," said Captain Grace, with doubtful articulation," never neglects a toast of that sort, nor any other duty.
10111-8{_}1262{_}A man who refuses to drink the health of the Duke--hang me, such a man should be tried by a court-martial!"
10111-8{_}1263{_}"What means this language to me?
10111-8{_}1264{_}You are drunk, sir!"
10111-8{_}1265{_}roared Colonel Washington, jumping up and striking the table with his first.
10111-8{_}1266{_}"A cursed provincial officer say I'm drunk!"
10111-8{_}1267{_}shrieks out Captain Grace.
10111-8{_}1268{_}"Waring, do you hear that?"
10111-8{_}1269{_}"_I_ heard it, sir!"
10111-8{_}1270{_}cried George Warrington.
10111-8{_}1271{_}"We all heard it.
10111-8{_}1272{_}We entered at my invitation--the liquor called for was mine; the table was mine--and I am shocked to hear such monstrous language used at it as Colonel Washington has just employed towards my esteemed guest, Captain Waring."
10111-8{_}1273{_}"Confound your impudence, you infernal young jackanapes!"
10111-8{_}1274{_}bellowed out Colonel Washington.
10111-8{_}1275{_}"_You_ dare to insult me before British officers, and find fault with my language?
10111-8{_}1276{_}For months past I have borne with such impudence from you, that if I had not loved your mother--yes, sir, and your good grandfather and your brother--I would--" Here his words failed him, and the irate Colonel, with glaring eyes and purple face, and every limb quivering with wrath, stood for a moment speechless before his young enemy.
10111-8{_}1277{_}"You would what, sir," says George, very quietly, "if you did not love my grandfather, and my brother, and my mother?
10111-8{_}1278{_}You are making her petticoat a plea for some conduct of yours!
10111-8{_}1279{_}You would do what, sir, may I ask again?"
10111-8{_}1280{_}"I would put you across my knee and whip you, you snarling little puppy!
10111-8{_}1281{_}That's what I would do!"
10111-8{_}1282{_}cried the Colonel, who had found breath by this time, and vented another explosion of fury.
10111-8{_}1283{_}"Because you have known us all our lives, and made our house your own, that is no reason why you should insult either of us!"
10111-8{_}1284{_}here cried Harry, starting up.
10111-8{_}1285{_}"What you have said, George Washington, is an insult to me and my brother alike.
10111-8{_}1286{_}You will ask our pardon, sir!"
10111-8{_}1287{_}"Pardon!"
10111-8{_}1288{_}"Or give us the reparation that is due to gentlemen," continues Harry.
10111-8{_}1289{_}The stout Colonel's heart smote him to think that he should be at mortal quarrel, or called upon to shed the blood of one of the lads he loved.
10111-8{_}1290{_}As Harry stood facing him, with his fair hair, flushing cheeks, and quivering voice, an immense tenderness and kindness filled the bosom of the elder man.
10111-8{_}1291{_}"I--I am bewildered," he said.
10111-8{_}1292{_}"My words, perhaps, were very hasty.
10111-8{_}1293{_}What has been the meaning of George's behaviour to me for months back?
10111-8{_}1294{_}Only tell me, and, perhaps--"  The evil spirit was awake and victorious in young George Warrington; his black eyes shot out scorn and hatred at the simple and guileless gentleman before him.
10111-8{_}1295{_}"You are shirking from the question, sir, as you did from the toast just now," he said.
10111-8{_}1296{_}"I am not a boy to suffer under your arrogance.
10111-8{_}1297{_}You have publicly insulted me in a public place, and I demand a reparation."
10111-8{_}1298{_}"As you please, George Warrington--and God forgive you, George!
10111-8{_}1299{_}God pardon you, Harry!
10111-8{_}1300{_}for bringing me into this quarrel," said the Colonel, with a face full of sadness and gloom.
10111-8{_}1301{_}Harry hung his head, but George continued with perfect calmness: "I, sir?
10111-8{_}1302{_}It was not I who called names, who talked of a cane, who insulted a gentleman in a public place before the gentlemen of the army.
10111-8{_}1303{_}It is not the first time you have chosen to take me for a negro, and talked of the whip for me."
10111-8{_}1304{_}The Colonel started back, turning very red, and as if struck by a sudden remembrance.
10111-8{_}1305{_}"Great heavens, George!
10111-8{_}1306{_}is it that boyish quarrel you are still recalling?"
10111-8{_}1307{_}"Who made you overseer of Castlewood?"
10111-8{_}1308{_}said the boy, grinding his teeth.
10111-8{_}1309{_}"I am not your slave, George Washington, and I never will be.
10111-8{_}1310{_}I hated you then, and I hate you now.
10111-8{_}1311{_}And you have insulted me, and I am a gentleman, and so are you.
10111-8{_}1312{_}Is that not enough?"
10111-8{_}1313{_}"Too much, only too much," said the Colonel, with a genuine grief on his face, and at his heart "Do you bear malice, too, Harry?
10111-8{_}1314{_}I had not thought this of thee!"
10111-8{_}1315{_}"I stand by my brother," said Harry, turning away from the Colonel's look, and grasping George's hand.
10111-8{_}1316{_}The sadness on their adversary's face did not depart.
10111-8{_}1317{_}"Heaven be good to us!
10111-8{_}1318{_}'Tis all clear now," he muttered to himself.
10111-8{_}1319{_}"The time to write a few letters, and I am at your service, Mr. Warrington," he said.
10111-8{_}1320{_}"You have your own pistols at your saddle.
10111-8{_}1321{_}I did not ride out with any; but will send Sady back for mine.
10111-8{_}1322{_}That will give you time enough, Colonel Washington?"
10111-8{_}1323{_}"Plenty of time, sir."
10111-8{_}1324{_}And each gentleman made the other a low bow, and, putting his arm in his brother's, George walked away.
10111-8{_}1325{_}The Virginian officer looked towards Captain Benson, the master of the tavern, saying, "Captain Benson, you are an old frontier man, and an officer of ours, before you turned farmer and taverner.
10111-8{_}1326{_}You will help me in this matter with yonder young gentleman?"
10111-8{_}1327{_}said the Colonel.
10111-8{_}1328{_}"I'll stand by and see fair play, Colonel.
10111-8{_}1329{_}I won't have any hand in it, beyond seeing fair play.
10111-8{_}1330{_}You ain't a-goin' to be very hard with them poor boys?
10111-8{_}1331{_}Though I seen 'em both shoot; the fair one hunts well, as you know, but the old one's a wonder at an ace of spades."
10111-8{_}1332{_}"Will you be pleased to send my man with my valise, Captain, into any private room which you can spare me?
10111-8{_}1333{_}I must write a few letters before this business comes on.
10111-8{_}1334{_}God grant it were well over!"
10111-8{_}1335{_}And the Captain led the Colonel into a room of his house where he remained occupied with gloomy preparations for the ensuing meeting.
10111-8{_}1336{_}His adversary in the other room also thought fit to make his testamentary dispositions, too, dictated by his own obedient brother and secretary, a grandiloquent letter to his mother, of whom, and by that writing, he took a solemn farewell.
10111-8{_}1337{_}She would hardly, he supposed, pursue _the scheme which she had in view_, after the event of that morning, should he fall, as probably would be the case.
10111-8{_}1338{_}"My dear, dear George, don't say that!"
10111-8{_}1339{_}cried the affrighted secretary.
10111-8{_}1340{_}"As probably will be the case," George persisted with great majesty.
10111-8{_}1341{_}"You know what a good shot Colonel George is, Harry.
10111-8{_}1342{_}I, myself, am pretty fair at a mark, and 'tis probable that one or both of us will drop--I scarcely suppose you will carry out the intentions you have at present in view."
10111-8{_}1343{_}This was uttered in a tone of still greater bitterness than George had used even in the previous phrase, and he added in a tone of surprise: "Why, Harry, what have you been writing, and who taught thee to spell?"
10111-8{_}1344{_}Harry had written the last words "in view," in _vew_, and a great blot of salt water from his honest, boyish eyes may have obliterated some other bad spelling.
10111-8{_}1345{_}"I can't think about the spelling now, Georgy," whimpered George's clerk.
10111-8{_}1346{_}"I'm too miserable for that.
10111-8{_}1347{_}I begin to think, perhaps, it's all nonsense; perhaps Colonel George never--"  "Never meant to take possession of Castlewood; never gave himself airs, and patronised us there; never advised my mother to have me flogged; never intended to marry her; never insulted me, and was insulted before the King's officers; never wrote to his brother to say that we should be the better for his parental authority?
10111-8{_}1348{_}The paper is there," cried the young man, slapping his breast-pocket, "and if anything happens to me, Harry Warrington, you will find it on my corpse!"
10111-8{_}1349{_}"Write, yourself, Georgie, I _can't_ write," says Harry, digging his fists into his eyes, and smearing over the whole composition, bad spelling and all, with his elbows.
10111-8{_}1350{_}On this, George, taking another sheet of paper, sat down at his brother's place, and produced a composition in which he introduced the longest words, the grandest Latin quotations, and the most profound satire of which the youthful scribe was master.
10111-8{_}1351{_}He desired that his negro boy, Sady, should be set free; that his "Horace," a choice of his books, and, if possible, a suitable provision should be made for his affectionate tutor, Mr. Dempster; that his silver fruit-knife, his music-books, and harpischord should be given to little Fannie Mountain; and that his brother should take a lock of his hair, and wear it in memory of his ever fond and faithfully attached George.
10111-8{_}1352{_}And he sealed the document with the seal of arms that his grandfather had worn.
10111-8{_}1353{_}"The watch, of course, will be yours," said George, taking out his grandfather's gold watch and looking at it.
10111-8{_}1354{_}"Why, two hours and a half are gone!
10111-8{_}1355{_}'Tis time that Sady should be back with the pistols.
10111-8{_}1356{_}Take the watch, Harry, dear."
10111-8{_}1357{_}"It's no good!"
10111-8{_}1358{_}cried out Harry, flinging his arms round his brother.
10111-8{_}1359{_}"If he fights you, I'll fight him, too.
10111-8{_}1360{_}If he kills my Georgie, he shall have a shot at me!"
10111-8{_}1361{_}cried the poor lad.
10111-8{_}1362{_}Meanwhile, Mr. Washington had written five letters in his large resolute hand, and sealed them with his seal.
10111-8{_}1363{_}One was to his mother, at Mount Vernon; one to his brother; one was addressed M.C.
10111-8{_}1364{_}only; and one to his Excellency, Major-General Braddock.
10111-8{_}1365{_}"And one, young gentlemen, is for your mother, Madame Esmond," said the boys' informant.
10111-8{_}1366{_}It was the landlord of the tavern who communicated these facts to the young men.
10111-8{_}1367{_}The Captain had put on his old militia uniform to do honour to the occasion, and informed the boys that the "Colonel was walking up and down the garden a-waiting for 'em, and that the Reg'lars was a'most sober, too, by this time."
10111-8{_}1368{_}A plot of ground near the Captain's log house had been enclosed with shingles, and cleared for a kitchen-garden; there indeed paced Colonel Washington, his hands behind his back, his head bowed down, a grave sorrow on his handsome face.
10111-8{_}1369{_}The negro servants were crowded at the palings and looking over.
10111-8{_}1370{_}The officers under the porch had wakened up also, as their host remarked.
10111-8{_}1371{_}There, then, stalked the tall young Colonel, plunged in dismal meditation.
10111-8{_}1372{_}There was no way out of his scrape, but the usual cruel one, which the laws of honour and the practice of the country ordered.
10111-8{_}1373{_}Goaded into fury by the impertinence of a boy, he had used insulting words.
10111-8{_}1374{_}The young man had asked for reparation.
10111-8{_}1375{_}He was shocked to think that George Warrington's jealousy and revenge should have rankled in the young fellow so long; but the wrong had been the Colonel's, and he was bound to pay the forfeit.
10111-8{_}1376{_}A great hallooing and shouting, such as negroes use, who love noise at all times, was now heard at a distance, and all heads were turned in the direction of this outcry.
10111-8{_}1377{_}It came from the road over which our travellers had themselves passed three hours before, and presently the clattering of a horse's hoofs was heard, and now Mr. Sady made his appearance on his foaming horse.
10111-8{_}1378{_}Presently he was in the court-yard, and was dismounting.
10111-8{_}1379{_}"Sady, sir, come here!"
10111-8{_}1380{_}roars out Master Harry.
10111-8{_}1381{_}"Sady, come here, confound you!"
10111-8{_}1382{_}shouts Master George.
10111-8{_}1383{_}"Come directly, Mas'r," says Sady.
10111-8{_}1384{_}He grins.
10111-8{_}1385{_}He takes the pistols out of the holster.
10111-8{_}1386{_}He snaps the locks.
10111-8{_}1387{_}He points them at a grunter, which plunges through the farm-yard.
10111-8{_}1388{_}He points down the road, over which he has just galloped, and says again, "Comin', Mas'r.
10111-8{_}1389{_}Everybody a-comin'."
10111-8{_}1390{_}And now, the gallop of other horses is heard.
10111-8{_}1391{_}And who is yonder?
10111-8{_}1392{_}Little Mr. Dempster, spurring and digging into his pony; and that lady in a riding-habit on Madame Esmond's little horse--can it be Madame Esmond?
10111-8{_}1393{_}No.
10111-8{_}1394{_}It is too stout.
10111-8{_}1395{_}As I live it is Mrs. Mountain on Madame's grey!"
10111-8{_}1396{_}"O Lor'!
10111-8{_}1397{_}O Golly!
10111-8{_}1398{_}Hoop!
10111-8{_}1399{_}Here dey come!
10111-8{_}1400{_}Hurray!"
10111-8{_}1401{_}Dr. Dempster and Mrs. Mountain having clattered into the yard, jumped from their horses, and ran to the garden where George and Harry were walking, their tall enemy stalking opposite to them; and almost ere George Warrington had time sternly to say, "What do you here, Madame?"
10111-8{_}1402{_}Mrs. Mountain flung her arms round his neck and cried: "Oh, George, my darling!
10111-8{_}1403{_}It's a mistake!
10111-8{_}1404{_}It's a mistake, and is all my fault!"
10111-8{_}1405{_}"What's a mistake?"
10111-8{_}1406{_}asks George, majestically separating himself from the embrace.
10111-8{_}1407{_}"What is it, Mounty?"
10111-8{_}1408{_}cries Harry, all of a tremble.
10111-8{_}1409{_}"That paper I took out of his portfolio, that paper I picked up, children; where the Colonel says he is going to marry a widow with two children.
10111-8{_}1410{_}Well, it's--it's not your mother.
10111-8{_}1411{_}It's that little Widow Custis whom the Colonel is going to marry.
10111-8{_}1412{_}It's not Mrs. Rachel Warrington.
10111-8{_}1413{_}He told Madame so to-day, just before he was going away, and that the marriage was to come off after the campaign.
10111-8{_}1414{_}And--and your mother is furious, boys.
10111-8{_}1415{_}And when Sady came for the pistols, and told the whole house how you were going to fight, I told him to fire the pistols off; and I galloped after him, and I've nearly broken my poor old bones in coming to you."
10111-8{_}1416{_}"What will Mr. Washington and those gentlemen think of my servant telling my mother at home that I was going to fight a duel?"
10111-8{_}1417{_}growled Mr. George in wrath.
10111-8{_}1418{_}"You should have shown your proofs before, George," says Harry, respectfully.
10111-8{_}1419{_}"And, thank Heaven, you are not going to fight our old friend.
10111-8{_}1420{_}For it was a mistake; and there is no quarrel now, dear, is there?
10111-8{_}1421{_}You were unkind to him under a wrong impression."
10111-8{_}1422{_}"I certainly acted under a wrong impression," owns George, "but--"  "George!
10111-8{_}1423{_}George Washington!"
10111-8{_}1424{_}Harry here cries out, springing over the cabbage garden towards the bowling-green, where the Colonel was stalking, and though we cannot hear him, we see him, with both his hands out, and with the eagerness of youth, and with a hundred blunders, and with love and affection thrilling in his honest voice, we imagine the lad telling his tale to his friend.
10111-8{_}1425{_}There was a custom in those days which has disappeared from our manners now, but which then lingered.
10111-8{_}1426{_}When Harry had finished his artless story his friend the Colonel took him fairly to his arms, and held him to his heart; and his voice faltered as he said, "Thank God, thank God for this!"
10111-8{_}1427{_}"Oh, George," said Harry, who felt now he loved his friend with all his heart, "how I wish I was going with you on the campaign!"
10111-8{_}1428{_}The other pressed both the boy's hands in a grasp of friendship, which, each knew, never would slacken.
10111-8{_}1429{_}Then the Colonel advanced, gravely holding out his hand to Harry's elder brother.
10111-8{_}1430{_}But, though hands were joined, the salutation was only formal and stern on both sides.
10111-8{_}1431{_}"I find I have done you a wrong, Colonel Washington," George said, "and must apologise, not for the error, but for much of my late behaviour, which has resulted from it."
10111-8{_}1432{_}"The error was mine!
10111-8{_}1433{_}It was I who found that paper in your room and showed it to George, and was jealous of you, Colonel.
10111-8{_}1434{_}All women are jealous," cried Mrs. Mountain.
10111-8{_}1435{_}"'Tis a pity you could not have kept your eyes off my paper, Madame," said Mr. Washington.
10111-8{_}1436{_}"You will permit me to say so.
10111-8{_}1437{_}A great deal of mischief has come because I chose to keep a secret which concerned only myself and another person.
10111-8{_}1438{_}For a long time George Warrington's heart has been black with anger against me, and my feeling towards him has, I own, scarce been more friendly.
10111-8{_}1439{_}All this pain might have been spared to both of us had my private papers only been read by those for whom they were written.
10111-8{_}1440{_}I shall say no more now, lest my feelings again should betray me into hasty words.
10111-8{_}1441{_}Heaven bless thee, Harry!
10111-8{_}1442{_}Farewell, George!
10111-8{_}1443{_}And take a true friend's advice, and try to be less ready to think evil of your friends.
10111-8{_}1444{_}We shall meet again at the camp, and will keep our weapons for the enemy.
10111-8{_}1445{_}Gentlemen!
10111-8{_}1446{_}if you remember this scene tomorrow, you will know where to find me."
10111-8{_}1447{_}And with a very stately bow to the English officers, the Colonel left the abashed company, and speedily rode away.
10111-8{_}1448{_}We must fancy that the parting between the brothers is over, that George has taken his place in Mr. Braddock's family, and Harry has returned home to Castlewood and his duty.
10111-8{_}1449{_}His heart is with the army, and his pursuits at home offer the boy no pleasure.
10111-8{_}1450{_}He does not care to own how deep his disappointment is, at being obliged to stay under the homely, quiet roof, now more melancholy than ever since George is away.
10111-8{_}1451{_}Harry passes his brother's empty chamber with an averted face; takes George's place at the head of the table, and sighs as he drinks from his silver tankard.
10111-8{_}1452{_}Madame Warrington calls the toast of "The King" stoutly every day; and on Sundays when Harry reads the Service, and prays for all travellers by land and by water, she says, "We beseech Thee to hear us," with a peculiar solemnity.
10111-8{_}1453{_}Mrs. Mountain is constantly on the whimper when George's name is mentioned, and Harry's face frequently wears a look of the most ghastly alarm; but his mother's is invariably grave and sedate.
10111-8{_}1454{_}She makes more blunders at piquet and backgammon than you would expect from her; and the servants find her awake and dressed, however early they may rise.
10111-8{_}1455{_}She has prayed Mr. Dempster to come back into residence at Castlewood.
10111-8{_}1456{_}She is not severe or haughty, as her wont certainly was, with any of the party, but quiet in her talk with them, and gentle in assertion and reply.
10111-8{_}1457{_}She is forever talking of her father and his campaigns, who came out of them all with no very severe wounds to hurt him; and so she hopes and trusts will her eldest son.
10111-8{_}1458{_}George writes frequent letters home to his brother, and, now the army is on its march, compiles a rough journal, which he forwards as occasion serves.
10111-8{_}1459{_}This document is read with great eagerness by Harry, and more than once read out in family councils on the long summer nights as Madame Esmond sits upright at her tea-table; as little Fanny Mountain is busy with her sewing, as Mr. Dempster and Mrs. Mountain sit over their cards, as the hushed old servants of the house move about silently in the gloaming and listen to the words of the young master.
10111-8{_}1460{_}Hearken to Harry Warrington reading out his brother's letter!
10111-8{_}1461{_}"It must be owned that the provinces are acting scurvily by his Majesty King George, and his representative here is in a flame of fury.
10111-8{_}1462{_}Virginia is bad enough, and poor Maryland not much better, but Pennsylvania is worst of all.
10111-8{_}1463{_}We pray them to send us troops from home to fight the French; and we propose to maintain the troops when they come.
10111-8{_}1464{_}We not only don't keep our promise, and make scarce any provision for our defenders, but our people insist upon the most exorbitant prices for their cattle and stores, and actually cheat the soldiers who are come to fight their battles.
10111-8{_}1465{_}No wonder the General swears, and the troops are sulky.
10111-8{_}1466{_}The delays have been endless.
10111-8{_}1467{_}Owing to the failure of the several provinces to provide their promised stores and means of locomotion, weeks and months have elapsed, during which time no doubt the French have been strengthening themselves on our frontier and in the forts they have turned us out of.
10111-8{_}1468{_}Though there never will be any love lost between me and Colonel Washington, it must be owned that _your favourite_ (I am not jealous, Hal) is a brave man and a good officer.
10111-8{_}1469{_}The family respect him very much, and the General is always asking his opinion.
10111-8{_}1470{_}Indeed, he is almost the only man who has seen the Indians in their war-paint, and I own I think he was right in firing upon Mons.
10111-8{_}1471{_}Jumonville last year."
10111-8{_}1472{_}Harry resumes: "We keep the strictest order here in camp, and the orders against drunkenness and ill behaviour on the part of the men are very severe.
10111-8{_}1473{_}The roll of each company is called at morning, noon, and night, and a return of the absent and disorderly is given in by the officer to the commanding officer of the regiment, who has to see that they are properly punished.
10111-8{_}1474{_}Each regiment has Divine Service performed at the head of its colours every Sunday.
10111-8{_}1475{_}The General does everything in the power of mortal man to prevent plundering, and to encourage the people round about to bring in provisions.
10111-8{_}1476{_}He has declared soldiers shall be shot who dare to interrupt or molest the market people.
10111-8{_}1477{_}He has ordered the price of provisions to be raised a penny a pound, and has lent money out of his own pocket to provide the camp.
10111-8{_}1478{_}Altogether he is a strange compound, this General, and shows many strange inconsistencies in his conduct.
10111-8{_}1479{_}"Colonel Washington has had the fever very smartly, and has hardly been well enough to keep up with the march.
10111-8{_}1480{_}When either of us is ill, we are almost as good friends again as ever, and though I don't love him as you do, I know he is a good soldier, a good officer, and a brave, honest man; and, at any rate, shall love him none the worse for not wanting to be our step-father."
10111-8{_}1481{_}"'Tis a pretty sight," Harry continued, reading from his brother's journal, "to see a long line of red coats threading through the woods or taking their ground after the march.
10111-8{_}1482{_}The care against surprise is so great and constant that we defy prowling Indians to come unawares upon us, and our advanced sentries and savages have on the contrary fallen in with the enemy and taken a scalp or two from them.
10111-8{_}1483{_}They are such cruel villains, these French and their painted allies, that we do not think of showing them mercy.
10111-8{_}1484{_}Only think, we found but yesterday a little boy scalped but yet alive in a lone house, where his parents had been attacked and murdered by the savage enemy, of whom--so great is his indignation at their cruelty--our General has offered a reward of £5 for all the Indian scalps brought in.
10111-8{_}1485{_}"When our march is over, you should see our camp, and all the care bestowed on it.
10111-8{_}1486{_}Our baggage and our General's tents and guard are placed quite in the centre of the camp.
10111-8{_}1487{_}We have outlying sentries by twos, by threes, by tens, by whole companies.
10111-8{_}1488{_}At the least surprise, they are instructed to run in on the main body and rally round the tents and baggage, which are so arranged themselves as to be a strong fortification.
10111-8{_}1489{_}Sady and I, you must know, are marching on foot now, and my horses are carrying baggage.
10111-8{_}1490{_}The Pennsylvanians sent such rascally animals into camp that they speedily gave in.
10111-8{_}1491{_}What good horses were left 'twas our duty to give up; and Roxana has a couple of packs upon her back instead of her young master.
10111-8{_}1492{_}She knows me right well, and whinnies when she sees me, and I walk by her side, and we have many a talk together on the march.
10111-8{_}1493{_}"July 4.
10111-8{_}1494{_}To guard against surprises, we are all warned to pay especial attention to the beat of the drum; always halting when we hear the long roll beat, and marching at the beat of the long march.
10111-8{_}1495{_}We are more on the alert regarding the enemy now.
10111-8{_}1496{_}We have our advanced pickets doubled, and two sentries at every post.
10111-8{_}1497{_}The men on the advanced pickets are constantly under arms, with fixed bayonets, all through the night, and relieved every two hours.
10111-8{_}1498{_}The half that are relieved lie down by their arms, but are not suffered to leave their pickets.
10111-8{_}1499{_}'Tis evident that we are drawing near to the enemy now.
10111-8{_}1500{_}This packet goes out with the General's to Colonel Dunbar's camp, who is thirty miles behind us; and will be carried thence to Frederick, and thence to my honoured mother's house at Castlewood, to whom I send my duty, with kindest remembrances, as to all friends there, and how much love I need not say to my dearest brother from his affectionate George E.
10111-8{_}1501{_}Warrington."
10111-8{_}1502{_}The whole land was now lying parched and scorching in the July heat.
10111-8{_}1503{_}For ten days no news had come from the column advancing on the Ohio.
10111-8{_}1504{_}Their march, though it toiled but slowly through the painful forest, must bring ere long up with the enemy; the troops, led by consummate captains, were accustomed now to the wilderness, and not afraid of surprise.
10111-8{_}1505{_}Every precaution had been taken against ambush.
10111-8{_}1506{_}It was the outlying enemy who were discovered, pursued, destroyed, by the vigilant scouts and skirmishers of the British force.
10111-8{_}1507{_}The last news heard was that the army had advanced considerably beyond the ground of Mr. Washington's discomfiture in the previous year, and two days after must be within a day's march of the French fort.
10111-8{_}1508{_}About taking it no fears were entertained; the amount of the French reinforcements from Montreal was known.
10111-8{_}1509{_}Mr. Braddock, with his two veteran regiments from Britain, and their allies of Virginia and Pennsylvania, was more than a match for any troops that could be collected under the white flag.
10111-8{_}1510{_}Such continued to be the talk, in the sparse towns of our Virginian province, at the gentry's houses, and the rough road-side taverns, where people met and canvassed the war.
10111-8{_}1511{_}The few messengers sent back by the General reported well of the main force.
10111-8{_}1512{_}It was thought the enemy would not stand or defend himself at all.
10111-8{_}1513{_}Had he intended to attack, he might have seized a dozen occasions for assaulting our troops at passes through which they had been allowed to go entirely free.
10111-8{_}1514{_}So George had given up his favourite mare, like a hero as he was, and was marching a-foot with the line.
10111-8{_}1515{_}Madame Esmond vowed that he should have the best horse in Virginia or Carolina in place of Roxana.
10111-8{_}1516{_}There were horses enough to be had in the provinces, and for money.
10111-8{_}1517{_}It was only for the King's service that they were not forthcoming.
10111-8{_}1518{_}Although at their family meetings and repasts the inmates of Castlewood always talked cheerfully, never anticipating any but a triumphant issue to the campaign, or acknowledging any feeling of disquiet, yet it must be owned they were mighty uneasy when at home, quitting it ceaselessly, and forever on the trot from one neighbour's house to another in quest of news.
10111-8{_}1519{_}It was prodigious how quickly reports ran and spread.
10111-8{_}1520{_}For three weeks after the army's departure, the reports regarding it were cheerful; and when our Castlewood friends met at their supper their tone was confident and their news pleasant.
10111-8{_}1521{_}But on the 10th of July a vast and sudden gloom spread over the province.
10111-8{_}1522{_}A look of terror and doubt seemed to fall upon every face.
10111-8{_}1523{_}Affrighted negroes wistfully eyed their masters and retired, to hum and whisper with one another.
10111-8{_}1524{_}The fiddles ceased in the quarters; the song and laugh of those cheery black folk were hushed.
10111-8{_}1525{_}Right and left everybody's servants were on the gallop for news.
10111-8{_}1526{_}The country taverns were thronged with horsemen, who drank and cursed and brawled at the bars, each bringing his gloomy story.
10111-8{_}1527{_}The army had been surprised.
10111-8{_}1528{_}The troops had fallen into an ambuscade, and had been cut up almost to a man.
10111-8{_}1529{_}All the officers were taken down by the French marksmen and the savages.
10111-8{_}1530{_}The General had been wounded, and carried off the field in his sash.
10111-8{_}1531{_}Four days afterwards the report was that the General was dead, and scalped by a French Indian.
10111-8{_}1532{_}Ah, what a scream poor Mrs. Mountain gave when Gumbo brought this news from across the James River, and little Fanny sprang crying to her mother's arms!
10111-8{_}1533{_}"Lord God Almighty, watch over us, and defend my boy!"
10111-8{_}1534{_}said Mrs. Esmond, sinking down on her knees and lifting her rigid hands to heaven.
10111-8{_}1535{_}The gentlemen were not at home when the rumour arrived, but they came in an hour or two afterwards, each from his hunt for news.
10111-8{_}1536{_}The Scotch tutor did not dare to meet the widow's agonising looks.
10111-8{_}1537{_}Harry Warrington was as pale as his mother.
10111-8{_}1538{_}It might not be true about the manner of the General's death--but he was dead.
10111-8{_}1539{_}The army had been surprised by Indians, and had fled, and been killed without seeing the enemy.
10111-8{_}1540{_}An express had arrived from Dunbar's camp.
10111-8{_}1541{_}Fugitives were pouring in there.
10111-8{_}1542{_}Should he go and see?
10111-8{_}1543{_}He must go and see.
10111-8{_}1544{_}He and stout little Dempster armed themselves and mounted, taking a couple of mounted servants with them.
10111-8{_}1545{_}They followed the northward track which the expeditionary army had hewed out for itself, and at every step which brought them nearer to the scene of action, the disaster of the fearful day seemed to magnify.
10111-8{_}1546{_}The day after the defeat a number of the miserable fugitives from the fatal battle of the 9th of July had reached Dunbar's camp, fifty miles from the field.
10111-8{_}1547{_}Thither poor Harry and his companions rode, stopping stragglers, asking news, giving money, getting from one and all the same gloomy tale.
10111-8{_}1548{_}A thousand men were slain--two-thirds of the officers were down--all the General's aides-de-camp were hit.
10111-8{_}1549{_}Were hit--but were they killed?
10111-8{_}1550{_}Those who fell never rose again.
10111-8{_}1551{_}The tomahawk did its work upon them.
10111-8{_}1552{_}Oh, brother brother!
10111-8{_}1553{_}All the fond memories of their youth, all the dear remembrances of their childhood, the love and the laughter, the tender romantic vows which they had pledged to each other as lads, were recalled by Harry with pangs inexpressibly keen.
10111-8{_}1554{_}Wounded men looked up and were softened by his grief; rough men melted as they saw the woe written on the handsome young face; the hardy old tutor could scarcely look at him for tears, and grieved for him even more than for his dear pupil, who, he believed, lay dead under the savage Indian knife.
10111-8{_}1555{_}At every step which Harry Warrington took towards Pennsylvania the reports of the British disaster were magnified and confirmed.
10111-8{_}1556{_}Those two famous regiments which had fought in the Scottish and Continental wars had fled from an enemy almost unseen, and their boasted discipline and valour had not enabled them to face a band of savages and a few French infantry.
10111-8{_}1557{_}The unfortunate commander of the expedition had shown the utmost bravery and resolution.
10111-8{_}1558{_}Four times his horse had been shot under him.
10111-8{_}1559{_}Twice he had been wounded, and the last time of the mortal hurt which ended his life three days after the battle.
10111-8{_}1560{_}More than one of Harry's informants described the action to the poor lad,--the passage of the river, the long line of advance through the wilderness, the firing in front, the vain struggle of the men to advance, and the artillery to clear the way of the enemy; then the ambushed fire from behind every bush and tree, and the murderous fusillade, by which at least half of the expeditionary force had been shot down.
10111-8{_}1561{_}But not all the General's suite were killed, Harry heard.
10111-8{_}1562{_}One of his aides-de-camp, a Virginian gentleman, was ill of fever and exhaustion at Dunbar's camp.
10111-8{_}1563{_}One of them--but which?
10111-8{_}1564{_}To the camp Harry hurried, and reached it at length.
10111-8{_}1565{_}It was George Washington Harry found stretched in a tent there, and not his brother.
10111-8{_}1566{_}A sharper pain than that of the fever Mr. Washington declared he felt, when he saw Harry Warrington, and could give him no news of George.
10111-8{_}1567{_}Mr. Washington did not dare to tell Harry all.
10111-8{_}1568{_}For three days after the fight his duty had been to be near the General.
10111-8{_}1569{_}On the fatal 9th of July he had seen George go to the front with orders from the chief, to whose side he never returned.
10111-8{_}1570{_}After Braddock himself died, the aide-de-camp had found means to retrace his course to the field.
10111-8{_}1571{_}The corpses which remained there were stripped and horribly mutilated.
10111-8{_}1572{_}One body he buried which he thought to be George Warrington's.
10111-8{_}1573{_}His own illness was increased, perhaps occasioned, by the anguish which he underwent in his search for the unhappy volunteer.
10111-8{_}1574{_}"Ah, George!
10111-8{_}1575{_}If you had loved him you would have found him dead or alive," Harry cried out.
10111-8{_}1576{_}Nothing would satisfy him but that he, too, should go to the ground and examine it.
10111-8{_}1577{_}With money he procured a guide or two.
10111-8{_}1578{_}He forded the river at the place where the army had passed over; he went from one end to the other of the dreadful field.
10111-8{_}1579{_}The horrible spectacle of mutilation caused him to turn away with shudder and loathing.
10111-8{_}1580{_}What news could the vacant woods, or those festering corpses lying under the trees, give the lad of his lost brother?
10111-8{_}1581{_}He was for going, unarmed, with a white flag, to the French fort, whither, after their victory, the enemy had returned; but his guides refused to advance with him.
10111-8{_}1582{_}The French might possibly respect them, but the Indians would not.
10111-8{_}1583{_}"Keep your hair for your lady-mother, my young gentleman," said the guide.
10111-8{_}1584{_}"Tis enough that she loses one son in this campaign."
10111-8{_}1585{_}When Harry returned to the English encampment at Dunbar's it was his turn to be down with the fever.
10111-8{_}1586{_}Delirium set in upon him, and he lay some time in the tent and on the bed from which his friend had just risen convalescent.
10111-8{_}1587{_}For some days he did not know who watched him; and poor Dempster, who had tended him in more than one of these maladies, thought the widow must lose both her children; but the fever was so far subdued that the boy was enabled to rally somewhat, and get on horseback.
10111-8{_}1588{_}Mr. Washington and Dempster both escorted him home.
10111-8{_}1589{_}It was with a heavy heart, no doubt, that all three beheld once more the gates of Castlewood.
10111-8{_}1590{_}A servant in advance had been sent to announce their coming.
10111-8{_}1591{_}First came Mrs. Mountain and her little daughter, welcoming Harry with many tears and embraces; but she scarce gave a nod of recognition to Mr. Washington; and the little girl caused the young officer to start, and turn deadly pale, by coming up to him with her hands behind her, and asking, "Why have you not brought George back, too?"
10111-8{_}1592{_}Dempster was graciously received by the two ladies.
10111-8{_}1593{_}"Whatever could be done, we know _you_ would do, Mr. Dempster," says Mrs. Mountain, giving him her hand.
10111-8{_}1594{_}"Make a curtsey to Mr. Dempster, Fanny, and remember, child, to be grateful to all who have been friendly to our benefactors.
10111-8{_}1595{_}Will it please you to take any refreshment before you ride, Colonel Washington?"
10111-8{_}1596{_}Mr. Washington had had a sufficient ride already, and counted as certainly upon the hospitality of Castlewood as he would upon the shelter of his own house.
10111-8{_}1597{_}"The time to feed my horse, and a glass of water for myself, and I will trouble Castlewood hospitality no farther," Mr. Washington said.
10111-8{_}1598{_}"Sure, George, you have your room here, and my mother is above stairs getting it ready!"
10111-8{_}1599{_}cries Harry.
10111-8{_}1600{_}"That poor horse of yours stumbled with you, and can't go farther this evening."
10111-8{_}1601{_}"Hush!
10111-8{_}1602{_}Your mother won't see him, child," whispered Mrs. Mountain.
10111-8{_}1603{_}"Not see George?
10111-8{_}1604{_}Why, he is like a son of the house," cries Harry.
10111-8{_}1605{_}"She had best not see him.
10111-8{_}1606{_}I don't meddle any more in family matters, child; but when the Colonel's servant rode in, and said you were coming, Madame Esmond left this room and said she felt she could not see Mr. Washington.
10111-8{_}1607{_}Will you go to her?"
10111-8{_}1608{_}Harry took Mrs. Mountain's arm, and excusing himself to the Colonel, to whom he said he would return in a few minutes, he left the parlour in which they had assembled, and went to the upper rooms, where Madame Esmond was.
10111-8{_}1609{_}He was hastening across the corridor, and, with an averted head, passing by one especial door, which he did not like to look at, for it was that of his brother's room; and as he came to it, Madame Esmond issued from it, and folded him to her heart, and led him in.
10111-8{_}1610{_}A settee was by the bed, and a book of psalms lay on the coverlet.
10111-8{_}1611{_}All the rest of the room was exactly as George had left it.
10111-8{_}1612{_}"My poor child!
10111-8{_}1613{_}How thin thou art grown--how haggard you look!
10111-8{_}1614{_}Never mind.
10111-8{_}1615{_}A mother's care will make thee well again.
10111-8{_}1616{_}'Twas nobly done to go and brave sickness and danger in search of your brother.
10111-8{_}1617{_}Had others been as faithful, he might be here now.
10111-8{_}1618{_}Never mind, my Harry; our hero will come back to us.
10111-8{_}1619{_}I know he is not dead.
10111-8{_}1620{_}He will come back to us, I know he will come."
10111-8{_}1621{_}And when Harry pressed her to give a reason for her belief, she said she had seen her father two nights running in a dream, and he had told her that her boy was a prisoner among the Indians.
10111-8{_}1622{_}Madame Esmond's grief had not prostrated her as Harry's had when first it fell upon him; it had rather stirred and animated her; her eyes were eager, her countenance angry and revengeful.
10111-8{_}1623{_}The lad wondered almost at the condition in which he found his mother.
10111-8{_}1624{_}But when he besought her to go downstairs, and give her a hand of welcome to George Washington, who had accompanied him, the lady's excitement painfully increased.
10111-8{_}1625{_}She said she should shudder at touching his hand.
10111-8{_}1626{_}She declared Mr. Washington had taken her son from her; she could not sleep under the same roof with him.
10111-8{_}1627{_}"No gentleman," cried Harry, warmly, "was ever refused shelter under my grandfather's roof."
10111-8{_}1628{_}"Oh, no, gentlemen!"
10111-8{_}1629{_}exclaims the little widow; "well let us go down, if you like, son, and pay our respects to this one.
10111-8{_}1630{_}Will you please to give us your arm?"
10111-8{_}1631{_}and taking an arm which was very little able to give her support, she walked down the broad stairs and into the apartment where the Colonel sat.
10111-8{_}1632{_}She made him a ceremonious curtsey, and extended one of the little hands, which she allowed for a moment to rest in his.
10111-8{_}1633{_}"I wish that our meeting had been happier, Colonel Washington," she said.
10111-8{_}1634{_}"You do not grieve more than I do that it is otherwise, Madame," said the Colonel.
10111-8{_}1635{_}"I might have wished that the meeting had been spared, that I might not have kept you from friends whom you are naturally anxious to see, that my boy's indisposition had not detained you.
10111-8{_}1636{_}Home and his good nurse Mountain, and his mother and our good Dr. Dempster will soon restore him.
10111-8{_}1637{_}'Twas scarce necessary, Colonel, that you who have so many affairs on your hands, military and domestic, should turn doctor too."
10111-8{_}1638{_}"Harry was ill and weak, and I thought it was my duty to ride by him," faltered the Colonel.
10111-8{_}1639{_}"You yourself, sir, have gone through the _fatigues_ and _dangers_ of the campaign in the most wonderful manner," said the widow, curtseying again, and looking at him with her impenetrable black eyes.
10111-8{_}1640{_}"I wish to Heaven, Madame, someone else had come back in my place!"
10111-8{_}1641{_}"Nay, sir, you have ties which must render your life more than ever valuable and dear to you, and duties to which, I know, you must be anxious to betake yourself.
10111-8{_}1642{_}In our present deplorable state of doubt and distress Castlewood can be a welcome place to no stranger, much less to you, and so I know, sir, you will be for leaving us ere long.
10111-8{_}1643{_}And you will pardon me if the state of my own spirits obliges me for the most part to keep my chamber.
10111-8{_}1644{_}But my friends here will bear you company as long as you favour us, whilst I nurse my poor Harry upstairs.
10111-8{_}1645{_}Mountain!
10111-8{_}1646{_}you will have the cedar room on the ground floor ready for Mr. Washington and anything in the house is at his command.
10111-8{_}1647{_}Farewell, sir.
10111-8{_}1648{_}Will you be pleased to present my compliments to your mother, who will be thankful to have her son safe and sound out of the war?--as also to my young friend, Martha Custis, to whom and to whose children I wish every happiness.
10111-8{_}1649{_}Come, my son!"
10111-8{_}1650{_}and with these words, and another freezing curtsey, the pale little woman retreated, looking steadily at the Colonel, who stood dumb on the floor.
10111-8{_}1651{_}Strong as Madame Esmond's belief appeared to be respecting her son's safety, the house of Castlewood naturally remained sad and gloomy.
10111-8{_}1652{_}To look for George was hoping against hope.
10111-8{_}1653{_}No authentic account of his death had indeed arrived, and no one appeared who had seen him fall, but hundreds more had been so stricken on that fatal day, with no eyes to behold their last pangs, save those of the lurking enemy and the comrades dying by their side.
10111-8{_}1654{_}A fortnight after the defeat, when Harry was absent on his quest, George's servant, Sady, reappeared, wounded and maimed, at Castlewood.
10111-8{_}1655{_}But he could give no coherent account of the battle, only of his flight from the centre, where he was with the baggage.
10111-8{_}1656{_}He had no news of his master since the morning of the action.
10111-8{_}1657{_}For many days Sady lurked in the negro quarters away from the sight of Madame Esmond, whose anger he did not dare to face.
10111-8{_}1658{_}That lady's few neighbours spoke of her as labouring under a delusion.
10111-8{_}1659{_}So strong was it that there were times when Harry and the other members of the little Castlewood family were almost brought to share in it.
10111-8{_}1660{_}No.
10111-8{_}1661{_}George was not dead; George was a prisoner among the Indians; George would come back and rule over Castlewood; as sure, as sure as his Majesty would send a great force from home to recover the tarnished glory of the British arms, and to drive the French out of the Americas.
10111-8{_}1662{_}As for Mr. Washington, she would never, with her own good will, behold him again.
10111-8{_}1663{_}He had promised to guard George's life with his own, and where was her boy.
10111-8{_}1664{_}So, if Harry wanted to meet his friend, he had to do so in secret.
10111-8{_}1665{_}Madame Esmond was exceedingly excited when she heard that the Colonel and her son absolutely had met, and said to Harry, "How you can talk, sir, of loving George, and then go and meet your Mr. Washington, I can't understand."
10111-8{_}1666{_}So there was not only grief in the Castlewood House, but there was disunion.
10111-8{_}1667{_}As a result of the gloom, and of his grief for the loss of his brother, Harry was again and again struck down by the fever, and all the Jesuits' bark in America could not cure him.
10111-8{_}1668{_}They had a tobacco-house and some land about the new town of Richmond, and he went thither and there mended a little, but still did not get quite well, and the physicians strongly counselled a sea-voyage.
10111-8{_}1669{_}Madame Esmond at one time had thoughts of going with him, but, as she and Harry did not agree very well, though they loved each other very heartily, 'twas determined that Harry should see the world for himself.
10111-8{_}1670{_}Accordingly he took passage on the "Young Rachel," Virginian ship, Edward Franks master.
10111-8{_}1671{_}She proceeded to Bristol and moored as near as possible to Trail's wharf, to which she was consigned.
10111-8{_}1672{_}Mr.
10111-8{_}1673{_}Trail, who could survey his ship from his counting-house windows, straightway took boat and came up her side, and gave the hand of welcome to Captain Franks, congratulating the Captain upon the speedy and fortunate voyage which he had made.
10111-8{_}1674{_}Franks was a pleasant man, who loved a joke.
10111-8{_}1675{_}"We have," says he, "but yonder ugly negro boy, who is fetching the trunks, and a passenger who has the state cabin to himself."
10111-8{_}1676{_}Mr.
10111-8{_}1677{_}Trail looked as if he would have preferred more mercies from Heaven.
10111-8{_}1678{_}"Confound you, Franks, and your luck!
10111-8{_}1679{_}The 'Duke William,' which came in last week, brought fourteen, and she is not half of our tonnage."
10111-8{_}1680{_}"And this passenger, who has the whole cabin, don't pay nothin'," continued the Captain.
10111-8{_}1681{_}"Swear now, it will do you good, Mr.
10111-8{_}1682{_}Trail, indeed it will.
10111-8{_}1683{_}I have tried the medicine."
10111-8{_}1684{_}"A passenger take the whole cabin and not pay?
10111-8{_}1685{_}Gracious mercy, are you a fool, Captain Franks?"
10111-8{_}1686{_}"Ask the passenger himself, for here he comes."
10111-8{_}1687{_}And as the master spoke, a young man of some nineteen years of age came up the hatchway.
10111-8{_}1688{_}He had a cloak and a sword under his arm, and was dressed in deep mourning, and called out, "Gumbo, you idiot, why don't you fetch the baggage out of the cabin?
10111-8{_}1689{_}Well, shipmate, our journey is ended.
10111-8{_}1690{_}You will see all the little folks to-night whom you have been talking about.
10111-8{_}1691{_}Give my love to Polly, and Betty, and little Tommy; not forgetting my duty to Mrs. Franks.
10111-8{_}1692{_}I thought, yesterday, the voyage would never be done, and now I am almost sorry it is over.
10111-8{_}1693{_}That little berth in my cabin looks very comfortable now I am going to leave it."
10111-8{_}1694{_}Mr.
10111-8{_}1695{_}Trail scowled at the young passenger who had paid no money for his passage.
10111-8{_}1696{_}He scarcely nodded his head to the stranger, when Captain Franks said: "This here gentleman is Mr.
10111-8{_}1697{_}Trail, sir, whose name you have a-heerd of."
10111-8{_}1698{_}"It's pretty well known in Bristol, sir," says Mr.
10111-8{_}1699{_}Trail, majestically.
10111-8{_}1700{_}"And this is Mr. Warrington, Madame Esmond Warrington's son, of Castlewood," continued the Captain.
10111-8{_}1701{_}The British merchant's hat was instantly off his head, and the owner of the beaver was making a prodigious number of bows, as if a crown-prince were before him.
10111-8{_}1702{_}"Gracious powers, Mr. Warrington!
10111-8{_}1703{_}This is a delight, indeed!
10111-8{_}1704{_}What a crowning mercy that your voyage should have been so prosperous!
10111-8{_}1705{_}You have my boat to go on shore.
10111-8{_}1706{_}Let me cordially and respectfully welcome you to England!
10111-8{_}1707{_}Let me shake your hand as the son of my benefactress and patroness, Mrs. Esmond Warrington, whose name is known and honoured on Bristol 'Change, I warrant you.
10111-8{_}1708{_}Isn't it, Franks?"
10111-8{_}1709{_}"There's no sweeter tobacco comes from Virginia," says Mr. Franks, drawing a great brass tobacco-box from his pocket, and thrusting a quid into his jolly mouth.
10111-8{_}1710{_}"You don't know what a comfort it is, sir; you'll take to it, bless you, as you grow older.
10111-8{_}1711{_}Won't he, Mr.
10111-8{_}1712{_}Trail?
10111-8{_}1713{_}I wish you had ten shiploads of it instead of one.
10111-8{_}1714{_}You might have ten shiploads; I've told Madame Esmond so; I've rode over her plantation; she treats me like a lord when I go to the house.
10111-8{_}1715{_}She is a real-born lady, she is; and might have a thousand hogsheads as easy as her hundreds, if there were but hands enough."
10111-8{_}1716{_}"I have lately engaged in the Guinea trade, and could supply her ladyship with any number of healthy young negroes before next fall," said Mr.
10111-8{_}1717{_}Trail, obsequiously.
10111-8{_}1718{_}"We are averse to the purchase of negroes from Africa," said the young gentleman, coldly.
10111-8{_}1719{_}"My grandfather and my mother have always objected to it, and I do not like to think of selling or buying the poor wretches."
10111-8{_}1720{_}"It is for their good, my dear young sir!

10111-8{_}1722{_}I can introduce you to a happy home, a Christian family, and a British merchant's honest fare.
