10111-8{_}1730{_}The young gentleman made a bow.
10111-8{_}1731{_}"I would urge upon you, at the very earliest moment, the duty of increasing the ample means with which Heaven has blessed you.
10111-8{_}1732{_}As an honest factor, I could not do otherwise: as a prudent man, should I scruple to speak of what will tend to your profit and mine?
10111-8{_}1733{_}No, my dear Mr.
10111-8{_}1734{_}George."

10111-8{_}1736{_}"Gracious powers!

10111-8{_}1739{_}"Hold your tongue, you fool!"
10111-8{_}1740{_}cried Mr. Franks, striking the merchant a tough blow on his sleek sides, as the young lad turned away.
10111-8{_}1741{_}"Don't you see the young gentleman a-swabbing his eyes, and note his black clothes?"
10111-8{_}1742{_}"What do you mean, Captain Franks, by laying your hand on your owners?
10111-8{_}1743{_}Mr. George is the heir; I know the Colonel's will well enough."
10111-8{_}1744{_}"Mr. George is there," said the Captain, pointing with his thumb to the deck.
10111-8{_}1745{_}"Where?"
10111-8{_}1746{_}cries the factor.
10111-8{_}1747{_}"Mr. George is there!"
10111-8{_}1748{_}reiterated the Captain, again lifting up his finger towards the topmast, or the sky beyond.
10111-8{_}1749{_}"He is dead a year, sir, come next 9th of July.
10111-8{_}1750{_}He would go out with General Braddock on that dreadful business to the Belle Riviere.
10111-8{_}1751{_}He and a thousand more never came back again.
10111-8{_}1752{_}Every man of them was murdered as he fell.
10111-8{_}1753{_}You know the Indian way, Mr.
10111-8{_}1754{_}Trail?"
10111-8{_}1755{_}And here the Captain passed his hand rapidly round his head.
10111-8{_}1756{_}"Horrible!
10111-8{_}1757{_}ain't it, sir?
10111-8{_}1758{_}Horrible!
10111-8{_}1759{_}He was a fine young man, the very picture of this one; only his hair was black, which is now hanging in a bloody Indian wigwam.
10111-8{_}1760{_}He was often and often on board of the 'Young Rachel,' and would have his chests of books broke open on deck before they landed.
10111-8{_}1761{_}He was a shy and silent young gent, not like this one, which was the merriest, wildest young fellow, full of his songs and fun.
10111-8{_}1762{_}He took on dreadful at the news; went to his bed, had that fever which lays so many of 'em by the heels along that swampy Potomac, but he's got better on the voyage: the voyage makes everyone better; and, in course, the young gentleman can't be forever a-crying after a brother who dies and leaves him a great fortune.
10111-8{_}1763{_}Ever since we sighted Ireland he has been quite gay and happy, only he would go off at times when he was most merry, saying, 'I wish my dearest Georgie could enjoy this here sight along with me,' and when you mentioned t'other's name, you see, he couldn't stand it."
10111-8{_}1764{_}And the honest Captain's own eyes filled with tears, as he turned and looked towards the object of his compassion.
10111-8{_}1765{_}Mr.
10111-8{_}1766{_}Trail assumed a sad expression befitting the tragic compliment with which he prepared to greet the young Virginian; but the latter answered him very curtly, declining his offers of hospitality, and only stayed in Mr.
10111-8{_}1767{_}Trail's house long enough to drink a glass of wine and to take up a sum of money of which he stood in need.
10111-8{_}1768{_}But he and Captain Franks parted on the very warmest terms, and all the little crew of the "Young Rachel" cheered from the ship's side as their passenger left it.
10111-8{_}1769{_}Again and again Harry Warrington and his brother had pored over the English map, and determined upon the course which they should take upon arriving at Home.
10111-8{_}1770{_}All Americans of English ancestry who love their mother country have rehearsed their English travels, and visited in fancy the spots with which their hopes, their parents' fond stories, their friends' descriptions, have rendered them familiar.
10111-8{_}1771{_}There are few things to me more affecting in the history of the quarrel which divided the two great nations than the recurrence of that word Home, as used by the younger towards the elder country.
10111-8{_}1772{_}Harry Warrington had his chart laid out.
10111-8{_}1773{_}Before London, and its glorious temples of St. Paul's and St. Peter's; its grim Tower, where the brave and loyal had shed their blood, from Wallace down to Balmerino and Kilmarnock, pitied by gentle hearts; before the awful window at Whitehall, whence the martyr Charles had issued, to kneel once more, and then ascended to Heaven; before playhouses, parks, and palaces, wondrous resorts of wit, pleasure and splendour; before Shakespeare's resting-place under the tall spire which rises by Avon, amidst the sweet Warwickshire pastures; before Derby, and Falkirk, and Culloden, where the cause of honour and loyalty had fallen, it might be to rise no more: before all these points in their pilgrimage there was one which the young Virginian brothers held even more sacred, and that was the home of their family, that old Castlewood in Hampshire, about which their parents had talked so fondly.
10111-8{_}1774{_}From Bristol to Bath, from Bath to Salisbury, to Winchester, to Hexton, to Home; they knew the way, and had mapped the journey many and many a time.
10111-8{_}1775{_}We must fancy our American traveller to be a handsome young fellow, whose suit of sables only makes him look the more interesting.
10111-8{_}1776{_}The plump landlady looked kindly after the young gentleman as he passed through the inn-hall from his post-chaise, and the obsequious chamberlain bowed him upstairs to the "Rose" or the "Dolphin."
10111-8{_}1777{_}The trim chambermaid dropped her best curtsey for his fee, and Gumbo, in the inn-kitchen, where the townsfolk drank their mug of ale by the great fire, bragged of his young master's splendid house in Virginia, and of the immense wealth to which he was heir.
10111-8{_}1778{_}The post-chaise whirled the traveller through the most delightful home scenery his eyes had ever lighted on.
10111-8{_}1779{_}If English landscape is pleasant to the American of the present day, who must needs contrast the rich woods and growing pastures and picturesque ancient villages of the old country with the rough aspect of his own, how much pleasanter must Harry Warrington's course have been, whose journeys had lain through swamps and forest solitudes from one Virginian ordinary to another log-house at the end of the day's route, and who now lighted suddenly upon the busy, happy, splendid scene of English summer?
10111-8{_}1780{_}And the high-road, a hundred years ago, was not that grass-grown desert of the present time.
10111-8{_}1781{_}It was alive with constant travel and traffic: the country towns and inns swarmed with life and gaiety.
10111-8{_}1782{_}The ponderous waggon, with its bells and plodding team; the light post-coach that achieved the journey from the "White Hart," Salisbury, to the "Swan with Two Necks," London, in two days; the strings of pack-horses that had not yet left the road; my lord's gilt post-chaise and six, with the outriders galloping on ahead; the country squire's great coach and heavy Flanders mares; the farmers trotting to market, or the parson jolting to the cathedral town on Dumpling, his wife behind on the pillion--all these crowding sights and brisk people greeted the young traveller on his summer journey.
10111-8{_}1783{_}Hodge, the farmer's boy, took off his hat, and Polly, the milk-maid, bobbed a curtsey, as the chaise whirled over the pleasant village-green, and the white-headed children lifted their chubby faces and cheered.
10111-8{_}1784{_}The church-spires glistened with gold, the cottage-gables glared in sunshine, the great elms murmured in summer, or cast purple shadows over the grass.
10111-8{_}1785{_}Young Warrington never had had such a glorious day, or witnessed a scene so delightful.
10111-8{_}1786{_}To be nineteen years of age, with high health, high spirits, and a full purse, to be making your first journey, and rolling through the country in a post-chaise at nine miles an hour--Oh, happy youth!
10111-8{_}1787{_}almost it makes one young to think of him!
10111-8{_}1788{_}And there let us leave him at Castlewood Inn, on ground hallowed by the footsteps of his ancestors.
10111-8{_}1789{_}There he stands, with new scenes, new friends, new experiences ahead, rich in hope, in expectation, and in the enthusiasm of youth--youth that comes but once, and is so fleet of foot!
10111-8{_}1790{_}And still more glad would he have been had he known that the near future was to verify his mother's belief; to restore to him the twin-brother now mourned as dead.
10111-8{_}1791{_}And glad are we, in looking beyond this story of boyhood days, to find that though in the Revolutionary War the subjects of this sketch fought on different sides in the quarrel, they came out peacefully at its conclusion, as brothers should, their love never having materially diminished, however angrily the contest divided them.
10111-8{_}1792{_}The colonel in scarlet and the general in blue and buff hang side by side in the wainscoted parlour of the Warringtons in England, and the portraits are known by the name of "The Virginians."
10111-8{_}1793{_}BECKY SHARP AT SCHOOL   [Illustration: BECKY SHARP LEAVING CHISWICK.]
10111-8{_}1794{_}While the last century was in its teens, and on one sunshiny morning in June, there drove up to the great iron gate of Miss Pinkerton's Academy for young ladies, on Chiswick Mall, a large family coach, with two fat horses in blazing harness, driven by a fat coachman in a three-cornered hat and wig, at the rate of four miles an hour.
10111-8{_}1795{_}A black servant, who reposed on the box beside the fat coachman, uncurled his bandy legs as soon as the equipage drew up opposite Miss Pinkerton's shining brass plate; and as he pulled the bell at least a score of young heads were seen peering out of the narrow windows of the stately old brick house.
10111-8{_}1796{_}Nay, the acute observer might have recognised the little red nose of good-natured Miss Jemima Pinkerton herself, rising over some geranium-pots in the window of that lady's own drawing-room.
10111-8{_}1797{_}"It is Mrs. Sedley's coach, sister," said Miss Jemima.
10111-8{_}1798{_}"Sambo, the black servant, has just rung the bell; and the coachman has a new red waistcoat."
10111-8{_}1799{_}"Have you completed all the necessary preparations incident to Miss Sedley's departure, Miss Jemima?"
10111-8{_}1800{_}asked Miss Pinkerton, that majestic lady, the friend of the famous literary man, Dr. Johnson, the author of the great Dixonary of the English language, called commonly the great Lexicographer.
10111-8{_}1801{_}"The girls were up at four this morning, packing her trunks, sister," replied Miss Jemima; "we have made her a bow-pot."
10111-8{_}1802{_}"Say a bouquet, sister Jemima, 'tis more genteel."
10111-8{_}1803{_}"Well, a booky as big almost as a hay-stack; I have put up two bottles of the gillyflower-water for Mrs. Sedley, and the receipt for making it, in Amelia's box."
10111-8{_}1804{_}"And I trust, Miss Jemima, you have made a copy of Miss Sedley's account.
10111-8{_}1805{_}This is it, is it?
10111-8{_}1806{_}Very good--ninety-three pounds, four shillings.
10111-8{_}1807{_}Be kind enough to address it to John Sedley, Esquire, and to seal this billet which I have written to his lady."
10111-8{_}1808{_}In Miss Jemima's eyes an autograph letter of her sister, Miss Pinkerton, was an object of as deep veneration as would have been a letter from a sovereign.
10111-8{_}1809{_}Only when her pupils quitted the establishment, or when they were about to be married, and once, when poor Miss Birch died of the scarlet fever, was Miss Pinkerton known to write personally to the parents of her pupils; and it was Jemima's opinion that if anything could have consoled Mrs. Birch for her daughter's loss, it would have been that pious and eloquent composition in which Miss Pinkerton announced the event.
10111-8{_}1810{_}In the present instance Miss Pinkerton's "billet" was to the following effect:         *       *       *       *       *  THE MALL, CHISWICK, June 15, 18--.
10111-8{_}1811{_}_Madam_: After her six years' residence at the Mall, I have the honour and happiness of presenting Miss Amelia Sedley to her parents, as a young lady not unworthy to occupy a fitting position in their polished and refined circle.
10111-8{_}1812{_}Those virtues which characterise the young English gentlewoman; those accomplishments which become her birth and station, will not be found wanting in the amiable Miss Sedley, whose industry and obedience have endeared her to her instructors, and whose delightful sweetness of temper has charmed her aged and her youthful companions.
10111-8{_}1813{_}In music, dancing, in orthography, in every variety of embroidery and needle-work, she will be found to have realised her friends' fondest wishes.
10111-8{_}1814{_}In geography there is still much to be desired; and a careful and undeviating use of the back-board, for four hours daily during the next three years is recommended as necessary to the acquirement of that dignified deportment and carriage so requisite for every young lady of fashion.
10111-8{_}1815{_}In the principles of religion and morality, Miss Sedley will be found worthy of an establishment which has been honoured by the presence of The Great Lexicographer, and the patronage of the admirable Mrs. Chapone.
10111-8{_}1816{_}In leaving them all, Miss Amelia carries with her the hearts of her companions, and the affectionate regards of her mistress, who has the honour to subscribe herself, Madam, your most obliged humble servant,  BARBARA PINKERTON.
10111-8{_}1817{_}P.S.--Miss Sharp accompanies Miss Sedley.
10111-8{_}1818{_}It is particularly requested that Miss Sharp's stay in Russell Square may not exceed ten days.
10111-8{_}1819{_}The family of distinction with whom she is engaged as governess desire to avail themselves of her services as soon as possible.
10111-8{_}1820{_}*       *       *       *       *  This letter completed, Miss Pinkerton proceeded to write her own name and Miss Sedley's in the fly-leaf of a Johnson's Dictionary, the interesting work which she invariably presented to her scholars on their departure from the Mall.
10111-8{_}1821{_}On the cover was inserted a copy of "Lines addressed to a young lady on quitting Miss Pinkerton's school, at the Mall; by the late revered Dr. Samuel Johnson."
10111-8{_}1822{_}In fact, the Lexicographer's name was always on the lips of this majestic woman, and a visit he had paid to her was the cause of her reputation and her fortune.
10111-8{_}1823{_}Being commanded by her elder sister to get The Dixonary from the cupboard, Miss Jemima had extracted two copies of the book from the receptacle in question.
10111-8{_}1824{_}When Miss Pinkerton had finished the inscription in the first, Jemima, with rather a dubious and timid air handed her the second.
10111-8{_}1825{_}"For whom is this, Miss Jemima?"
10111-8{_}1826{_}said Miss Pinkerton, with awful coldness.
10111-8{_}1827{_}"For Becky Sharp," answered Jemima, trembling very much, and blushing over her withered face and neck, as she turned her back on her sister.
10111-8{_}1828{_}"For Becky Sharp.
10111-8{_}1829{_}She's going, too."
10111-8{_}1830{_}"MISS JEMIMA!"
10111-8{_}1831{_}exclaimed Miss Pinkerton, in the largest capitals.
10111-8{_}1832{_}"Are you in your senses?
10111-8{_}1833{_}Replace the Dixonary in the closet, and never venture to take such a liberty in future."
10111-8{_}1834{_}"Well, sister, it's only two and nine-pence, and poor Becky will be miserable if she don't get one."
10111-8{_}1835{_}"Send Miss Sedley instantly to me," was Miss Pinkerton's only answer.
10111-8{_}1836{_}And, venturing not to say another word, poor Jemima trotted off, exceedingly flurried and nervous, while the two pupils, Miss Sedley and Miss Sharp, were making final preparation for their departure for Miss Sedley's home.
10111-8{_}1837{_}Now, Miss Sedley's papa was a merchant in London, and a man of some wealth, whereas Miss Sharp was only an articled pupil, for whom Miss Pinkerton had done, as she thought, quite enough, without conferring upon her at parting the high honour of the dixonary.
10111-8{_}1838{_}Miss Sharp's father had been an artist, and in former years had given lessons in drawing at Miss Pinkerton's school.
10111-8{_}1839{_}He was a clever man, a pleasant companion, a careless student, with a great propensity for running into debt, and a partiality for the tavern.
10111-8{_}1840{_}As it was with the utmost difficulty that he could keep himself, and as he owed money for a mile round Soho, where he lived, he thought to better his circumstances by marrying a young woman of the French nation, who was by profession an opera-girl, who had had some education somewhere, and her daughter Rebecca spoke French with purity and a Parisian accent.
10111-8{_}1841{_}It was in those days rather a rare accomplishment, and led to her engagement with the orthodox Miss Pinkerton.
10111-8{_}1842{_}For, her mother being dead, her father, finding himself fatally ill, as a consequence of his bad habits, wrote a manly and pathetic letter to Miss Pinkerton, recommending the orphan child to her protection, and so descended to the grave, after two bailiffs had quarrelled over his corpse.
10111-8{_}1843{_}Rebecca was seventeen when she came to Chiswick, and was bound over as an articled pupil; her duties being to talk French, as we have seen; and her privileges to live cost free, and with a few guineas a year, to gather scraps of knowledge from the professors who attended the school.
10111-8{_}1844{_}She was small, and slight in person; pale, sandy-haired, and with eyes almost habitually cast down.
10111-8{_}1845{_}When they looked up, they were very large, odd, and attractive.
10111-8{_}1846{_}By the side of many tall and bouncing young ladies in the establishment Rebecca Sharp looked like a child.
10111-8{_}1847{_}But she had the dismal precocity of poverty.
10111-8{_}1848{_}Many a dun had she talked to, and turned away from her father's door; many a tradesman had she coaxed and wheedled into good-humour, and into the granting of one meal more.
10111-8{_}1849{_}She had sat commonly with her father, who was very proud of her wit, and heard the talk of many of his wild companions, often but ill-suited for a girl to hear; but she had never been a girl, she said; she had been a woman since she was eight years old.
10111-8{_}1850{_}Miss Jemima, however, believed her to be the most innocent creature in the world, so admirably did Rebecca play the part of a child on the occasions when her father brought her to Chiswick as a young girl, and only a year before her father's death, and when she was sixteen years old, Miss Pinkerton majestically and with a little speech made her a present of a doll, which was, by the way, the confiscated property of Miss Swindle, discovered surreptitiously nursing it in school-hours.
10111-8{_}1851{_}How the father and daughter laughed as they trudged home together after the evening party, and how Miss Pinkerton would have raged had she seen the caricature of herself which the little mimic, Rebecca, managed to make out of the doll.
10111-8{_}1852{_}Becky used to go through dialogues with it; it formed the delight of the circle of young painters who frequented the studio, who used regularly to ask Rebecca if Miss Pinkerton was at home.
10111-8{_}1853{_}Once Rebecca had the honour to pass a few days at Chiswick, after which she brought back another doll which she called Miss Jemmy; for, though that honest creature had made and given her jelly and cake enough for three children, and a seven-shillings piece at parting, the girl's sense of ridicule was far stronger than her gratitude; and she sacrificed Miss Jemmy as pitilessly as her sister.
10111-8{_}1854{_}Then came the ending of Becky's studio days, and, an orphan, she was transplanted to the Mall as her home.
10111-8{_}1855{_}The rigid formality of the place suffocated her; the prayers and meals, the lessons and the walks, which were arranged with the regularity of a convent, oppressed her almost beyond endurance; and she looked back to the freedom and the beggary of her father's old studio with bitter regret.
10111-8{_}1856{_}She had never mingled in the society of women: her father, reprobate as he was, was a man of talent; his conversation was a thousand times more agreeable to her than the silly chat and scandal of the schoolgirls, and the frigid correctness of the governesses equally annoyed her.
10111-8{_}1857{_}She had no soft maternal heart, this unlucky girl.
10111-8{_}1858{_}The prattle of the younger children, with whose care she was chiefly entrusted, might have soothed and interested her; but she lived among them two years, and not one was sorry that she went away.
10111-8{_}1859{_}The gentle, tender-hearted Amelia Sedley was the only person to whom she could attach herself in the least; and who could help attaching herself to Amelia?
10111-8{_}1860{_}The happiness, the superior advantages of the young women round about her, gave Rebecca inexpressible pangs of envy.
10111-8{_}1861{_}"What airs that girl gives herself, because she is an Earl's granddaughter," she said of one.
10111-8{_}1862{_}"How they cringe and bow to the Creole, because of her hundred thousand pounds.
10111-8{_}1863{_}I am a thousand times cleverer and more charming than that creature, for all her wealth.
10111-8{_}1864{_}I am as well bred as the Earl's granddaughter, for all her fine pedigree; and yet everyone passes me by here."
10111-8{_}1865{_}She determined to get free from the prison in which she found herself, and now began to act for herself, and for the first time to make connected plans for the future.
10111-8{_}1866{_}She took advantage, therefore, of the means of study the place offered her; and as she was already a musician and a good linguist, she speedily went through the little course of study considered necessary for ladies in those days.
10111-8{_}1867{_}Her music she practised incessantly; and one day, when the girls were out, and she remained at home, she was overheard to play a piece so well that Miss Minerva thought, wisely, she could spare herself the expense of a master for the juniors, and intimated to Miss Sharp that she was to instruct them in music for the future.
10111-8{_}1868{_}The girl refused; and for the first time, and to the astonishment of the majestic mistress of the school.
10111-8{_}1869{_}"I am here to speak French with the children," Rebecca said abruptly, "not to teach them music, and save money for you.
10111-8{_}1870{_}Give me money, and I will teach them."
10111-8{_}1871{_}Miss Minerva was obliged to yield, and of course disliked her from that day.
10111-8{_}1872{_}"For five-and-thirty years," she said, and with great justice, "I never have seen the individual who has dared in my own house to question my authority.
10111-8{_}1873{_}I have nourished a viper in my bosom."
10111-8{_}1874{_}"A viper--a fiddlestick!"
10111-8{_}1875{_}said Miss Sharp to the old lady, who was almost fainting with astonishment.
10111-8{_}1876{_}"You took me because I was useful.
10111-8{_}1877{_}There is no question of gratitude between us.
10111-8{_}1878{_}I hate this place, and want to leave it.
10111-8{_}1879{_}I will do nothing here but what I am obliged to do."
10111-8{_}1880{_}It was in vain that the old lady asked her if she was aware she was speaking to Miss Pinkerton?
10111-8{_}1881{_}Rebecca laughed in her face.
10111-8{_}1882{_}"Give me a sum of money," said the girl, "and get rid of me.
10111-8{_}1883{_}Or, if you like better, get me a good place as governess in a nobleman's family.
10111-8{_}1884{_}You can do so if you please."
10111-8{_}1885{_}And in their further disputes she always returned to this point: "Get me a situation--I am ready to go."
10111-8{_}1886{_}Worthy Miss Pinkerton, although she had a Roman nose and a turban, and was as tall as a grenadier, and had been up to this time an irresistible princess, had no will or strength like that of her little apprentice, and in vain did battle against her, and tried to overawe her.
10111-8{_}1887{_}Attempting once to scold her in public, Rebecca hit upon the plan of answering her in French, which quite routed the old woman, who did not understand or speak that language.
10111-8{_}1888{_}In order to maintain authority in her school, it became necessary to remove this rebel, this firebrand; and hearing about this time that Sir Pitt Crawley's family was in want of a governess, she actually recommended Miss Sharp for the situation, firebrand and serpent as she was.
10111-8{_}1889{_}"I cannot certainly," she said, "find fault with Miss Sharp's conduct, except to myself; and must allow that her talents and accomplishments are of a high order.
10111-8{_}1890{_}As far as the head goes, at least, she does credit to the educational system pursued at my establishment."
10111-8{_}1891{_}And so the schoolmistress reconciled the recommendation to her conscience, and the apprentice was free.
10111-8{_}1892{_}And as Miss Sedley, being now in her seventeenth year, was about to leave school, and had a friendship for Miss Sharp ("'Tis the only point in Amelia's behaviour," said Miss Minerva, "which has not been satisfactory to her mistress"), Miss Sharp was invited by her friend to pass a week with her in London, before Becky entered upon her duties as governess in a private family; which thoughtfulness on the part of Amelia was only an additional proof of the girl's affectionate nature.
10111-8{_}1893{_}In fact, Miss Amelia Sedley was a young lady who deserved not only all that Miss Pinkerton said in her praise, but had many charming qualities which that pompous old woman could not see, from the differences of rank and age between her pupil and herself.
10111-8{_}1894{_}She could not only sing like a lark, and dance divinely, and embroider beautifully, and spell as well as a "Dixonary" itself, but she had such a kindly, smiling, tender, gentle, generous heart of her own as won the love of everybody who came near her, from Miss Minerva herself down to the poor girl in the scullery and the one-eyed tart woman's daughter, who was permitted to vend her wares once a week to the young ladies in the Mall.
10111-8{_}1895{_}She had twelve intimate and bosom friends out of the twenty-four young ladies.
10111-8{_}1896{_}Even envious Miss Briggs never spoke ill of her: high and mighty Miss Saltire allowed that her figure was genteel; and as for Miss Swartz, the rich woolly-haired mulatto from St. Kitts, on the day Amelia went away she was in such a passion of tears that they were obliged to send for Dr. Floss, and half-tipsify her with salvolatile.
10111-8{_}1897{_}Miss Pinkerton's attachment was, as may be supposed, from the high position and eminent virtues of that lady, calm and dignified; but Miss Jemima had already whimpered several times at the idea of Amelia's departure; and but for fear of her sister would have gone off in downright hysterics, like the heiress of St. Kitts.
10111-8{_}1898{_}As Amelia is not a heroine, there is no need to describe her person; indeed I am afraid that her nose was rather short than otherwise, and her cheeks a great deal too round and red for a heroine; but her face blushed with rosy health, and her lips with the freshest of smiles, and she had a pair of eyes which sparkled with the brightest and honestest good-humour, except indeed when they filled with tears, and that was a great deal too often; for the silly thing would cry over a dead canary bird; or over a mouse that the cat haply had seized upon; or over the end of a novel, were it ever so stupid; and as for saying an unkind word to her, were any persons hard-hearted enough to do so--why so much the worse for them.
10111-8{_}1899{_}Even Miss Pinkerton, that austere woman, ceased scolding her after the first time, and, though she no more comprehended sensibility than she did capital Algebra, gave all masters and teachers particular orders to treat Miss Sedley with the utmost gentleness, as harsh treatment was injurious to her.
10111-8{_}1900{_}So that when the day of departure came, between her two customs of laughing and crying, Miss Sedley was greatly puzzled how to act.
10111-8{_}1901{_}She was glad to go home, and yet most woefully sad at leaving school.
10111-8{_}1902{_}For three days before, little Laura Martin, the orphan, followed her about like a little dog.
10111-8{_}1903{_}She had to make and receive at least fourteen presents, to make fourteen solemn promises of writing every week.
10111-8{_}1904{_}"Send my letters under cover to my grandpa, the Earl of Dexter," said Miss Saltire.
10111-8{_}1905{_}"Never mind the postage, but write every day, you dear darling," said the impetuous and woolly-headed, but generous and affectionate, Miss Schwartz; and little Laura Martin took her friend's hand and said, looking up in her face wistfully, "Amelia, when I write to you I shall call you mamma."
10111-8{_}1906{_}All of these details, foolish and sentimental as they may seem, go to show the extreme popularity and personal charm of Amelia.
10111-8{_}1907{_}Well then.
10111-8{_}1908{_}The flowers, and the presents, and the trunks, and bonnet-boxes of Miss Sedley having been arranged by Mr. Sambo in the carriage, together with a very small and weather-beaten old cowskin trunk with Miss Sharp's card neatly nailed upon it, which was delivered by Sambo with a grin, and packed by the coachman with a corresponding sneer, the hour for parting came; and the grief of that moment was considerably lessened by the admirable discourse which Miss Pinkerton addressed to her pupil.
10111-8{_}1909{_}Not that the parting speech caused Amelia to philosophise, or that it armed her in any way with a calmness, the result of argument; but it was intolerably dull, and having the fear of her schoolmistress greatly before her eyes, Miss Sedley did not venture, in her presence, to give way to any ablutions of private grief.
10111-8{_}1910{_}A seed-cake and a bottle of wine were produced in the drawing-room, as on the solemn occasions of the visits of parents, and these refreshments being partaken of, Miss Sedley was at liberty to depart.
10111-8{_}1911{_}"You'll go in and say good-bye to Miss Pinkerton, Becky!"
10111-8{_}1912{_}said Miss Jemima to that young lady, of whom nobody took any notice, and who was coming downstairs with her own bandbox.
10111-8{_}1913{_}"I suppose I must," said Miss Sharp calmly, and much to the wonder of Miss Jemima; and the latter, having knocked at the door, and receiving permission to come in, Miss Sharp advanced in a very unconcerned manner, and said in French, and with a perfect accent, _"Mademoiselle, je viens vous faire mes adieux.
10111-8{_}1914{_}"_  Miss Pinkerton did not understand French, as we know; she only directed those who did; but biting her lips and throwing up her venerable and Roman-nosed head, she said: "Miss Sharp, I wish you a good-morning."
10111-8{_}1915{_}As she spoke, she waved one hand, both by way of adieu and to give Miss Sharp an opportunity of shaking one of the fingers of the hand, which was left out for that purpose.
10111-8{_}1916{_}Miss Sharp only folded her own hands with a very frigid smile and bow, and quite declined to accept the proffered honour; on which Miss Pinkerton tossed up her turban more indignantly than ever.
10111-8{_}1917{_}In fact, it was a little battle between the young lady and the old one, and the latter was worsted.
10111-8{_}1918{_}"Heaven bless you, my child," she exclaimed, embracing Amelia, and scowling the while over the girl's shoulder at Miss Sharp.
10111-8{_}1919{_}"Come away, Becky," said Miss Jemima, pulling the young woman away in great alarm, and the drawing-room door closed upon them forever.
10111-8{_}1920{_}Then came the struggle and parting below.
10111-8{_}1921{_}Words refuse to tell it.
10111-8{_}1922{_}All the servants were there in the hall--all the dear friends--all the young ladies--even the dancing master, who had just arrived; and there was such a scuffling, and hugging, and kissing, and crying, with the hysterical _yoops_ of Miss Schwartz, the parlour boarder, from her room, as no pen can depict, and as the tender heart would feign pass over.
10111-8{_}1923{_}The embracing was over; they parted--that is, Miss Sedley parted from her friends.
10111-8{_}1924{_}Miss Sharp had demurely entered the carriage some minutes before.
10111-8{_}1925{_}Nobody cried for leaving _her_.
10111-8{_}1926{_}Sambo of the bandy legs slammed the carriage door on his young weeping mistress.
10111-8{_}1927{_}He sprang up behind the carriage.
10111-8{_}1928{_}"Stop!"
10111-8{_}1929{_}cried Miss Jemima, rushing to the gate with a parcel.
10111-8{_}1930{_}"It's some sandwiches, my dear," she called to Amelia.
10111-8{_}1931{_}"You may be hungry, you know; ... and Becky--Becky Sharp--here's a book for you, that my sister--that is, I--Johnson's Dixonary, you know; ... you mustn't leave us without that!
10111-8{_}1932{_}Good-bye!
10111-8{_}1933{_}Drive on, coachman!--God bless you!"
10111-8{_}1934{_}And the kind creature retreated into the garden, overcome with emotion.
10111-8{_}1935{_}But, lo!
10111-8{_}1936{_}and just as the coach drove off, Miss Sharp suddenly put her pale face out of the window, and flung the book back into the garden--flung it far and fast--watching it fall at the feet of astonished Miss Jemima; then sank back in the carriage, exclaiming: "So much for the 'Dixonary'; and, thank God, I am out of Chiswick!"
10111-8{_}1937{_}The shock of such an act almost caused Jemima to faint with terror.
10111-8{_}1938{_}"Well, I never--" she began.
10111-8{_}1939{_}"What an audacious--" she gasped.
10111-8{_}1940{_}Emotion prevented her from completing either sentence.
10111-8{_}1941{_}The carriage rolled away; the great gates were closed; the bell rang for the dancing lesson.
10111-8{_}1942{_}The world is before the two young ladies; and so, farewell to Chiswick Mall.
10111-8{_}1943{_}CUFFS FIGHT WITH "FIGS"   [Illustration: CUFF'S FIGHT WITH "FIGS."]
10111-8{_}1944{_}Cuff's fight with Figs, and the unexpected issue of that contest, will long be remembered by every man who was educated at Dr. Swishtail's famous school.
10111-8{_}1945{_}The latter youth (who used to be called Heigh-ho Dobbin, Gee-ho Dobbin, Figs, and by many other names indicative of puerile contempt) was the quietest, the clumsiest, and, as it seemed, the dullest of all Dr. Swishtail's young gentlemen.
10111-8{_}1946{_}His parent was a grocer in the city: and it was bruited abroad that he was admitted into Dr. Swishtails academy upon what are called "mutual principles"--that is to say, the expenses of his board and schooling were defrayed by his father in goods, not money; and he stood there--almost at the bottom of the school--in his scraggy corduroys and jacket, through the seams of which his great big bones were bursting, as the representative of so many pounds of tea, candles, sugar, mottled-soap, plums (of which a very mild proportion was supplied for the puddings of the establishment), and other commodities.
10111-8{_}1947{_}A dreadful day it was for young Dobbin when one of the youngsters of the school, having run into the town upon a poaching excursion for hardbake and polonies, espied the cart of Dobbin & Rudge, Grocers and Oilmen, Thames Street, London, at the Doctor's door, discharging a cargo of the wares in which the firm dealt.
10111-8{_}1948{_}Young Dobbin had no peace after that.
10111-8{_}1949{_}The jokes were frightful and merciless against him.
10111-8{_}1950{_}"Hullo, Dobbin," one wag would say, "here's good news in the paper.
10111-8{_}1951{_}Sugar is ris', my boy."
10111-8{_}1952{_}Another would set a sum--"If a pound of mutton-candles cost sevenpence-halfpenny, how much must Dobbin cost?"
10111-8{_}1953{_}and a roar would follow from all the circle of young knaves, usher and all, who rightly considered that the selling of goods by retail is a shameful and infamous practice, meriting the contempt and scorn of all real gentlemen.
10111-8{_}1954{_}"Your father's only a merchant, Osborne," Dobbin said in private to the little boy who had brought down the storm upon him.
10111-8{_}1955{_}At which the latter replied haughtily, "My father's a gentleman, and keeps his carriage;" and Mr. William Dobbin retreated to a remote out-house in the playground, where he passed a half-holiday in the bitterest sadness and woe.
10111-8{_}1956{_}Now, William Dobbin, from an incapacity to acquire the rudiments of the Latin language, as they are propounded in that wonderful book, the Eton Latin Grammar, was compelled to remain among the very last of Dr. Swishtail's scholars, and was "taken down" continually by little fellows with pink faces and pinafores when he marched up with the lower form, a giant amongst them, with his downcast, stupefied look, his dog's-eared primer, and his tight corduroys.
10111-8{_}1957{_}High and low, all made fun of him.
10111-8{_}1958{_}They sewed up those corduroys, tight as they were.
10111-8{_}1959{_}They cut his bed-springs.
10111-8{_}1960{_}They upset buckets and benches, so that he might break his shins over them, which he never failed to do.
10111-8{_}1961{_}They sent him parcels, which, when opened, were found to contain the paternal soap and candles.
10111-8{_}1962{_}There was no little fellow but had his jeer and joke at Dobbin; and he bore everything quite patiently, and was entirely dumb and miserable.
10111-8{_}1963{_}Cuff, on the contrary, was the great chief and dandy of the Swishtail Seminary.
10111-8{_}1964{_}He smuggled wine in.
10111-8{_}1965{_}He fought the town-boys.
10111-8{_}1966{_}Ponies used to come for him to ride home on Saturdays.
10111-8{_}1967{_}He had his top-boots in his room in which he used to hunt in the holidays.
10111-8{_}1968{_}He had a gold repeater, and took snuff like the Doctor.
10111-8{_}1969{_}He had been to the Opera, and knew the merits of the principal actors, preferring Mr. Kean to Mr. Kemble.
10111-8{_}1970{_}He could knock you off forty Latin verses in an hour.
10111-8{_}1971{_}He could make French poetry.
10111-8{_}1972{_}What else didn't he know, or couldn't he do?
10111-8{_}1973{_}They said even the Doctor himself was afraid of him.
10111-8{_}1974{_}Cuff, the unquestioned king of the school, ruled over his subjects, and bullied them, with splendid superiority.
10111-8{_}1975{_}This one blacked his shoes, that toasted his bread, others would fag out, and give him balls at cricket during whole summer afternoons.
10111-8{_}1976{_}Figs was the fellow whom he despised most, and with whom, though always abusing him, and sneering at him, he scarcely ever condescended to hold personal communication.
10111-8{_}1977{_}One day in private the two young gentlemen had had a difference.
10111-8{_}1978{_}Figs, alone in the school-room, was blundering over a home letter, when Cuff, entering, bade him go upon some message, of which tarts were probably the subject.
10111-8{_}1979{_}"I can't," says Dobbin; "I want to finish my letter."
10111-8{_}1980{_}"You _can't?_" says Mr. Cuff, laying hold of that document (in which many words were scratched out, many were misspelt, on which had been spent I don't know how much thought, and labour, and tears; for the poor fellow was writing to his mother, who was fond of him, although she was a grocer's wife, and lived in a back parlour in Thames Street).
10111-8{_}1981{_}"You _can't?
10111-8{_}1982{_}"_ says Mr. Cuff.
10111-8{_}1983{_}"I should like to know why, pray?
10111-8{_}1984{_}Can't you write to old Mother Figs tomorrow?"
10111-8{_}1985{_}"Don't call names," Dobbin said, getting off the bench, very nervous.
10111-8{_}1986{_}"Well, sir, will you go?"
10111-8{_}1987{_}crowed the cock of the school.
10111-8{_}1988{_}"Put down the letter," Dobbin replied; "no gentleman readth letterth."
10111-8{_}1989{_}"Well, _now_ will you go?"
10111-8{_}1990{_}says the other.
10111-8{_}1991{_}"No, I won't.
10111-8{_}1992{_}Don't strike, or I'll _thmash_ you," roars out Dobbin, springing to a leaden inkstand, and looking so wicked that Mr. Cuff paused, turned down his coat sleeves again, put his hands into his pockets, and walked away with a sneer.
10111-8{_}1993{_}But he never meddled personally with the grocer's boy after that; though we must do him the justice to say he always spoke of Mr. Dobbin with contempt behind his back.
10111-8{_}1994{_}Some time after this interview it happened that Mr. Cuff, on a sunshiny afternoon, was in the neighbourhood of poor William Dobbin, who was lying under a tree in the playground, spelling over a favourite copy of the "Arabian Nights" which he had--apart from the rest of the school, who were pursuing their various sports--quite lonely, and almost happy.
10111-8{_}1995{_}Well, William Dobbin had for once forgotten the world, and was away with Sindbad the Sailor in the Valley of Diamonds, or with Prince Ahmed and the Fairy Peribanou in that delightful cavern where the Prince found her, and whither we should all like to make a tour, when shrill cries, as of a little fellow weeping, woke up his pleasant reverie, and, looking up, he saw Cuff before him, belabouring a little boy.
10111-8{_}1996{_}It was the lad who had peached upon him about the grocer's cart, but he bore little malice, not at least towards the young and small.
10111-8{_}1997{_}"How dare you, sir, break the bottle?"
10111-8{_}1998{_}says Cuff to the little urchin, swinging a yellow cricket-stump over him.
10111-8{_}1999{_}The boy had been instructed to get over the playground wall (at a selected spot where the broken glass had been removed from the top, and niches made convenient in the brick), to run a quarter of a mile, to purchase a pint of rum-shrub on credit, to brave all the Doctor's outlying spies, and to clamber back into the playground again; during the performance of which feat his foot had slipped, and the bottle broken, and the shrub had been spilt, and his pantaloons had been damaged, and he appeared before his employer a perfectly guilty and trembling, though harmless, wretch.
10111-8{_}2000{_}"How dare you, sir, break it?"
10111-8{_}2001{_}says Cuff; "you blundering little thief.
10111-8{_}2002{_}You drank the shrub, and now you pretend to have broken the bottle.
10111-8{_}2003{_}Hold out your hand, sir."
10111-8{_}2004{_}Down came the stump with a great heavy thump on the child's hand.
10111-8{_}2005{_}A moan followed.
10111-8{_}2006{_}Dobbin looked up.
10111-8{_}2007{_}The Fairy Peribanou had fled into the inmost cavern with Prince Ahmed; the Roc had whisked away Sindbad, the Sailor, out of the Valley of Diamonds, out of sight, far into the clouds; and there was every-day life before honest William; and a big boy beating a little one without cause.
10111-8{_}2008{_}"Hold out your other hand, sir," roars Cuff to his little school-fellow, whose face was distorted with pain.
10111-8{_}2009{_}Dobbin quivered, and gathered himself up in his narrow old clothes.
10111-8{_}2010{_}"Take that, you little devil!"
10111-8{_}2011{_}cried Mr. Cuff, and down came the wicket again on the child's hand.
10111-8{_}2012{_}Down came the wicket again, and Dobbin started up.
10111-8{_}2013{_}I can't tell what his motive was.
10111-8{_}2014{_}Perhaps his foolish soul revolted against that exercise of tyranny, or perhaps he had a hankering feeling of revenge in his mind, and longed to measure himself against that splendid bully and tyrant, who had all the glory, pride, pomp, circumstance, banners flying, drums beating, guards saluting, in the place.
10111-8{_}2015{_}Whatever may have been his incentive, however, up he sprang, and screamed out, "Hold off, Cuff; don't bully that child any more, or I'll--"  "Or you'll what?"
10111-8{_}2016{_}Cuff asked in amazement at this interruption.
10111-8{_}2017{_}"Hold out your hand, you little beast."
10111-8{_}2018{_}"I'll give you the worst thrashing you ever had in your life," Dobbin said, in reply to the first part of Cuff's sentence; and the little lad, Osborne, gasping and in tears, looked up with wonder and incredulity at seeing this amazing champion put up suddenly to defend him, while Cuff's astonishment was scarcely less.
10111-8{_}2019{_}Fancy our late monarch George III., when he heard of the revolt of the North American colonies; fancy brazen Goliath when little David stepped forward and claimed a meeting; and you have the feeling of Mr. Reginald Cuff when this encounter was proposed to him.
10111-8{_}2020{_}"After school," says he, "of course," after a pause and a look, as much as to say, "Make your will, and communicate your last wishes to your friends between this time and that."
10111-8{_}2021{_}"As you please," Dobbin said.
10111-8{_}2022{_}"You must be my bottle-holder, Osborne."
10111-8{_}2023{_}"Well, if you like," little Osborne replied; for you see his papa kept a carriage, and he was rather ashamed of his champion.
10111-8{_}2024{_}Yes, when the hour of battle came he was almost ashamed to say, "Go it, Figs"; and not a single other boy in the place uttered that cry for the first two or three rounds of this famous combat; at the commencement of which the scientific Cuff, with a contemptuous smile on his face, and as light and as gay as if he was at a ball, planted his blows upon his adversary, and floored that unlucky champion three times running.
10111-8{_}2025{_}At each fall there was a cheer, and everybody was anxious to have the honour of offering the conqueror a knee.
10111-8{_}2026{_}"What a licking I shall get when it's over," young Osborne thought, picking up his man.
10111-8{_}2027{_}"You'd best give in," he said to Dobbin; "it's only a thrashing, Figs, and you know I'm used to it."
10111-8{_}2028{_}But Figs, all whose limbs were in a quiver, and whose nostrils were breathing rage, put his little bottle-holder aside, and went in for a fourth time.
10111-8{_}2029{_}As he did not in the least know how to parry the blows that were aimed at himself, and Cuff had begun the attack on the three preceding occasions without ever allowing his enemy to strike, Figs now determined that he would commence the engagement by a charge on his own part; and, accordingly, being a left-handed man, brought that arm into action, and hit out a couple of times with all his might--once at Mr. Cuff's left eye, and once on his beautiful Roman nose.
10111-8{_}2030{_}Cuff went down this time, to the astonishment of the assembly.
10111-8{_}2031{_}"Well hit, by Jove," says little Osborne, with the air of a connoisseur, clapping his man on the back.
10111-8{_}2032{_}"Give it to him with the left, Figs, my boy."
10111-8{_}2033{_}Figs's left made terrific play during all the rest of the combat.
10111-8{_}2034{_}Cuff went down every time.
10111-8{_}2035{_}At the sixth round there were almost as many fellows shouting out, "Go it, Figs," as there were youths exclaiming, "Go it, Cuff."
10111-8{_}2036{_}At the twelfth round the latter champion was all abroad, as the saying is, and had lost all presence of mind and power of attack or defence.
10111-8{_}2037{_}Figs, on the contrary, was as calm as a Quaker.
10111-8{_}2038{_}His face being quite pale, his eyes shining open, and a great cut on his under lip bleeding profusely, gave this young fellow a fierce and ghastly air, which perhaps struck terror into many spectators.
10111-8{_}2039{_}Nevertheless, his intrepid adversary prepared to close for the thirteenth time.
10111-8{_}2040{_}If I had the pen of a Napier, or a Bell's Life, I should like to describe this combat properly.
10111-8{_}2041{_}It was the last charge of the Guard--(that is, it _would_ have been, only Waterloo had not yet taken place); it was Ney's column breasting the hill of La Haye Sainte, bristling with ten thousand bayonets, and crowned with twenty eagles; it was the shout of the beef-eating British, as, leaping down the hill, they rushed to hug the enemy in the savage arms of battle; in other words, Cuff, coming up full of pluck, but quite reeling and groggy, the Fig-merchant put in his left as usual on his adversary's nose, and sent him down for the last time.
10111-8{_}2042{_}"I think _that_ will do for him," Figs said, as his opponent dropped as neatly on the green as I have seen Jack Spot's ball plump into the pocket at billiards; and the fact is, when time was called, Mr. Reginald Cuff was not able, or did not choose, to stand up again.
10111-8{_}2043{_}And now all the boys set up such a shout for Figs as would have made you think he had been their darling champion through the whole battle; and as absolutely brought Dr. Swishtail out of his study, curious to know the cause of the uproar.
10111-8{_}2044{_}He threatened to flog Figs violently, of course; but Cuff, who had come to himself by this time, and was washing his wounds, stood up and said, "It's my fault, sir--not Figs's--not Dobbin's.
10111-8{_}2045{_}I was bullying a little boy; and he served me right."
10111-8{_}2046{_}By which magnanimous speech he not only saved his conqueror a whipping, but got back all his ascendancy over the boys which his defeat had nearly cost him.
10111-8{_}2047{_}Young Osborne wrote home to his parents an account of the transaction:         *       *       *       *       *  SUGARCANE HOUSE, RICHMOND, March 18--  _Dear Mamma_: I hope you are quite well.
10111-8{_}2048{_}I should be much obliged to you to send me a cake and five shillings.
10111-8{_}2049{_}There has been a fight here between Cuff & Dobbin.
10111-8{_}2050{_}Cuff, you know, was the Cock of the School.
10111-8{_}2051{_}They fought thirteen rounds, and Dobbin Licked.
10111-8{_}2052{_}So Cuff is now Only Second Cock.
10111-8{_}2053{_}The fight was about me.
10111-8{_}2054{_}Cuff was licking me for breaking a bottle of milk, and Figs wouldn't stand it.
10111-8{_}2055{_}We call him Figs because his father is a Grocer--Figs & Rudge, Thames St., City.
10111-8{_}2056{_}I think as he fought for me you ought to buy your Tea & Sugar at his father's.
10111-8{_}2057{_}Cuff goes home every Saturday, but can't this, because he has 2 Black Eyes.
10111-8{_}2058{_}He has a white Pony to come and fetch him, and a groom and livery on a bay mare.
10111-8{_}2059{_}I wish my Papa would let me have a Pony, and I am  Your dutiful Son,  GEORGE SEDLEY OSBORNE.
10111-8{_}2060{_}P.S.--Give my love to little Emmy.
10111-8{_}2061{_}I am cutting her out a Coach in card-board.
10111-8{_}2062{_}Please not a seed-cake, but a plum-cake.
10111-8{_}2063{_}*       *       *       *       *  In consequence of Dobbin's victory, his character rose prodigiously in the estimation of all his school fellows, and the name of Figs, which had been a byword of reproach, became as respectable and popular a nickname as any other in use in the school.
10111-8{_}2064{_}"After all, it's not his fault that his father's a grocer," George Osborne said, who, though a little chap, had a very high popularity among the Swishtail youth; and his opinion was received with great applause.
10111-8{_}2065{_}It was voted low to sneer at Dobbin about this accident of birth.
10111-8{_}2066{_}"Old Figs" grew to be a name of kindness and endearment, and the sneak of an usher jeered at him no longer.
10111-8{_}2067{_}And Dobbin's spirit rose with his altered circumstances.
10111-8{_}2068{_}He made wonderful advances in scholastic learning.
10111-8{_}2069{_}The superb Cuff himself, at whose condenscension Dobbin could only blush and wonder, helped him on with his Latin verses, "coached" him in play-hours, carried him triumphantly out of the little-boy class into the middle-sized form, and even there got a fair place for him.
10111-8{_}2070{_}It was discovered that, although dull at classical learning, at mathematics he was uncommonly quick.
10111-8{_}2071{_}To the contentment of all he passed third in Algebra, and got a French prize-book at the public Midsummer examination.
10111-8{_}2072{_}You should have seen his mother's face when Telemaque (that delicious romance) was presented to him by the Doctor in the face of the whole school and the parents and company, with an inscription to Guielmo Dobbin.
10111-8{_}2073{_}All the boys clapped hands in token of applause and sympathy.
10111-8{_}2074{_}His blushes, his stumbles, his awkwardness, and the number of feet which he crushed as he went back to his place, who shall describe or calculate?
10111-8{_}2075{_}Old Dobbin, his father, who now respected him for the first time, gave him two guineas publicly; most of which he spent in a general tuck-out for the school: and he came back in a tail-coat after the holidays.
10111-8{_}2076{_}Dobbin was much too modest a young fellow to suppose that this happy change in all his circumstances arose from his own generous and manly disposition; he chose, from some perverseness, to attribute his good fortune to the sole agency and benevolence of little George Osborne, to whom henceforth he vowed such a love and affection as is only felt by children, an affection as we read of in the charming fairy-book, which uncouth Orson had for splendid young Valentine, his conqueror.
10111-8{_}2077{_}He flung himself down at little Osborne's feet, and loved him.
10111-8{_}2078{_}Even before they were acquainted, he had admired Osborne in secret.
10111-8{_}2079{_}Now he was his valet, his dog, his man Friday.
10111-8{_}2080{_}He believed Osborne to be the possessor of every perfection, to be the handsomest, the bravest, the most active, the cleverest, the most generous of boys.
10111-8{_}2081{_}He shared his money with him, bought him uncountable presents of knives, pencil cases, gold seals, toffee, little warblers, and romantic books, with large coloured pictures of knights and robbers, in many of which latter you might read inscriptions to George Sedley Osborne, Esquire, from his attached friend William Dobbin--which tokens of homage George received very graciously, as became his superior merit, as often and as long as they were proffered him.
10111-8{_}2082{_}In after years Dobbin's father, the despised grocer, became Alderman, and Colonel of the City Light Horse, in which corps George Osborne's father was but an indifferent Corporal.
10111-8{_}2083{_}Colonel Dobbin was knighted by his sovereign, which honour placed his son William in a social position above that of the old school friends who had once been so scornful of him at Swishtail Academy; even above the object of his deepest admiration, George Osborne.
10111-8{_}2084{_}But this did not in the least alter honest, simple-minded William Dobbin's feelings, and his adoration for young Osborne remained unchanged.
10111-8{_}2085{_}The two entered the army in the same regiment, and served together, and Dobbin's attachment for George was as warm and loyal then as when they were school-boys together.
10111-8{_}2086{_}Honest William Dobbin,--I would that there were more such staunch comrades as you to answer to the name of friend!
10111-8{_}2087{_}GEORGE OSBORNE--RAWDON CRAWLEY   [Illustration: GEORGE OSBORNE AND RAWDON CRAWLEY.]
10111-8{_}2088{_}Rebecca sharp, the teacher of French at Miss Pinkerton's Academy for young ladies, and intimate friend of Miss Amelia Sedley, the most popular scholar in Miss Pinkerton's select establishment, left the institution at the same time to become a governess in the family of Sir Pitt Crawley.
10111-8{_}2089{_}Amelia was the only daughter of John Sedley, a wealthy London stock broker, and upon leaving school was to take her place in fashionable society.
10111-8{_}2090{_}Being the sweetest, most kind-hearted girl in the world, Amelia invited Becky to visit her in London before taking up her new duties as governess; which invitation Becky was only too glad to accept.
10111-8{_}2091{_}Now, Miss Sharp was in no way like the gentle Amelia, but as keen, brilliant, and selfish a young person of eighteen as ever schemed to have events turn to her advantage.
10111-8{_}2092{_}These characteristics she showed so plainly while visiting at the Sedleys' that she left anything but a good impression behind her.
10111-8{_}2093{_}In fact, her visit was cut short because of some unpleasant circumstances connected with her behaviour.
10111-8{_}2094{_}From that time she and Amelia did not meet for many months, during which Amelia had become the wife of George Osborne, and Rebecca Sharp had married Rawdon Crawley, son of Sir Pitt Crawley, Baronet.
10111-8{_}2095{_}The circumstances of Amelia's life during these months altered greatly, for shortly after she left school honest John Sedley met with such severe losses that his family were obliged to live in a much more modest way than formerly.
10111-8{_}2096{_}Because of this misfortune, the course of Amelia's love affair with young Lieutenant Osborne did not run smoothly; for his father was far too ambitious to consent to his only son's marriage with the daughter of a ruined man, although John Sedley was his son's godfather, and George had been devoted to Amelia since early boyhood.
10111-8{_}2097{_}Lieutenant Osborne therefore went away with his regiment, and poor little Amelia was left behind, to pine and mourn until it seemed there was no hope of saving her life unless happiness should speedily come to her.
10111-8{_}2098{_}Then it was that Major Dobbin, George Osborne's staunch friend of schooldays, and also an ardent admirer of Amelia's, saw how she was grieving and took upon himself to inform George Osborne of the state of affairs.
10111-8{_}2099{_}The young lieutenant came hurrying home just in time to save a gentle little heart from wearing itself away with sorrowing, and married Amelia without his father's consent.
10111-8{_}2100{_}This so enraged the old gentleman that he refused to have his name mentioned in the home where the boy had grown up; the veriest tyrant and idol of his sisters and father.
10111-8{_}2101{_}To Brighton George and Amelia went on their honeymoon, and there they met Becky Sharp and her husband.
10111-8{_}2102{_}Though the circumstances of the two young women's career had altered, Amelia and Becky were unchanged in character, but that is of small concern to us, except as it affects their children, to whose lives we now turn with keen interest, noting how they reflect the dispositions, and are affected by the characters of their mothers.
10111-8{_}2103{_}As for little Rawdon Crawley, Becky's only child, he had few early happy recollections of his mother.
10111-8{_}2104{_}She had not, to say the truth, seen much of the young gentleman since his birth.
10111-8{_}2105{_}After the amiable fashion of French mothers, she had placed him out at nurse in a village in the neighbourhood of Paris, where little Rawdon lived, not unhappily, with a numerous family of foster brothers in wooden shoes.
10111-8{_}2106{_}His father, who was devotedly attached to the little fellow, would ride over many a time to see him here, and the elder Rawdon's paternal heart glowed to see him rosy and dirty, shouting lustily, and happy in the making of mud-pies under the superintendence of the gardener's wife, his nurse.
10111-8{_}2107{_}Rebecca, however, did not care much to go and see her son and heir, who as a result preferred his nurse's caresses to his mamma's, and when finally he quitted that jolly nurse, he cried loudly for hours.
10111-8{_}2108{_}He was only consoled by his mother's promise that he should return to his nurse the next day; which promise, it is needless to say, was not kept; instead the boy was consigned to the care of a French maid, Genevieve, while his mother was seldom with him, and the French woman was so neglectful of her young charge that at one time he very narrowly escaped drowning on Calais sands, where Genevieve had left and lost him.
10111-8{_}2109{_}So with little care and less love his childhood passed until presently he went with his father and mother, Colonel and Mrs. Crawley, to London, to their new home in Curzon Street, Mayfair.
10111-8{_}2110{_}There little Rawdon's time was mostly spent hidden upstairs in a garret somewhere, or crawling below into the kitchen for companionship.
10111-8{_}2111{_}His mother scarcely ever took notice of him.
10111-8{_}2112{_}He passed the days with his French nurse as long as she remained in the family, and when she went away, a housemaid took compassion on the little fellow, who was howling in the loneliness of the night, and got him out of his solitary nursery into her bed in the garret and comforted him.
10111-8{_}2113{_}Rebecca, her friend, my Lord Steyne, and one or two more were in the drawing-room taking tea after the opera, when this shouting was heard overhead.
10111-8{_}2114{_}"It's my cherub crying for his nurse," said his mother, who did not offer to move and go and see the child.
10111-8{_}2115{_}"Don't agitate your feelings by going to look after him," said Lord Steyne sardonically.
10111-8{_}2116{_}"Bah!"
10111-8{_}2117{_}exclaimed Becky, with a sort of blush.
10111-8{_}2118{_}"He'll cry himself to sleep"; and they fell to talking about the opera.
10111-8{_}2119{_}Mr. Rawdon Crawley had stolen off, however, to look after his son and heir; and came back to the company when he found that honest Dolly was consoling the child.
10111-8{_}2120{_}The Colonel's dressing-room was in those upper regions.
10111-8{_}2121{_}He used to see the boy there in private.
10111-8{_}2122{_}They had interviews together every morning when he shaved; Rawdon minor sitting on a box by his father's side, and watching the operation with never-ceasing pleasure.
10111-8{_}2123{_}He and the sire were great friends.
10111-8{_}2124{_}The father would bring him sweet-meats from the dessert, and hide them in a certain old epaulet box where the child went to seek them, and laughed with joy on discovering the treasure; laughed, but not too loud; for mamma was asleep and must not be disturbed.
10111-8{_}2125{_}She did not go to rest until very late, and seldom rose until afternoon.
10111-8{_}2126{_}His father bought the boy plenty of picture books, and crammed his nursery with toys.
10111-8{_}2127{_}Its walls were covered with pictures pasted up by the father's own hand.
10111-8{_}2128{_}He passed hours with the boy, who rode on his chest, pulled his great moustaches as if they were driving reins, and spent days with him in indefatigable gambols.
10111-8{_}2129{_}The room was a low one, and once, when the child was not five years old, his father, who was tossing him wildly up in his arms, hit the poor little chap's scull so violently against the ceiling that he almost dropped him, so terrified was he at the disaster.
10111-8{_}2130{_}Rawdon minor had made up his face for a tremendous howl, but just as he was going to begin, the father interposed.
10111-8{_}2131{_}"For God's sake, Rawdy, don't wake mamma," he cried.
10111-8{_}2132{_}And the child, looking in a very hard and piteous way at his father, bit his lips, clenched his hands, and didn't cry a bit.
10111-8{_}2133{_}Rawdon told that story at the clubs, at the mess, to everybody in town.
10111-8{_}2134{_}"By Gad, sir," he explained to the public in general, "what a good plucky one that boy of mine is.
10111-8{_}2135{_}What a trump he is!
10111-8{_}2136{_}I half sent his head through the ceiling, and he wouldn't cry for fear of disturbing mother!"
10111-8{_}2137{_}Sometimes, once or twice in a week, that lady visited the upper regions in which the child lived.
10111-8{_}2138{_}She came like a vivified picture, blandly smiling in the most beautiful new clothes and little gloves and boots.
10111-8{_}2139{_}Wonderful scarfs, laces, and jewels glittered about her.
10111-8{_}2140{_}She had always a new bonnet on; and flowers bloomed perpetually in it, or else magnificent curling ostrich feathers, soft and snowy as camellias.
10111-8{_}2141{_}She nodded twice or thrice patronisingly to the little boy, who looked up from his dinner or from the pictures of soldiers he was painting.
10111-8{_}2142{_}When she left the room, an odour of rose, or some other magical fragrance, lingered about the nursery.
10111-8{_}2143{_}She was an unearthly being in his eyes, superior to his father, to all the world, to be worshipped and admired at a distance.
10111-8{_}2144{_}To drive with that lady in a carriage was an awful rite.
10111-8{_}2145{_}He sat in the back seat, and did not dare to speak; he gazed with all his eyes at the beautifully dressed princess opposite to him.
10111-8{_}2146{_}Gentlemen on splendid prancing horses came up, and smiled and talked with her.
10111-8{_}2147{_}How her eyes beamed upon all of them!
10111-8{_}2148{_}Her hand used to quiver and wave gracefully as they passed.
10111-8{_}2149{_}When he went out with her he had his new red dress on.
10111-8{_}2150{_}His old brown holland was good enough when he stayed at home.
10111-8{_}2151{_}Sometimes, when she was away, and Dolly the maid was making his bed, he came into his mother's room.
10111-8{_}2152{_}It was as the abode of a fairy to him--a mystic chamber of splendour and delight.
10111-8{_}2153{_}There in the wardrobe hung those wonderful robes--pink and blue and many-tinted.
10111-8{_}2154{_}There was the jewel case, silver clasped; and a hundred rings on the dressing table.
10111-8{_}2155{_}There was a cheval glass, that miracle of art, in which he could just see his own wondering head, and the reflection of Dolly, plumping and patting the pillows of the bed.
10111-8{_}2156{_}Poor lonely little benighted boy!
10111-8{_}2157{_}Mother is the name for God in the lips and hearts of little children; and here was one who was worshipping a stone!
10111-8{_}2158{_}His father used to take him out of mornings, when they would go to the stables together and to the park.
10111-8{_}2159{_}Little Lord Southdown, the best natured of men, who would make you a present of a hat from his head, and whose main occupation in life was to buy nicknacks that he might give them away afterwards, bought the little chap a pony, not much bigger than a large rat, and on this little black Shetland pony young Rawdon's great father would mount the boy, and walk by his side in the Park.
10111-8{_}2160{_}One Sunday morning as Rawdon Crawley, his little son, and the pony were taking their accustomed walk, they passed an old acquaintance of the Colonel's, Corporal Clink, who was in conversation with an old gentleman, who held a boy in his arms about the age of little Rawdon.
10111-8{_}2161{_}The other youngster had seized hold of the Waterloo medal which the Corporal wore, and was examining it with delight.
10111-8{_}2162{_}"Good-morning, your honour," said Clink, in reply to the "How do, Clink?"
10111-8{_}2163{_}of the Colonel.
10111-8{_}2164{_}"This 'ere young gentleman is about the little Colonel's age, sir," continued the Corporal.
10111-8{_}2165{_}"His father was a Waterloo man, too," said the old gentleman who carried the boy.
10111-8{_}2166{_}"Wasn't he, Georgie?"
10111-8{_}2167{_}"Yes, sir," said Georgie.
10111-8{_}2168{_}He and the little chap on the pony were looking at each other with all their might, solemnly scanning each other as children do.
10111-8{_}2169{_}"His father was a captain in the--the regiment," said the old gentleman rather pompously.
10111-8{_}2170{_}"Captain George Osborne, sir--perhaps you knew him.
10111-8{_}2171{_}He died the death of a hero, sir, fighting against the Corsican tyrant"  "I knew him very well, sir," said Colonel Crawley, "and his wife, his dear little wife, sir--how is she?"
10111-8{_}2172{_}"She is my daughter, sir," said the old gentleman proudly, putting down the boy, and taking out his card, which he handed to the Colonel, while little Georgie went up and looked at the Shetland pony.
10111-8{_}2173{_}"Should you like to have a ride?"
10111-8{_}2174{_}said Rawdon minor from the saddle.
10111-8{_}2175{_}"Yes," said Georgie.
10111-8{_}2176{_}The Colonel, who had been looking at him with some interest, took up the child and put him on the pony behind Rawdon minor.
10111-8{_}2177{_}"Take hold of him, Georgie," he said; "take my little boy around the waist; his name is Rawdon."
10111-8{_}2178{_}And both the children began to laugh.
10111-8{_}2179{_}"You won't see a prettier pair, I think, this summer's day, sir," said the good-natured Corporal; and the Colonel, the Corporal, and old Mr. Sedley, with his umbrella, walked by the side of the children, who enjoyed each other and the pony enormously.
10111-8{_}2180{_}In later years they often talked of that first meeting.
10111-8{_}2181{_}But this is anticipating our story, for between the time of their first ride together, and the time when circumstances brought them together again, the little chaps saw nothing of one another for a number of years, during which the incidents of their lives differed as widely as did the lives of their parents.
10111-8{_}2182{_}About the time when the little boys first met, Sir Pitt Crawley, Baronet, father of Pitt and Rawdon Crawley, died, and Rebecca and her husband hastened to Queen's Crawley, the old family home, where Rebecca had once been governess, to shed a last tear over the departed Baronet.
10111-8{_}2183{_}Rebecca was not bowed down with grief, we must confess, but keenly alive to the benefits which might come to herself and Rawdon if she could please Sir Pitt Crawley, the new Baronet, and Lady Jane his wife, a simple-minded woman mostly absorbed in the affairs of her nursery.
10111-8{_}2184{_}This interest aroused Becky's private scorn, but the first thing that clever little lady did was to attack Lady Jane at her vulnerable point.
10111-8{_}2185{_}After being conducted to the apartments prepared for her, and having taken off her bonnet and cloak, Becky asked her sister-in-law in what more she could be useful.
10111-8{_}2186{_}"What I should like best," she added, "would be to see your dear little nursery," at which the two ladies looked very kindly at each other, and went to the nursery hand in hand.
10111-8{_}2187{_}Becky admired little Matilda, who was not quite four years old, as the most charming little love in the world; and the boy, Pitt Blinkie Southdown, a little fellow of two years, pale, heavy-eyed, and large-headed, she pronounced to be a perfect prodigy in size, intelligence and beauty.
10111-8{_}2188{_}The funeral over, Rebecca and her husband remained for a visit at Queen's Crawley, which assumed its wonted aspect.
10111-8{_}2189{_}Rawdon senior received constant bulletins respecting little Rawdon, who was left behind in London, and sent messages of his own.
10111-8{_}2190{_}"I am very well," he wrote.
10111-8{_}2191{_}"I hope you are very well.
10111-8{_}2192{_}I hope mamma is very well.
10111-8{_}2193{_}The pony is very well.
10111-8{_}2194{_}Grey takes me to ride in the Park.
10111-8{_}2195{_}I can canter.
10111-8{_}2196{_}I met the little boy who rode before.
10111-8{_}2197{_}He cried when he cantered.
10111-8{_}2198{_}I do not cry."
10111-8{_}2199{_}Rawdon read these letters to his brother, and Lady Jane, who was delighted with them, gave Rebecca a banknote, begging her to buy a present with it for her little nephew.
10111-8{_}2200{_}Like all other good things, the visit came to an end, and one night the London lamps flashed joyfully as the stage rolled into Piccadilly, and Briggs had made a beautiful fire on the hearth in Curzon Street, and little Rawdon was up to welcome back his papa and mamma.
10111-8{_}2201{_}At this time he was a fine open-faced boy, with blue eyes and waving flaxen hair, sturdy in limb, but generous and soft in heart, fondly attaching himself to all who were good to him: to the pony, to Lord Southdown, who gave him the horse; to the groom who had charge of the pony; to Molly the cook, who crammed him with ghost stories at night and with good things from the dinner; to Briggs, his meek, devoted attendant, whom he plagued and laughed at; and to his father especially.
10111-8{_}2202{_}Here, as he grew to be about eight years old, his attachment may be said to have ended.
10111-8{_}2203{_}The beautiful mother vision had faded away after a while.
10111-8{_}2204{_}During nearly two years his mother had scarcely spoken to the child.
10111-8{_}2205{_}She disliked him.
10111-8{_}2206{_}He had the measles and the whooping cough.
10111-8{_}2207{_}He bored her.
10111-8{_}2208{_}One day when he was standing at the landing-place, having crept down from the upper regions, attracted by the sound of his mother's voice, who was singing to Lord Steyne, the drawing-room door opening suddenly discovered the little spy, who but a moment before had been rapt in delight and listening to the music.
10111-8{_}2209{_}His mother came out and struck him violently a couple of boxes on the ear.
10111-8{_}2210{_}He heard a laugh from the Marquis in the inner room, and fled down below to his friends of the kitchen, bursting in an agony of grief.
10111-8{_}2211{_}"It is not because it hurts me," little Rawdon gasped out, "only--only--" sobs and tears wound up the sentence in a storm.
10111-8{_}2212{_}It was the little boy's heart that was bleeding.
10111-8{_}2213{_}"Why mayn't I hear her singing?
10111-8{_}2214{_}Why don't she ever sing to me, as she does to that bald-headed man with the large teeth?"
10111-8{_}2215{_}He gasped out at various intervals these exclamations of grief and rage.
10111-8{_}2216{_}The cook looked at the housemaid; the housemaid looked knowingly at the footman, who all sat in judgment on Rebecca from that moment.
10111-8{_}2217{_}After this incident the mother's dislike increased to hatred; the consciousness that the child was in the house was a reproach and a pain to her.
10111-8{_}2218{_}His very sight annoyed her.
10111-8{_}2219{_}Fear, doubt, and resistance sprang up too, in the boy's own bosom.
10111-8{_}2220{_}He and his mother were separated from that day of the boxes on the ear.
10111-8{_}2221{_}Lord Steyne also disliked the boy.
10111-8{_}2222{_}When they met he made sarcastic bows or remarks to the child, or glared at him with savage-looking eyes.
10111-8{_}2223{_}Rawdon used to stare him in the face and double his little fists in return.
10111-8{_}2224{_}Had it not been for his father, the child would have been desolate indeed, in his own home.
10111-8{_}2225{_}But an unexpected good time came to him a day or two before Christmas, when he was taken by his father and mother to pass the holidays at Queen's Crawley.
10111-8{_}2226{_}Becky would have liked to leave him at home, but for Lady Jane's urgent invitation to the youngster; and the symptoms of revolt and discontent manifested by Rawdon at her neglect of her son.
10111-8{_}2227{_}"He is the finest boy in England," the father said reproachfully, "and you don't seem to care for him as much as you do for your spaniel.
10111-8{_}2228{_}He shan't bother you much; at home he will be away from you in the nursery, and he shall go outside on the coach with me."
10111-8{_}2229{_}So little Rawdon was wrapped up in shawls and comforters for the winter's journey, and hoisted respectfully onto the roof of the coach in the dark morning; with no small delight watched the dawn arise, and made his first journey to the place which his father still called home.
10111-8{_}2230{_}It was a journey of infinite pleasure to the boy, to whom the incidents of the road afforded endless interest; his father answering all questions connected with it, and telling him who lived in the great white house to the right, and whom the park belonged to.
10111-8{_}2231{_}Presently the boy fell asleep, and it was dark when he was wakened up to enter his uncle's carriage at Mudbury, and he sat and looked out of it wondering as the great iron gates flew open, and at the white trunks of the limes as they swept by, until they stopped at length before the lighted windows of the Hall, which were blazing and comfortable with Christmas welcome.
10111-8{_}2232{_}The hall-door was flung open; a big fire was burning in the great old fireplace, a carpet was down over the chequered black flags, and the next instant Becky was kissing Lady Jane.
10111-8{_}2233{_}She and Sir Pitt performed the same salute with great gravity, while Sir Pitt's two children came up to their cousin.
10111-8{_}2234{_}Matilda held out her hand and kissed him.
10111-8{_}2235{_}Pitt Blinkie Southdown, the son and heir, stood aloof, and examined him as a little dog does a big one.
10111-8{_}2236{_}Then the kind hostess conducted her guests to snug apartments blazing with cheerful fires, and after some conversation with the fine young ladies of the house, the great dinner bell having rung, the family assembled at dinner, at which meal Rawdon junior was placed by his aunt, and exhibited not only a fine appetite, but a gentlemanlike behaviour.
10111-8{_}2237{_}"I like to dine here," he said to his aunt when he had completed his meal, at the conclusion of which, and after a decent grace by Sir Pitt, the younger son and heir was introduced and was perched on a high chair by the Baronet's side, while the daughter took possession of the place prepared for her, near her mother.
10111-8{_}2238{_}"I like to dine here," said Rawdon minor, looking up at his relation's kind face.
10111-8{_}2239{_}"Why?"
10111-8{_}2240{_}said the good Lady Jane.
10111-8{_}2241{_}"I dine in the kitchen when I am at home," replied Rawdon minor, "or else with Briggs."
10111-8{_}2242{_}This honest confession was fortunately not heard by Becky, who was deep in conversation with the Baronet, or it might have been worse for little Rawdon.
10111-8{_}2243{_}As a guest, and it being the first night of his arrival, he was allowed to sit up until the hour when, tea being over and a great gilt book being laid on the table before Sir Pitt, all the domestics of the family streamed in and Sir Pitt read prayers.
10111-8{_}2244{_}It was the first time the poor little boy had ever witnessed or heard of such a ceremonial.
10111-8{_}2245{_}Queen's Crawley had been much improved since the young Baronet's brief reign, and was pronounced by Becky to be perfect, charming, delightful, when she surveyed it in his company.
10111-8{_}2246{_}As for little Rawdon, who examined it with the children for his guides, it seemed to him a perfect palace of enchantment and wonder.
10111-8{_}2247{_}There were long galleries, and ancient state bed-rooms; there were pictures and old china and armour which enchanted little Rawdon, who had never seen their like before, and who, poor child, had never before been in such an atmosphere of kindness and good cheer.
10111-8{_}2248{_}On Christmas day a great family gathering took place, and one and all agreed that little Rawdon was a fine boy.
10111-8{_}2249{_}They respected a possible Baronet in the boy between whom and the title there was only the little sickly, pale Pitt Blinkie.
10111-8{_}2250{_}The children were very good friends.
10111-8{_}2251{_}Pitt Blinkie was too little a dog for such a big dog as Rawdon to play with, and Matilda, being only a girl, of course not fit companion for a young gentleman who was near eight years old, and going into jackets very soon.
10111-8{_}2252{_}He took the command of this small party at once, the little girl and the little boy following him about with great reverence at such times as he condescended to sport with them.
10111-8{_}2253{_}His happiness and pleasure in the country were extreme.
10111-8{_}2254{_}The kitchen-garden pleased him hugely, the flowers moderately; but the pigeons and the poultry, and the stables, when he was allowed to visit them, were delightful objects to him.
10111-8{_}2255{_}He resisted being kissed by the Misses Crawley; but he allowed Lady Jane sometimes to embrace him, and it was by her side that he liked to sit rather than by his mother.
