10111-8{_}2789{_}When his wife died, Miss Hobson, who since her father's death had become a partner in the firm, met Mr. Newcome with his little boy as she was coming out of meeting one Sunday, and the child looked so pretty, and Mr. Newcome so personable, that Miss Hobson invited him and little Tommy into the grounds; let the child frisk about in the hay on the lawn, and at the end of the visit gave him a large piece of pound-cake, a quantity of the finest hot-house grapes, and a tract in one syllable.
10111-8{_}2790{_}Tommy was ill the next day; but on the next Sunday his father was at meeting, and not very long after that Miss Hobson became Mrs. Newcome.
10111-8{_}2791{_}After his father's second marriage, Tommy and Sarah, his nurse, who was also a cousin of Mr. Newcome's first wife, were transported from the cottage, where they had lived in great comfort, to the palace hard by, surrounded by lawns and gardens, graperies, aviaries, luxuries of all kinds.
10111-8{_}2792{_}This paradise was separated from the outer world by a, thick hedge of tall trees and an ivy-covered porter's gate, through which they who travelled to London on the top of the Clapham coach could only get a glimpse of the bliss within.
10111-8{_}2793{_}It was a serious paradise.
10111-8{_}2794{_}As you entered at the gate, gravity fell on you; and decorum wrapped you in a garment of starch.
10111-8{_}2795{_}The butcher boy who galloped his horse and cart madly about the adjoining lanes, on passing that lodge fell into an undertaker's pace, and delivered his joints and sweetbreads silently at the servant's entrance.
10111-8{_}2796{_}The rooks in the elms cawed sermons at morning and evening; the peacocks walked demurely on the terraces; the guinea fowls looked more Quaker-like than those birds usually do.
10111-8{_}2797{_}The lodge-keeper was serious, and a clerk at the neighbouring chapel.
10111-8{_}2798{_}The pastor, who entered at that gate and greeted his comely wife and children, fed the little lambkins with tracts.
10111-8{_}2799{_}The head gardener was a Scotch Calvinist, after the strictest order.
10111-8{_}2800{_}On a Sunday the household marched away to sit under his or her favourite minister, the only man who went to church being Thomas Newcome, with Tommy, his little son.
10111-8{_}2801{_}Tommy was taught hymns suited to his tender age, pointing out the inevitable fate of wicked children and giving him a description of the punishment of little sinners, which poems he repeated to his step-mother after dinner, before a great shining mahogany table, covered with grapes, pineapples, plum cake, port wine, and madeira, and surrounded by stout men in black, with baggy white neckcloths, who took the little man between their knees and questioned him as to his right understanding of the place whither naughty boys were bound.
10111-8{_}2802{_}They patted his head if he said well, or rebuked him if he was bold, as he often was.
10111-8{_}2803{_}Then came the birth of Mrs. Newcome's twin boys, Hobson and Bryan, and now there was no reason why young Newcome, their step-brother, should not go to school, and to Grey Friars Thomas Newcome was accordingly sent, exchanging--O ye gods!
10111-8{_}2804{_}with what delight--the splendour of Clapham for the rough, plentiful fare of the new place.
10111-8{_}2805{_}The pleasures of school-life were such to him that he did not care to go home for a holiday; for by playing tricks and breaking windows, by taking the gardener's peaches and the housekeeper's jam, by upsetting his two little brothers in a go-cart (of which injury the Baronet's nose bore marks to his dying day), by going to sleep during the sermons, and treating reverend gentlemen with levity, he drew down on himself the merited anger of his step-mother; and many punishments.
10111-8{_}2806{_}To please Mrs. Newcome, his father whipped Tommy for upsetting his little brothers in the go-cart; but, upon being pressed to repeat the whipping for some other prank, Mr. Newcome refused, saying that the boy got flogging enough at school, with which opinion Master Tommy fully agreed.
10111-8{_}2807{_}His step-mother, however, determined to make the young culprit smart for his offences, and one day, when Mr. Newcome was absent, and Tommy refractory as usual, summoned the butler and footman to flog the young criminal.
10111-8{_}2808{_}But he dashed so furiously against the butler's shins as to cause that menial to limp and suffer for many days after; and, seizing the decanter, he threatened to discharge it at Mrs. Newcome's head before he would submit to the punishment she desired administered.
10111-8{_}2809{_}When Mr. Newcome returned, he was indignant at his wife's treatment of Tommy, and said so, to her great displeasure.
10111-8{_}2810{_}This affair, indeed, almost caused a break in their relations, and friends and clergy were obliged to interfere to allay the domestic quarrel.
10111-8{_}2811{_}At length Mrs. Newcome, who was not unkind, and could be brought to own that she was sometimes in fault, was induced to submit to the decrees of her husband, whom she had vowed to love and honour.
10111-8{_}2812{_}When Tommy fell ill of scarlet fever she nursed him through his illness, and uttered no reproach to her husband when the twins took the disease.
10111-8{_}2813{_}And even though Tommy in his delirium vowed that he would put on his clothes and run away to his old nurse Sarah, Mrs. Newcome's kindness to him never faltered.
10111-8{_}2814{_}What the boy threatened in his delirium, a year later he actually achieved.
10111-8{_}2815{_}He ran away from home, and appeared one morning, gaunt and hungry, at Sarah's cottage two hundred miles away from Clapham.
10111-8{_}2816{_}She housed the poor prodigal with many tears and kisses, and put him to bed and to sleep; from which slumber he was aroused by the appearance of his father, whose instinct, backed by Mrs. Newcome's intelligence, had made him at once aware whither the young runaway had fled.
10111-8{_}2817{_}Seeing a horsewhip in his parent's hand, Tommy, scared out of a sweet sleep and a delightful dream of cricket, knew his fate; and getting out of bed, received his punishment without a word.
10111-8{_}2818{_}Very likely the father suffered more than the child; for, when the punishment was over, the little man yet quivering with the pain, held out his little bleeding hand, and said, "I can--I can take it from you, sir," saying which his face flushed, and his eyes filled, whereupon the father burst into a passion of tears, and embraced the boy, and kissed him, besought him to be rebellious no more, flung the whip away from him, and swore, come what would, he would never strike him again.
10111-8{_}2819{_}The quarrel was the means of a great and happy reconciliation.
10111-8{_}2820{_}But the truce was only a temporary one.
10111-8{_}2821{_}War very soon broke out again between the impetuous lad and his rigid, domineering step-mother.
10111-8{_}2822{_}It was not that he was very bad, nor she so very stern, but the two could not agree.
10111-8{_}2823{_}The boy sulked and was miserable at home, and, after a number of more serious escapades than he had before indulged in, he was sent to a tutor for military instruction, where he was prepared for the army and received a fairly good professional education.
10111-8{_}2824{_}He cultivated mathematics and fortification, and made rapid progress in his study of the French language.
10111-8{_}2825{_}But again did our poor Tommy get into trouble, and serious trouble indeed this time, for it involved his French master's pretty young daughter as well as himself.
10111-8{_}2826{_}Frantic with wrath and despair at the unfortunate climax of events, young Newcome embarked for India, and quitted the parents whom he was never more to see.
10111-8{_}2827{_}His name was no more mentioned at Clapham, but he wrote constantly to his father, who sent Tom liberal private remittances to India, and was in turn made acquainted with the fact of his son's marriage, and later received news of the birth of his grandson, Clive.
10111-8{_}2828{_}Old Thomas Newcome would have liked to leave all his private fortune to his son Thomas, for the twins were only too well provided for, but he dared not, for fear of his wife, and he died, and poor Tom was only secretly forgiven.
10111-8{_}2829{_}So much for the history of Clive Newcome's father and grandfather.
10111-8{_}2830{_}Having related it in full detail, we can now proceed to the narrative of Clive's life, he being the hero of this tale.
10111-8{_}2831{_}From the day of his birth until he was some seven years old, Clive's English relatives knew nothing about him.
10111-8{_}2832{_}Then, Colonel Newcome's wife having died, and having kept the boy with him as long as the climate would allow, Thomas Newcome, now Lieutenant-Colonel, decided that it was wise to send Clive to England, to entrust him to the boy's maternal aunt, Miss Honeyman, who was living at Brighton, that Clive might have the superior advantages of school days in England.
10111-8{_}2833{_}Let us glance at a few extracts from letters received by Colonel Newcome after his boy had reached England.
10111-8{_}2834{_}The aunt to whose care he was entrusted wrote as follows:         *       *       *       *       *  With the most heartfelt joy, my dear Major, I take up my pen to announce to you the happy arrival of the Ramchunder and the dearest and handsomest little boy who, I am sure, ever came from India.
10111-8{_}2835{_}Little Clive is in perfect health.
10111-8{_}2836{_}He speaks English wonderfully well.
10111-8{_}2837{_}He cried when he parted from Mr. Sneid, the supercargo, who most kindly brought him from Southhampton in a postchaise, but these tears in childhood are of very brief duration!...
10111-8{_}2838{_}You may be sure that the most liberal sum which you have placed to my credit with the Messrs. Hobson & Co. shall be faithfully expended on my dear little charge.
10111-8{_}2839{_}Of course, unless Mrs. Newcome,--who can scarcely be called his grandmamma, I suppose,--writes to invite dear Clive to Clapham, I shall not think of sending him there.
10111-8{_}2840{_}My brother, who thanks you for your continuous bounty, will write next month, and report progress as to his dear pupil.
10111-8{_}2841{_}Clive will add a postscript of his own, and I am, my dear Major,  Your grateful and affectionate,  MARTHA HONEYMAN.
10111-8{_}2842{_}*       *       *       *       *  In a round hand and on lines ruled with pencil:         *       *       *       *       *  _Dearest Papa_ I am very well I hope you are Very Well.
10111-8{_}2843{_}Mr. Sneed brought me in a postchaise I like Mr. Sneed very much.
10111-8{_}2844{_}I like Aunt Martha I like Hannah.
10111-8{_}2845{_}There are no ships here I am your affectionate son CLIVE NEWCOME.
10111-8{_}2846{_}*       *       *       *       *  There was also a note from Colonel Newcome's stepbrother, Bryan, as follows:         *       *       *       *       *  _My Dear Thomas_: Mr. Sneid, supercargo of the Ramchunder, East Indiaman, handed over to us yesterday your letter, and, to-day, I have purchased three thousand three hundred and twenty-three pounds 6 and 8, three per cent Consols, in our joint names (H. and B. Newcome), held for your little boy.
10111-8{_}2847{_}Mr. S. gives a favourable account of the little man, and left him in perfect health two days since, at the house of his aunt, Miss Honeyman.
10111-8{_}2848{_}We have placed £200 to that lady's credit, at your desire.
10111-8{_}2849{_}I dare say my mother will ask your little boy to the Hermitage; and when we have a house of our own I am sure Ann and I shall be very happy to see him.
10111-8{_}2850{_}Yours affectionately,  B. NEWCOME.
10111-8{_}2851{_}*       *       *       *       *  And another from Miss Honeyman's brother, containing the following:         *       *       *       *       *  MAJOR NEWCOME:  _My Dear Colonel_: ... Clive is everything that a father's and uncle's, a pastor's, a teacher's, affections could desire.
10111-8{_}2852{_}He is not a premature genius; he is not, I frankly own, more advanced in his classical and mathematical studies than some children even younger than himself; but he has acquired the rudiments of health; he has laid in a store of honesty and good-humour which are not less likely to advance him in life than mere science and language ...
10111-8{_}2853{_}etc., etc.,  Your affectionate brother-in-law,  CHARLES HONEYMAN.
10111-8{_}2854{_}*       *       *       *       *  Another letter from Miss Honeyman herself said:         *       *       *       *       *  _My Dear Colonel_: ... As my dearest little Clive was too small for a great school, I thought he could not do better than stay with his old aunt and have his uncle Charles for a tutor, who is one of the finest scholars in the world.
10111-8{_}2855{_}Of late he has been too weak to take a curacy, so I thought he could not do better than become Clive's tutor, and agreed to pay him out of your handsome donation of £250 for Clive, a sum of one hundred pounds per year.
10111-8{_}2856{_}But I find that Charles is too kind to be a schoolmaster, and Master Clive laughs at him.
10111-8{_}2857{_}It was only the other day after his return from his grandmamma's that I found a picture of Mrs. Newcome and Charles, too, and of both their spectacles, quite like.
10111-8{_}2858{_}He has done me and Hannah, too.
10111-8{_}2859{_}Mr. Speck, the artist, says he is a wonder at drawing.
10111-8{_}2860{_}Our little Clive has been to London on a visit to his uncles and to Clapham, to pay his duty to his step-grandmother, the wealthy Mrs. Newcome.
10111-8{_}2861{_}She was very gracious to him, and presented him with a five pound note, a copy of Kirk White's poems and a work called Little Henry and his Bearer, relating to India, and the excellent catechism of our Church.
10111-8{_}2862{_}Clive is full of humour, and I enclose you a rude scrap representing the Bishopess of Clapham, as Mrs. Newcome is called.
10111-8{_}2863{_}Instead then of allowing Clive to be with Charles in London next month I shall send him to Doctor Timpany's school, Marine Parade, of which I hear the best account; but I hope you will think of soon sending him to a great school.
10111-8{_}2864{_}My father always said it was the best place for boys, and I have a brother to whom my poor mother spared the rod, and who I fear has turned out but a spoiled child.
10111-8{_}2865{_}I am, dear Colonel, your most faithful servant,  MARTHA HONEYMAN.
10111-8{_}2866{_}*       *       *       *       *  Besides the news gleaned from these letters we gather the main facts concerning little Clive's departure from the Colonel's side.
10111-8{_}2867{_}He had kept the child with him until he felt sure that the change would be of advantage to the pretty boy, then had parted from him with bitter pangs of heart, and thought constantly of him with longing and affection.
10111-8{_}2868{_}With the boy, it was different.
10111-8{_}2869{_}Half an hour after his father had left him and in grief and loneliness was rowing back to shore, Clive was at play with a dozen other children on the sunny deck of the ship.
10111-8{_}2870{_}When two bells rang for their dinner, they were all hurrying to the table, busy over their meal, and forgetful of all but present happiness.
10111-8{_}2871{_}But with that fidelity which was an instinct of his nature, Colonel Newcome thought ever of his absent child and longed after him.
10111-8{_}2872{_}He never forsook the native servants who had had charge of Clive, but endowed them with money sufficient to make all their future lives comfortable.
10111-8{_}2873{_}No friends went to Europe, nor ship departed, but Newcome sent presents to the boy and costly tokens of his love and thanks to all who were kind to his son.
10111-8{_}2874{_}His aim was to save money for the youngster, but he was of a nature so generous that he spent five rupees where another would save them.
10111-8{_}2875{_}However, he managed to lay by considerable out of his liberal allowances, and to find himself and Clive growing richer every year.
10111-8{_}2876{_}"When Clive has had five or six years at school"--that was his scheme--"he will be a fine scholar, and have at least as much classical learning as a gentleman in the world need possess.
10111-8{_}2877{_}Then I will go to England, and we will pass three or four years together, in which he will learn to be intimate with me, and, I hope, to like me.
10111-8{_}2878{_}I shall be his pupil for Latin and Greek, and try and make up for lost time.
10111-8{_}2879{_}I know there is nothing like a knowledge of the classics to give a man good breeding.
10111-8{_}2880{_}I shall be able to help him with my knowledge of the world, and to keep him out of the way of sharpers and a pack of rogues who commonly infest young men.
10111-8{_}2881{_}And we will travel together, first through England, Scotland, and Ireland, for every man should know his own country, and then we will make the grand tour.
10111-8{_}2882{_}Then by the time he is eighteen he will be able to choose his profession.
10111-8{_}2883{_}He can go into the army, or, if he prefers, the church, or the law--they are open to him; and when he goes to the university, by which time I shall be, in all probability, a major-general, I can come back to India for a few years, and return by the time he has a wife and a home for his old father; or, if I die, I shall have done the best for him, and my boy will be left with the best education, a tolerable small fortune, and the blessing of his old father."
10111-8{_}2884{_}Such were the plans of the kind schemer.
10111-8{_}2885{_}How fondly he dwelt on them, how affectionately he wrote of them to his boy!
10111-8{_}2886{_}How he read books of travels and looked over the maps of Europe!
10111-8{_}2887{_}and said, "Rome, sir, glorious Rome; it won't be very long, major, before my boy and I see the Colosseum, and kiss the Pope's toe.
10111-8{_}2888{_}We shall go up the Rhine to Switzerland, and over the Simplon, the work of the great Napoleon.
10111-8{_}2889{_}By jove, sir, think of the Turks before Vienna, and Sobieski clearing eighty thousand of 'em off the face of the earth!
10111-8{_}2890{_}How my boy will rejoice in the picture galleries there, and in Prince Eugene's prints!
10111-8{_}2891{_}The boy's talent for drawing is wonderful, sir, wonderful.
10111-8{_}2892{_}He sent me a picture of our old school.
10111-8{_}2893{_}The very actual thing, sir; the cloisters, the school, the head gown boy going in with the rods, and the doctor himself.
10111-8{_}2894{_}It would make you die of laughing!"
10111-8{_}2895{_}He regaled the ladies of the regiment with dive's letters, and those of Miss Honeyman, which contained an account of the boy.
10111-8{_}2896{_}He even bored some of his hearers with this prattle; and sporting young men would give or take odds that the Colonel would mention Clive's name, once before five minutes, three times in ten minutes, twenty-five times in the course of dinner, and so on.
10111-8{_}2897{_}But they who laughed at the Colonel laughed very kindly; and everybody who knew him, loved him; everybody that is, who loved modesty, generosity and honour.
10111-8{_}2898{_}As to Clive himself, by this time he was thoroughly enjoying his new life in England.
10111-8{_}2899{_}After remaining for a time at Doctor Timpany's school, where he was first placed by his aunt, Miss Honeyman, he was speedily removed to that classical institution in which Colonel Newcome had been a student in earlier days.
10111-8{_}2900{_}My acquaintance with young Clive was at this school, Grey Friars, where our acquaintance was brief and casual.
10111-8{_}2901{_}He had the advantage of being six years my junior, and such a difference of age between lads at a public school puts intimacy out of the question, even though we knew each other at home, as our school phrase was, and our families were somewhat acquainted.
10111-8{_}2902{_}When Newcome's uncle, the Reverend Charles Honeyman, brought Newcome to the Grey Friars School, he recommended him to my superintendence and protection, and told me that his young nephew's father, Colonel Thomas Newcome, C.B., was a most gallant and distinguished officer in the Bengal establishment of the honourable East India Company; and that his uncles, the Colonel's half-brothers, were the eminent bankers, heads of the firm of Hobson Brothers & Newcome, Hobson Newcome, Esquire, Brianstone Square, and Marblehead, Sussex, and Sir Brian Newcome, of Newcome, and Park Lane, "whom to name," says Mr. Honeyman, with the fluent eloquence with which he decorated the commonest circumstances of life, "is to designate two of the merchant princes of the wealthiest city the world has ever known; and one, if not two, of the leaders of that aristocracy which rallies round the throne of the most elegant and refined of European sovereigns."
10111-8{_}2903{_}I promised Mr. Honeyman to do what I could for the boy; and he proceeded to take leave of his little nephew in my presence in terms equally eloquent, pulling out a long and very slender green purse, from which he extracted the sum of two and sixpence, which he presented to the child, who received the money with rather a queer twinkle in his blue eyes.
10111-8{_}2904{_}After that day's school I met my little protege in the neighbourhood of the pastry cook's, regaling himself with raspberry tarts.
10111-8{_}2905{_}"You must not spend all the money, sir, which your uncle gave you," said I, "in tarts and ginger-beer."
10111-8{_}2906{_}The urchin rubbed the raspberry jam off his mouth, and said, "It don't matter, sir, for I've got lots more."
10111-8{_}2907{_}"How much?"
10111-8{_}2908{_}says the Grand Inquisitor: for the formula of interrogation used to be, when a new boy came to the school, "What's your name?
10111-8{_}2909{_}Who's your father?
10111-8{_}2910{_}and how much money have you got?"
10111-8{_}2911{_}The little fellow pulled such a handful of sovereigns out of his pocket as might have made the tallest scholar feel a pang of envy.
10111-8{_}2912{_}"Uncle Hobson," says he, "gave me two; Aunt Hobson gave me one--no, Aunt Hobson gave me thirty shillings; Uncle Newcome gave me three pound; and Aunt Ann gave me one pound five; and Aunt Honeyman sent me ten shillings in a letter.
10111-8{_}2913{_}And Ethel wanted to give me a pound, only I wouldn't have it, you know; because Ethel's younger than me, and I have plenty."
10111-8{_}2914{_}"And who is Ethel?"
10111-8{_}2915{_}I ask, smiling at the artless youth's confessions.
10111-8{_}2916{_}"Ethel is my cousin," replied little Newcome; "Aunt Ann's daughter.
10111-8{_}2917{_}There's Ethel and Alice, and Aunt Ann wanted the baby to be called Boadicea, only uncle wouldn't; and there's Barnes and Egbert and little Alfred, only he don't count; he's quite a baby, you know.
10111-8{_}2918{_}Egbert and me was at school at Timpany's; he's going to Eton next half.
10111-8{_}2919{_}He's older than me, but I can lick him."
10111-8{_}2920{_}"And how old is Egbert?"
10111-8{_}2921{_}asks the smiling senior.
10111-8{_}2922{_}"Egbert's ten, and I'm nine, and Ethel's seven," replied the little chubby-faced hero, digging his hands deep into his trousers, and jingling all the sovereigns there.
10111-8{_}2923{_}I advised him to let me be his banker; and, keeping one out of his many gold pieces, he handed over the others, on which he drew with great liberality till his whole stock was expended.
10111-8{_}2924{_}The school hours of the upper and under boys were different at that time; the little fellows coming out of their hall half an hour before the Fifth and Sixth Forms; and many a time I used to find my little blue-jacket in waiting, with his honest square face, and white hair, and bright blue eyes, and I knew that he was come to draw on his bank.
10111-8{_}2925{_}Ere long one of the pretty blue eyes was shut up, and a fine black one substituted in its place.
10111-8{_}2926{_}He had been engaged, it appeared, in a pugilistic encounter with a giant of his own form whom he had worsted in the combat.
10111-8{_}2927{_}"Didn't I pitch into him, that's all?"
10111-8{_}2928{_}says he in the elation of victory; and, when I asked whence the quarrel arose, he stoutly informed me that "Wolf Minor, his opponent, had been bullying a little boy, and that he, the gigantic Newcome, wouldn't stand it."
10111-8{_}2929{_}So, being called away from the school, I said "Farewell and God bless you," to the brave little man, who remained a while at the Grey Friars, where his career and troubles had only just begun, and lost sight of him for several years.
10111-8{_}2930{_}Nor did we meet again until I was myself a young man occupying chambers in the Temple.
10111-8{_}2931{_}Meanwhile the years of Clive's absence had slowly worn away for Colonel Newcome, and at last the happy time came which he had been longing more passionately than any prisoner for liberty, or schoolboy for holiday.
10111-8{_}2932{_}The Colonel had taken leave of his regiment.
10111-8{_}2933{_}He had travelled to Calcutta; and the Commander-in-Chief announced that in giving to Lieutenant-Colonel Thomas Newcome, of the Bengal Cavalry, leave for the first time, after no less than thirty-four years' absence from home, he could not refrain from expressing his sense of the great services of this most distinguished officer, who had left his regiment in a state of the highest discipline and efficiency.
10111-8{_}2934{_}This kind Colonel had also to take leave of a score, at least, of adopted children to whom he chose to stand in the light of a father.
10111-8{_}2935{_}He was forever whirling away in post-chaises to this school and that, to see Jack Brown's boys, of the Cavalry; or Mrs. Smith's girls, of the Civil Service; or poor Tom Hick's orphan, who had nobody to look after him now that the cholera had carried off Tom and his wife, too.
10111-8{_}2936{_}On board the ship in which he returned from Calcutta were a dozen of little children, some of whom he actually escorted to their friends before he visited his own, though his heart was longing for his boy at Grey Friars.
10111-8{_}2937{_}The children at the schools seen, and largely rewarded out of his bounty (his loose white trousers had great pockets, always heavy with gold and silver, which he jingled when he was not pulling his moustaches, and to see the way in which he tipped children made one almost long to be a boy again) and when he had visited Miss Pinkerton's establishment, or Doctor Ramshorn's adjoining academy at Chiswick, and seen little Tom Davis or little Fanny Holmes, the honest fellow would come home and write off straightway a long letter to Tom's or Fanny's parents, far away in the country, whose hearts he made happy by his accounts of their children, as he had delighted the children themselves by his affection and bounty.
10111-8{_}2938{_}All the apple and orange-women (especially such as had babies as well as lollipops at their stalls), all the street-sweepers on the road between Nerot's and the Oriental, knew him, and were his pensioners.
10111-8{_}2939{_}His brothers in Threadneedle Street cast up their eyes at the cheques which he drew.
10111-8{_}2940{_}The Colonel had written to his brothers from Portsmouth, announcing his arrival, and three words to Clive, conveying the same intelligence.
10111-8{_}2941{_}The letter was served to the boy along with one bowl of tea and one buttered roll, of eighty such which were distributed to fourscore other boys, boarders of the same house with our young friend.
10111-8{_}2942{_}How the lad's face must have flushed and his eyes brightened when he read the news!
10111-8{_}2943{_}When the master of the house, the Reverend Mister Popkinson, came into the lodging-room, with a good-natured face, and said, "Newcome, you're wanted," he knew who had come.
10111-8{_}2944{_}He did not heed that notorious bruiser, old Hodge, who roared out, "Confound you, Newcome: I'll give it you for upsetting your tea over my new trousers."
10111-8{_}2945{_}He ran to the room where the stranger was waiting for him.
10111-8{_}2946{_}We will shut the door, if you please, upon that scene.
10111-8{_}2947{_}If Clive had not been as fine and handsome a young lad as any in that school or country, no doubt his fond father would have been just as well pleased and endowed him with a hundred fanciful graces; but, in truth, in looks and manners he was everything which his parent could desire.
10111-8{_}2948{_}He was the picture of health, strength, activity, and good-humour.
10111-8{_}2949{_}He had a good forehead shaded with a quantity of waving light hair; a complexion which ladies might envy; a mouth which seemed accustomed to laughing; and a pair of blue eyes that sparkled with intelligence and frank kindness.
10111-8{_}2950{_}No wonder the pleased father could not refrain from looking at him.
10111-8{_}2951{_}The bell rang for second school, and Mr. Popkinson, arrayed in cap and gown, came in to shake Colonel Newcome by the hand, and to say he supposes it was to be a holiday for Newcome that day.
10111-8{_}2952{_}He said not a word about Clive's scrape of the day before, and that awful row in the bedrooms, where the lad and three others were discovered making a supper off a pork pie and two bottles of prime old port from the Red Cow public-house in Grey Friars Lane.
10111-8{_}2953{_}When the bell was done ringing, and all these busy little bees swarmed into their hive, there was a solitude in the place.
10111-8{_}2954{_}The Colonel and his son walked the play-ground together, that gravelly flat, as destitute of herbage as the Arabian desert, but, nevertheless, in the language of the place, called the green.
10111-8{_}2955{_}They walked the green, and they paced the cloisters, and Clive showed his father his own name of Thomas Newcome carved upon one of the arches forty years ago.
10111-8{_}2956{_}As they talked, the boy gave sidelong glances at his new friend, and wondered at the Colonel's loose trousers, long moustaches, and yellow face.
10111-8{_}2957{_}He looked very odd, Clive thought, very odd and very kind, and like a gentleman, every inch of him:--not like Martin's father, who came to see his son lately in highlows, and a shocking bad hat, and actually flung coppers amongst the boys for a scramble.
10111-8{_}2958{_}He burst out a-laughing at the exquisitely ludicrous idea of a gentleman of his fashion scrambling for coppers.
10111-8{_}2959{_}And now enjoining the boy to be ready against his return, the Colonel whirled away in his cab to the city to shake hands with his brothers, whom he had not seen since they were demure little men in blue jackets under charge of a serious tutor.
10111-8{_}2960{_}He rushed into the banking house, broke into the parlour where the lords of the establishment were seated, and astonished these trim, quiet gentlemen by the warmth of his greeting, by the vigour of his handshake, and the loud tones of his voice, which might actually be heard by the busy clerks in the hall without.
10111-8{_}2961{_}He knew Bryan from Hobson at once--that unlucky little accident in the go-cart having left its mark forever on the nose of Sir Bryan Newcome.
10111-8{_}2962{_}He had a bald head and light hair, a short whisker cut to his cheek, a buff waistcoat, very neat boots and hands, and was altogether dignified, bland, smiling, and statesmanlike.
10111-8{_}2963{_}Hobson Newcome, Esquire, was more portly than his elder brother, and allowed his red whiskers to grow on his cheeks and under his chin.
10111-8{_}2964{_}He wore thick shoes with nails in them, and affected the country gentleman in his appearance.
10111-8{_}2965{_}His hat had a broad brim, and his ample pockets always contained agricultural produce, samples of bean or corn, or a whiplash or balls for horses.
10111-8{_}2966{_}In fine, he was a good old country gentleman, and a better man of business than his more solemn brother, at whom he laughed in his jocular way; and said rightly that a gentleman must get up very early to get ahead of him.
10111-8{_}2967{_}These gentlemen each received the Colonel in a manner consistent with his peculiar nature.
10111-8{_}2968{_}Sir Bryan regretted that Lady Ann was away from London, being at Brighton with the children, who were all ill of the measles.
10111-8{_}2969{_}Hobson said, "Maria can't treat you to such good company as Lady Ann could give you; but when will you take a day and come and dine with us?
10111-8{_}2970{_}Let's see, to-day is Wednesday; to-morrow we are engaged.
10111-8{_}2971{_}Friday, we dine at Judge Budge's; Saturday I am going down to Marblehead to look after the hay.
10111-8{_}2972{_}Come on Monday, Tom, and I'll introduce you to the missus and the young uns."
10111-8{_}2973{_}"I will bring Clive," says Colonel Newcome, rather disturbed at this reception.
10111-8{_}2974{_}"After his illness my sister-in-law was very kind to him."
10111-8{_}2975{_}"No, hang it, don't bring boys; there's no good in boys; they stop the talk downstairs, and the ladies don't want 'em in the drawing-room.
10111-8{_}2976{_}Send him to dine with the children on Sunday, if you like, and come along down with me to Marblehead, and I'll show you such a crop of hay as will make your eyes open.
10111-8{_}2977{_}Are you fond of farming?"
10111-8{_}2978{_}"I have not seen my boy for years," says the Colonel; "I had rather pass Saturday and Sunday with him, if you please, and some day we will go to Marblehead together."
10111-8{_}2979{_}"Well, an offer's an offer.
10111-8{_}2980{_}I don't know any pleasanter thing than getting out of this confounded city and smelling the hedges, and looking at the crops coming up, and passing the Sunday in quiet."
10111-8{_}2981{_}And his own tastes being thus agricultural, the worthy gentleman thought that everybody else must delight in the same recreation.
10111-8{_}2982{_}"In the winter, I hope, we shall see you at Newcome," says the elder brother, blandly smiling.
10111-8{_}2983{_}"I can't give you any tiger-shooting, but I'll promise you that you shall find plenty of pheasants in our jungle," and he laughed very gently at this mild sally.
10111-8{_}2984{_}At this moment a fair-haired young gentleman, languid and pale, and dressed in the height of fashion, made his appearance and was introduced as the Baronet's oldest son, Barnes Newcome.
10111-8{_}2985{_}He returned Colonel Newcome's greeting with a smile, saying, "Very happy to see you, I am sure.
10111-8{_}2986{_}You find London very much changed since you were here?
10111-8{_}2987{_}Very good time to come, the very full of the season."
10111-8{_}2988{_}Poor Thomas Newcome was quite abashed by his strange reception.
10111-8{_}2989{_}Here was a man, hungry for affection, and one relation asked him to dinner next Monday, and another invited him to shoot pheasants at Christmas.
10111-8{_}2990{_}Here was a beardless young sprig, who patronised him and asked him whether he found London was changed.
10111-8{_}2991{_}As soon as possible he ended the interview with his step-brothers, and drove back to Ludgate Hill, where he dismissed his cab and walked across the muddy pavements of Smithfield, on his way back to the old school where his son was, a way which he had trodden many a time in his own early days.
10111-8{_}2992{_}There was Cistercian Street, and the Red Cow of his youth; there was the quaint old Grey Friars Square, with its blackened trees and garden, surrounded by ancient houses of the build of the last century, now slumbering like pensioners in the sunshine.
10111-8{_}2993{_}Under the great archway of the hospital he could look at the old Gothic building; and a black-gowned pensioner or two crawling over the quiet square, or passing from one dark arch to another.
10111-8{_}2994{_}The boarding-houses of the school were situated in the square, hard by the more ancient buildings of the hospital.
10111-8{_}2995{_}A great noise of shouting, crying, clapping forms and cupboards, treble voices, bass voices, poured out of the schoolboys' windows; their life, bustle, and gaiety contrasted strangely with the quiet of those old men, creeping along in their black gowns under the ancient arches yonder, whose struggle of life was over, whose hope and noise and bustle had sunk into that grey calm.
10111-8{_}2996{_}There was Thomas Newcome arrived at the middle of life, standing between the shouting boys and the tottering seniors and in a situation to moralise upon both, had not his son Clive, who espied him, come jumping down the steps to greet his sire.
10111-8{_}2997{_}Clive was dressed in his very best; not one of those four hundred young gentlemen had a better figure, a better tailor, or a neater boot.
10111-8{_}2998{_}Schoolfellows, grinning through the bars, envied him as he walked away; senior boys made remarks on Colonel Newcome's loose clothes and long moustaches, his brown hands and unbrushed hat.
10111-8{_}2999{_}The Colonel was smoking a cheroot as he walked; and the gigantic Smith, the cock of the school, who happened to be looking majestically out of the window, was pleased to say that he thought Newcome's governor was a fine manly-looking fellow.
10111-8{_}3000{_}"Tell me about your uncles, Clive," said the Colonel, as they walked on arm in arm.
10111-8{_}3001{_}"What about them, sir?"
10111-8{_}3002{_}asks the boy.
10111-8{_}3003{_}"I don't think I know much."
10111-8{_}3004{_}"You have been to stay with them.
10111-8{_}3005{_}You wrote about them.
10111-8{_}3006{_}Were they kind to you?"
10111-8{_}3007{_}"Oh, yes, I suppose they are very kind.
10111-8{_}3008{_}They always tipped me: only you know when I go there I scarcely ever see them.
10111-8{_}3009{_}Mr. Newcome asks me the oftenest--two or three times a quarter when he's in town, and gives me a sovereign regular."
10111-8{_}3010{_}"Well, he must see you to give you the sovereign," says Clive's father, laughing.
10111-8{_}3011{_}The boy blushed rather.
10111-8{_}3012{_}"Yes.
10111-8{_}3013{_}When it's time to go back to Smithfield on a Saturday night, I go into the dining-room to shake hands, and he gives it to me; but he don't speak to me much, you know, and I don't care about going to Bryanstone Square, except for the tip (of course that's important), because I am made to dine with the children, and they are quite little ones; and a great cross French governess, who is always crying and shrieking after them, and finding fault with them.
10111-8{_}3014{_}My uncle generally has his dinner parties on Saturday, or goes out; and aunt gives me ten shillings and sends me to the play; that's better fun than a dinner party."
10111-8{_}3015{_}Here the lad blushed again.
10111-8{_}3016{_}"I used," said he, "when I was younger, to stand on the stairs and prig things out of the dishes when they came out from dinner, but I'm past that now.
10111-8{_}3017{_}Maria (that's my cousin) used to take the sweet things and give 'em to the governess.
10111-8{_}3018{_}Fancy!
10111-8{_}3019{_}she used to put lumps of sugar into her pocket and eat them in the schoolroom!
10111-8{_}3020{_}Uncle Hobson don't live in such good society as Uncle Newcome.
10111-8{_}3021{_}You see, Aunt Hobson, she's very kind, you know, and all that, but I don't think she's what you call _comme il faut_"  "Why, how are you to judge?"
10111-8{_}3022{_}asks the father, amused at the lad's candid prattle, "and where does the difference lie?"
10111-8{_}3023{_}"I can't tell you what it is, or how it is," the boy answered, "only one can't help seeing the difference.
10111-8{_}3024{_}It isn't rank and that: only somehow there are some men gentlemen and some not, and some women ladies and some not.
10111-8{_}3025{_}There's Jones now, the fifth-form master, every man sees he's a gentleman, though he wears ever so old clothes; and there's Mr. Brown, who oils his hair, and wears rings, and white chokers--my eyes!
10111-8{_}3026{_}such white chokers!--and yet we call him the handsome snob!
10111-8{_}3027{_}And so about Aunt Maria, she's very handsome and she's very finely dressed, only somehow she's not the ticket, you see."
10111-8{_}3028{_}"Oh, she's not the ticket?"
10111-8{_}3029{_}says the Colonel, much amused.
10111-8{_}3030{_}"Well, what I mean is--but never mind," says the boy.
10111-8{_}3031{_}"I can't tell you what I mean.
10111-8{_}3032{_}I don't like to make fun of her, you know, for after all she's very kind to me; but Aunt Ann is different, and it seems as if what she says is more natural; and though she has funny ways of her own, too, yet somehow she looks grander,"--and here the lad laughed again.
10111-8{_}3033{_}"And do you know, I often think that as good a lady as Aunt Ann herself, is old Aunt Honeyman at Brighton--that is, in all essentials, you know?
10111-8{_}3034{_}And she is not a bit ashamed of letting lodgings, or being poor herself, as sometimes I think some of our family--"  "I thought we were going to speak no ill of them," says the Colonel, smiling.
10111-8{_}3035{_}"Well, it only slipped out unawares," says Clive, laughing, "but at Newcome when they go on about the Newcomes, and that great ass, Barnes Newcome, gives himself his airs, it makes me die of laughing.
10111-8{_}3036{_}That time I went down to Newcome I went to see old Aunt Sarah, and she told me everything, and do you know, I was a little hurt at first, for I thought we were swells till then?
10111-8{_}3037{_}And when I came back to school, where perhaps I had been giving myself airs, and bragging about Newcome, why, you know, I thought it was right to tell the fellows."
10111-8{_}3038{_}"That's a man," said the Colonel, with delight; though had he said, "That's a boy," he had spoken more correctly.
10111-8{_}3039{_}"That's a man," cried the Colonel; "never be ashamed of your father, Clive."
10111-8{_}3040{_}"_Ashamed of my father_!"
10111-8{_}3041{_}says Clive, looking up to him, and walking on as proud as a peacock.
10111-8{_}3042{_}"I say," the lad resumed, after a pause--  "Say what you say," said the father.
10111-8{_}3043{_}"Is that all true what's in the Peerage--in the Baronetage, about Uncle Newcome and Newcome; about the Newcome who was burned at Smithfield; about the one that was at the battle of Bosworth; and the old, old Newcome who was bar--that is, who was surgeon to Edward the Confessor, and was killed at Hastings?
10111-8{_}3044{_}I am afraid it isn't; and yet I should like it to be true."
10111-8{_}3045{_}"I think every man would like to come of an ancient and honourable race," said the Colonel in his honest way.
10111-8{_}3046{_}"As you like your father to be an honourable man, why not your grandfather, and his ancestors before him?
10111-8{_}3047{_}But if we can't inherit a good name, at least we can do our best to leave one, my boy; and that is an ambition which, please God., you and I will both hold by."
10111-8{_}3048{_}With this simple talk the old and young gentleman beguiled their way, until they came into the western quarter of the town, where Hobson Newcome lived in a handsome and roomy mansion.
10111-8{_}3049{_}Colonel Newcome was bent on paying a visit to his sister-in-law, although as they waited to be let in they could not but remark through the opened windows of the dining-room that a great table was laid and every preparation was made for a feast.
10111-8{_}3050{_}"My brother said he was engaged to dinner to-day," said the Colonel.
10111-8{_}3051{_}"Does Mrs. Newcome give parties when he is away?"
10111-8{_}3052{_}"She invites all the company," answered Clive.
10111-8{_}3053{_}"My uncle never asks any one without aunt's leave."
10111-8{_}3054{_}The Colonel's countenance fell.
10111-8{_}3055{_}"He has a great dinner, and does not ask his own brother!"
10111-8{_}3056{_}Newcome thought.
10111-8{_}3057{_}"Why, if he had come to India with all his family, he might have stayed for a year, and I should have been offended had he gone elsewhere."
10111-8{_}3058{_}A hot menial in a red waistcoat came and opened the door, and without waiting for preparatory queries said, "Not at home."
10111-8{_}3059{_}"It's my father, John," said Clive.
10111-8{_}3060{_}"My aunt will see Colonel Newcome."
10111-8{_}3061{_}"Missis is not at home," said the man.
10111-8{_}3062{_}"Missis is gone in carriage--Not at this door!--Take them things down the area steps, young man!"
10111-8{_}3063{_}This latter speech was addressed to a pastry cook's boy with a large sugar temple and many conical papers containing delicacies for dessert.
10111-8{_}3064{_}"Mind the hice is here in time; or there'll be a blow-up with your governor,"--and John struggled back, closing the door on the astonished Colonel.
10111-8{_}3065{_}"Upon my life, they actually shut the door in our faces," said the poor gentleman.
10111-8{_}3066{_}"The man is very busy, sir.
10111-8{_}3067{_}There's a great dinner.
10111-8{_}3068{_}I'm sure my aunt would not refuse you," Clive interposed.
10111-8{_}3069{_}"She is very kind.
10111-8{_}3070{_}I suppose it's different here from what it is in India.
10111-8{_}3071{_}There are the children in the Square,--those are the girls in blue,--that's the French governess, the one with the yellow parasol.
10111-8{_}3072{_}How d'ye do, Mary?
10111-8{_}3073{_}How d'ye do, Fanny?
10111-8{_}3074{_}This is my father,--this is your uncle."
10111-8{_}3075{_}The Colonel surveyed his little nieces with that kind expression which his face always wore when it was turned toward children.
10111-8{_}3076{_}"Have you heard of your uncle in India?"
10111-8{_}3077{_}he asked them.
10111-8{_}3078{_}"No," says Maria.
10111-8{_}3079{_}"Yes," says Fannie.
10111-8{_}3080{_}"You know mademoiselle said that if we were naughty we should be sent to our uncle in India.
10111-8{_}3081{_}I think I should like to go with you."
10111-8{_}3082{_}"Oh, you silly child!"
10111-8{_}3083{_}cries Maria.
10111-8{_}3084{_}"Yes, I should, if Clive went, too," says little Fanny.
10111-8{_}3085{_}"Behold madame, who arrives from her promenade!"
10111-8{_}3086{_}mademoiselle exclaimed, and, turning round, Colonel Newcome beheld, for the first time, his sister-in-law, a stout lady with fair hair and a fine bonnet and a pelisse, who was reclining in her barouche with the scarlet plush garments of her domestics blazing before and behind her.
10111-8{_}3087{_}Clive ran towards his aunt.
10111-8{_}3088{_}She bent over the carriage languidly towards him.
10111-8{_}3089{_}She liked him.
10111-8{_}3090{_}"What, you, Clive!"
10111-8{_}3091{_}she said, "How come you away from school of a Thursday, sir?"
10111-8{_}3092{_}"It is a holiday," said he.
10111-8{_}3093{_}"My father is come; and he is come to see you."
10111-8{_}3094{_}She bowed her head with an expression of affable surprise and majestic satisfaction.
10111-8{_}3095{_}"Indeed, Clive!"
10111-8{_}3096{_}she exclaimed, and the Colonel stepped forward and took off his hat and bowed and stood bareheaded.
10111-8{_}3097{_}She surveyed him blandly, and put forward a little hand, saying, "You have only arrived to-day, and you came to see me?
10111-8{_}3098{_}That was very kind.
10111-8{_}3099{_}Have you had a pleasant voyage?
10111-8{_}3100{_}These are two of my girls.
10111-8{_}3101{_}My boys are at school.
10111-8{_}3102{_}I shall be so glad to introduce them to their uncle.
10111-8{_}3103{_}_This_ naughty boy might never have seen you, but that we took him home after the scarlet fever, and made him well, didn't we Clive?
10111-8{_}3104{_}And we are all very fond of him, and you must not be jealous of his love for his aunt.
10111-8{_}3105{_}We feel that we quite know you through him, and we know that you know us, and we hope you will like us.
10111-8{_}3106{_}Do you think your papa will like us, Clive?
10111-8{_}3107{_}Or, perhaps you will like Lady Ann best?
10111-8{_}3108{_}Yes; you have been to her first, of course?
10111-8{_}3109{_}Not been?
10111-8{_}3110{_}Oh!
10111-8{_}3111{_}because she is not in town."
10111-8{_}3112{_}Leaning fondly on Clive's arm, mademoiselle standing with the children hard by, while John with his hat off stood at the opened door, Mrs. Newcome slowly uttered the above remarkable remarks to the Colonel, on the threshold of her house, which she never asked him to pass.
10111-8{_}3113{_}"If you will come in to us about ten this evening," she then said, "you will find some men not undistinguished, who honour me of an evening.
10111-8{_}3114{_}Perhaps they will be interesting to you, Colonel Newcome, as you are newly arriven in Europe.
10111-8{_}3115{_}A stranger coming to London could scarcely have a better opportunity of seeing some of our great illustrations of science and literature.
10111-8{_}3116{_}We have a few friends at dinner, and now I must go in and consult with my housekeeper.
10111-8{_}3117{_}Good-bye for the present.
10111-8{_}3118{_}Mind, not later than ten, as Mr. Newcome must be up betimes in the morning, and _our_ parties break up early.
10111-8{_}3119{_}When Clive is a little older I dare say we shall see him, too.
10111-8{_}3120{_}Goodbye!"
10111-8{_}3121{_}And again the Colonel was favoured with a shake of the hand, and the lady sailed up the stair, and passed in at the door, with not the faintest idea but that the hospitality which she was offering to her kinsman was of the most cordial and pleasant kind.
10111-8{_}3122{_}Having met Colonel Newcome on the steps of her house, she ordered him to come to her evening party; and though he had not been to an evening party for five and thirty years--though he had not been to bed the night before--he never once thought of disobeying Mrs. Newcome's order, but was actually at her door at five minutes past ten, having arrayed himself, to the wonderment of Clive, and left the boy to talk to Mr. Binnie, a friend and fellow-passenger, who had just arrived from Portsmouth, who had dined with him, and taken up his quarters at the same hotel.
10111-8{_}3123{_}Well, then, the Colonel is launched in English society of an intellectual order, and mighty dull he finds it.
10111-8{_}3124{_}During two hours of desultory conversation and rather meagre refreshments, the only bright spot is his meeting with Charles Honeyman, his dead wife's brother, whom he was mighty glad to see.
10111-8{_}3125{_}Except for this meeting there was little to entertain the Colonel, and as soon as possible he and Honeyman walked away together, the Colonel returning to his hotel, where he found his friend James Binnie installed in his room in the best arm-chair, sleeping-cosily, but he woke up briskly when the Colonel entered.
10111-8{_}3126{_}"It is you, you gadabout, is it?"
10111-8{_}3127{_}cried Binnie.
10111-8{_}3128{_}"See what it is to have a real friend now, Colonel!
10111-8{_}3129{_}I waited for you, because I knew you would want to talk about that scapegrace of yours."
10111-8{_}3130{_}"Isn't he a fine fellow, James?"
10111-8{_}3131{_}says the Colonel, lighting a cheroot as he sits on the table.
10111-8{_}3132{_}Was it joy, or the bedroom candle with which he lighted his cigar, which illuminated his honest features so, and made them so to shine?
10111-8{_}3133{_}"I have been occupied, sir, in taking the lad's moral measurement: and I have pumped him as successfully as ever I cross-examined a rogue in my court.
10111-8{_}3134{_}I place his qualities thus:--Love of approbation, sixteen.
10111-8{_}3135{_}Benevolence, fourteen.
10111-8{_}3136{_}Combativeness, fourteen.
10111-8{_}3137{_}Adhesiveness, two.
10111-8{_}3138{_}Amativeness is not yet of course fully developed, but I expect will be prodigiously strong.
10111-8{_}3139{_}The imaginative and reflective organs are very large; those of calculation weak.
10111-8{_}3140{_}He may make a poet or a painter, or you may make a sojor of him, though worse men than him's good enough for that--but a bad merchant, a lazy lawyer, and a miserable mathematician.
10111-8{_}3141{_}My opinion, Colonel, is that young scapegrace will give you a deal of trouble; or would, only you are so absurdly proud of him, and you think everything he does is perfection.
10111-8{_}3142{_}He'll spend your money for you; he'll do as little work as need be.
10111-8{_}3143{_}He'll get into scrapes with the sax.
10111-8{_}3144{_}He's almost as simple as his father, and that is to say that any rogue will cheat him; and he seems to me to have your obstinate habit of telling the truth, Colonel, which may prevent his getting on in the world; but on the other hand will keep him from going very wrong.
10111-8{_}3145{_}So that, though there is every fear for him, there's some hope and some consolation."
10111-8{_}3146{_}"What do you think of his Latin and Greek?"
10111-8{_}3147{_}asked the Colonel.
10111-8{_}3148{_}Before going out to his party Newcome had laid a deep scheme with Binnie, and it had been agreed that the latter should examine the young fellow in his humanities.
10111-8{_}3149{_}"Wall," cries the Scot, "I find that the lad knows as much about Greek and Latin as I knew myself when I was eighteen years of age."
10111-8{_}3150{_}"My dear Binnie, is it possible?
10111-8{_}3151{_}You, the best scholar in all India!"
10111-8{_}3152{_}"And which amounted to exactly nothing.
10111-8{_}3153{_}By the admirable seestem purshood at your public schools, just about as much knowledge as he could get by three months' application at home.
10111-8{_}3154{_}Mind ye, I don't say he would apply; it is most probable he would do no such thing.
10111-8{_}3155{_}But, at the cost of--how much?
10111-8{_}3156{_}two hundred pounds annually--for five years--he has acquired about five and twenty guineas' worth of classical leeterature--enough, I dare say, to enable him to quote Horace respectably through life, and what more do you want from a young man of his expectations?
10111-8{_}3157{_}I think I should send him into the army, that's the best place for him--there's the least to do and the handsomest clothes to wear," says the little wag, daintily taking up the tail of his friend's coat.
10111-8{_}3158{_}"In earnest now, Tom Newcome, I think your boy is as fine a lad as I ever set eyes on.
10111-8{_}3159{_}He seems to have intelligence and good temper.
10111-8{_}3160{_}He carries his letter of recommendation in his countenance; and with the honesty--and the rupees, mind ye,--which he inherits from his father, the deuce is in it if he can't make his way.
10111-8{_}3161{_}What time's the breakfast?
10111-8{_}3162{_}Eh, but it was a comfort this morning not to hear the holystoning on the deck.
10111-8{_}3163{_}We ought to go into lodgings, and not fling our money out of the window of this hotel.
10111-8{_}3164{_}We must make the young chap take us about and show us the town in the morning, eh, Colonel?"
10111-8{_}3165{_}With this the jolly gentleman nodded over his candle to his friend, and trotted off to bed.
10111-8{_}3166{_}The Colonel and his friend were light sleepers and early risers.
10111-8{_}3167{_}The next morning when Binnie entered the sitting-room he found the Colonel had preceded him.
10111-8{_}3168{_}"Hush," says the Colonel, putting a long finger up to his mouth, and advancing towards him as noiselessly as a ghost.
10111-8{_}3169{_}"What's in the wind now?"
10111-8{_}3170{_}asks the little Scot; "and what for have ye not got your shoes on?"
10111-8{_}3171{_}"Clive's asleep," says the Colonel, with a countenance full of extreme anxiety.
10111-8{_}3172{_}"The darling boy slumbers, does he?"
10111-8{_}3173{_}said the wag.
10111-8{_}3174{_}"Mayn't I just step in and look at his beautiful countenance whilst he's asleep, Colonel?"
10111-8{_}3175{_}"You may if you take off those confounded creaking, shoes," the other answered, quite gravely: and Binnie turned away to hide his jolly round face, which was screwed up with laughter.
10111-8{_}3176{_}"Have ye been breathing a prayer over your rosy infant's slumbers, Tom?"
10111-8{_}3177{_}asks Mr. Binnie.
10111-8{_}3178{_}"And if I have, James Binnie," the Colonel said gravely, and his sallow face blushing somewhat, "if I have I hope I've done no harm.
10111-8{_}3179{_}The last time I saw him asleep was nine years ago, a sickly little pale-faced boy, in his little cot, and now, sir, that I see him again, strong and handsome and all that a fond father can wish to see a boy, I should be an ungrateful villain, James, if I didn't do what you said just now, and thank God Almighty for restoring him to me."
10111-8{_}3180{_}Binnie did not laugh any more.
10111-8{_}3181{_}"By George!
10111-8{_}3182{_}Tom Newcome," said he, "you're just one of the saints of the earth.
10111-8{_}3183{_}If all men were like you there'd be an end of both our trades; and there would be no fighting and no soldiering, no rogues, and no magistrates to catch them."
10111-8{_}3184{_}The Colonel wondered at his friend's enthusiasm, who was not used to be complimentary; indeed what so usual with him as that simple act of gratitude and devotion about which his comrade spoke to him?
10111-8{_}3185{_}To ask a blessing for his boy was as natural to him as to wake with the sunrise, or to go to rest when the day was over.
10111-8{_}3186{_}His first and his last thought was always the child.
10111-8{_}3187{_}The two gentlemen were home in time enough to find Clive dressed, and his uncle arrived for breakfast.
10111-8{_}3188{_}The Colonel said a grace over that meal; the life was begun which he had longed and prayed for, and the son smiling before his eyes who had been in his thoughts for so many fond years.
10111-8{_}3189{_}If my memory serves me right it was at about this time that I, the humble biographer of Mr. Clive Newcome's life, met him again for the first time since my school days at Grey Friars.
10111-8{_}3190{_}Going to the play one night with some fellows of my own age, and laughing enthusiastically at the farce, we became naturally hungry at midnight, and a desire for Welch Rabbits and good old glee-singing led us to the "Cave of Harmony," then kept by the celebrated Hoskins, with whom we enjoyed such intimacy that he never failed to greet us with a kind nod.
10111-8{_}3191{_}We also knew the three admirable glee-singers.
10111-8{_}3192{_}It happened that there was a very small attendance at the "Cave" that night, and we were all more sociable and friendly because the company was select.
10111-8{_}3193{_}The songs were chiefly of the sentimental class; such ditties were much in vogue at the time of which I speak.
10111-8{_}3194{_}There came into the "Cave" a gentleman with a lean brown face and long black moustaches, dressed in very loose clothes, and evidently a stranger to the place.
10111-8{_}3195{_}At least he had not visited it for a long time.
10111-8{_}3196{_}He was pointing out changes to a lad who was in his company; and, calling for sherry and water, he listened to the music, and twirled his moustaches with great enthusiasm.
10111-8{_}3197{_}At the very first glimpse of me the boy jumped up from the table, bounded across the room, ran to me with his hands out, and, blushing, said, "Don't you know me?"
10111-8{_}3198{_}It was little Newcome, my school-fellow, whom I had not seen for six years, grown a fine tall young stripling now, with the same bright blue eyes which I remembered when he was quite a little boy.
10111-8{_}3199{_}"What the deuce brings you here?"
10111-8{_}3200{_}said I.
10111-8{_}3201{_}He laughed and looked roguish.
10111-8{_}3202{_}"My father--that's my father--would come.
10111-8{_}3203{_}He's just come back from India.
10111-8{_}3204{_}He says all the wits used to come here.
10111-8{_}3205{_}I told him your name, and that you used to be very kind to me when I first went to Smithfield.
10111-8{_}3206{_}I've left now: I'm to have a private tutor.
10111-8{_}3207{_}I say, I've got such a jolly pony.
10111-8{_}3208{_}It's better fun than old Smiffle."
10111-8{_}3209{_}Here the whiskered gentleman, Newcome's father, strode across the room twirling his moustaches, and came up to the table where we sat, making a salutation with his hat in a very stately and polite manner, so that Hoskins himself felt obliged to bow; the glee-singers murmured among themselves, and that mischievous little wag, little Nadab the Improvisatore, began to mimic him, feeling his imaginary whiskers, after the manner of the stranger, and flapping about his pocket-handkerchief in the most ludicrous manner.
10111-8{_}3210{_}Hoskins checked this sternly, looking towards Nadab, and at the same time calling upon the gents to give their orders.
10111-8{_}3211{_}Newcome's father came up and held out his hand to me, and he spoke in a voice so soft and pleasant, and with a cordiality so simple and sincere, that my laughter shrank away ashamed; and gave place to a feeling much more respectful and friendly.
10111-8{_}3212{_}"I have heard of your kindness, sir," says he, "to my boy.
10111-8{_}3213{_}And whoever is kind to him is kind to me.
10111-8{_}3214{_}Will you allow me to sit down by you?
10111-8{_}3215{_}And may I beg you to try my cheroots?"
10111-8{_}3216{_}We were friends in a minute, young Newcome snuggling by my side, his father opposite, to whom, after a minute or two of conversation, I presented my three college friends.
10111-8{_}3217{_}"You have come here, gentlemen, to see the wits," says the Colonel.
10111-8{_}3218{_}"Are there any celebrated persons in the room?
10111-8{_}3219{_}I have been five and thirty years from home, and want to see all there is to be seen."
10111-8{_}3220{_}King of Corpus (who was an incorrigible wag) was about to point out a half dozen of people in the room, as the most celebrated wits of that day; but I cut King's shins under the table, and got the fellow to hold his tongue, while Jones wrote on his card to Hoskins, hinted to him that a boy was in the room, and a gentleman who was quite a greenhorn: hence that the songs had better be carefully selected.
10111-8{_}3221{_}And so they were.
10111-8{_}3222{_}A lady's school might have come in, and have taken no harm by what happened.
10111-8{_}3223{_}It was worth a guinea to see the simple Colonel and his delight at the music.
10111-8{_}3224{_}He forgot all about the distinguished wits whom he had expected to see, in his pleasure over the glees, and joined in all the choruses with an exceedingly sweet voice.
10111-8{_}3225{_}And now young Nadab commenced one of those surprising feats of Improvisation with which he used to charm audiences.
10111-8{_}3226{_}He took us all off and had rhymes pat about all the principal persons in the room; when he came to the Colonel himself, he burst out--  A military gent I see, and while his face I scan, I think you'll all agree with me he came from Hindostan.
10111-8{_}3227{_}And by his side sits laughing free a youth with curly head, I think you'll all agree with me that he was best in bed.
10111-8{_}3228{_}Ritolderol, etc., etc.
10111-8{_}3229{_}The Colonel laughed immensely at this sally, and clapped his son, young Clive, on the shoulder.
10111-8{_}3230{_}"Hear what he says of you, sir?
10111-8{_}3231{_}Clive, best be off to bed, my boy--ho, ho!
10111-8{_}3232{_}No, no.
10111-8{_}3233{_}We know a trick worth two of that.
10111-8{_}3234{_}'We won't go home till morning, till daylight does appear.'
10111-8{_}3235{_}Why should we?
10111-8{_}3236{_}Why shouldn't my boy have innocent pleasure?
10111-8{_}3237{_}I was allowed none when I was a young chap, and the severity was nearly the ruin of me.
10111-8{_}3238{_}I must go and speak with that young man--the most astonishing thing I ever heard in my life.
10111-8{_}3239{_}What's his name?
10111-8{_}3240{_}Mr. Nadab?
10111-8{_}3241{_}Mr. Nadab; sir, you have delighted me.
10111-8{_}3242{_}May I make so free as to ask you to come and dine with me to-morrow at six.
10111-8{_}3243{_}I am always proud to make the acquaintance of men of genius, and you are one or my name is not Newcome!"
10111-8{_}3244{_}"Sir, you do me the Honour," says Mr. Nadab, "and perhaps the day will come when the world will do me justice,--may I put down your Honoured name for my book of poems?"
10111-8{_}3245{_}"Of course, my dear sir," says the enthusiastic Colonel, "I'll send them all over India.
10111-8{_}3246{_}Put me down for six copies and do me the favour to bring them to-morrow when you come to dinner."
10111-8{_}3247{_}And now Mr. Hoskins, asking if any gentleman would volunteer a song, what was our amazement when the simple Colonel offered to sing himself, at which the room applauded vociferously; whilst methought poor Clive Newcome hung down his head, and blushed as red as a peony.
10111-8{_}3248{_}The Colonel selected the ditty of "Wapping Old Stairs," which charming old song he sang so pathetically that even the professional gentlemen buzzed a sincere applause, and some wags who were inclined to jeer at the beginning of the performance, clinked their glasses and rapped their sticks with quite a respectful enthusiasm.
10111-8{_}3249{_}When the song was over, Clive held up his head too; looked round with surprise and pleasure in his eyes; and we, I need not say, backed our friend, delighted to see him come out of his queer scrape so triumphantly.
10111-8{_}3250{_}The Colonel bowed and smiled with very pleasant good-nature at our plaudits.
10111-8{_}3251{_}There was something touching in the naivetée and kindness of the placid and simple gentleman.
10111-8{_}3252{_}Whilst the Colonel had been singing his ballad there had come into the room a gentleman, by name Captain Costigan, who was in his usual condition at this hour of the night.
10111-8{_}3253{_}Holding on by various tables, he had sidled up without accident to himself or any of the jugs and glasses round about him, to the table where we sat, and seated himself warbling the refrain of the Colonel's song.
10111-8{_}3254{_}Then having procured a glass of whiskey and water he gave what he called one of his prime songs.
10111-8{_}3255{_}The unlucky wretch, who scarcely knew what he was doing or saying, selected the most offensive song in his repertoire.
10111-8{_}3256{_}At the end of the second verse the Colonel started up, clapping on his hat, seizing his stick, and looking ferocious.
10111-8{_}3257{_}"Silence!"
10111-8{_}3258{_}he roared out.
10111-8{_}3259{_}"Hear, hear!"
10111-8{_}3260{_}cried certain wags at a farther table.
10111-8{_}3261{_}"Go on, Costigan!"
10111-8{_}3262{_}said others.
10111-8{_}3263{_}"Go on!"
10111-8{_}3264{_}cries the Colonel in his high voice, trembling with anger.
10111-8{_}3265{_}"Does any gentleman say go on?
10111-8{_}3266{_}Does any man who has a wife and sisters or children at home, say go on?
10111-8{_}3267{_}Do you dare, sir, to call yourself a gentleman, and to say that you hold the King's commission, and to sit amongst Christians and men of honour, and defile the ears of young boys with this wicked balderdash?"
10111-8{_}3268{_}"Why do you bring young boys here, old boy?"
10111-8{_}3269{_}cries a voice of the malcontents.
10111-8{_}3270{_}"Why?
10111-8{_}3271{_}Because I thought I was coming to a society of gentlemen," cried out the indignant Colonel.
10111-8{_}3272{_}"Because I never could have believed that Englishmen could meet together and allow a man, and an old man, so to disgrace himself.
10111-8{_}3273{_}For shame, you old wretch!
10111-8{_}3274{_}Go home to your bed, you hoary old sinner!
10111-8{_}3275{_}And for my part, I'm not sorry that my son should see, for once in his life, to what shame and degradation and dishonour, drunkenness and whiskey may bring a man.
10111-8{_}3276{_}Never mind the change, sir!--Curse the change!"
10111-8{_}3277{_}says the Colonel, facing the amazed waiter.
10111-8{_}3278{_}"Keep it till you see me in this place again; which will be never--by George, never!"
10111-8{_}3279{_}And shouldering his stick, and scowling round at the company of scared bacchanalians, the indignant gentleman stalked away, his boy after him.
10111-8{_}3280{_}Clive seemed rather shamedfaced, but I fear the rest of the company looked still more foolish.
10111-8{_}3281{_}For if the truth be told that uplifted cane of the Colonel's had somehow fallen on the back of every man in the room.
10111-8{_}3282{_}While Clive and his father are becoming better acquainted let us pass on to Brighton, and glance at the household of that good, brisk old lady, Clive's Aunt Honeyman.
10111-8{_}3283{_}Now Aunt Honeyman was a woman of spirit and resolution, and when she found her income sadly diminished by financial reverses she brought her furniture to Brighton, also a faithful maid servant who had learned her letters and worked her first sampler under Miss Honeyman's own eye, and whom she adored all through her life.
10111-8{_}3284{_}With this outfit the brisk little lady took a house, and let the upper floors to lodgers, and because of her personal attractions and her good housekeeping her rooms were seldom empty.
10111-8{_}3285{_}On the morning when we first visit Miss Honeyman's a gentleman had just applied there for rooms.
10111-8{_}3286{_}"Please to speak to mistress," says Hannah, the maid, opening the parlour door with a curtsey.
10111-8{_}3287{_}"A gentleman about the apartments, mum."
10111-8{_}3288{_}"Fife bet-rooms," says the man entering.
10111-8{_}3289{_}"Six bets, two or dree sitting-rooms?
10111-8{_}3290{_}We gome from Dr.
10111-8{_}3291{_}Good-enough."
10111-8{_}3292{_}"Are the apartments for you, sir?"
10111-8{_}3293{_}says Miss Honeyman, looking up at the large gentleman.
10111-8{_}3294{_}"For my lady," answers the man.
10111-8{_}3295{_}"Had you not better take off your hat?"
10111-8{_}3296{_}asks Miss Honeyman.
10111-8{_}3297{_}The man grins and takes off his hat.
10111-8{_}3298{_}Whereupon Miss Honeyman, having heard also that a German's physician has especially recommended Miss Honeyman's as a place in which one of his patients can have a change of air and scene, informs the man that she can let his mistress have the desired number of apartments.
10111-8{_}3299{_}The man reports to his mistress, who descends to inspect the apartments, and pronounces them exceedingly neat and pleasant and exactly what are wanted.
10111-8{_}3300{_}The baggage is forthwith ordered to be brought from the carriages.
10111-8{_}3301{_}The little invalid, wrapped in his shawl, is carried upstairs as gently as possible, while the young ladies, the governess, the maids, are shown to their apartments.
10111-8{_}3302{_}The eldest young lady, a slim black-haired young lass of thirteen, frisks about the rooms, looks at all the pictures, runs in and out of the veranda, tries the piano, and bursts out laughing at its wheezy jingle.
10111-8{_}3303{_}She also kisses her languid little brother laid on the sofa, and performs a hundred gay and agile motions suited to her age.
10111-8{_}3304{_}"Oh, what a piano!
10111-8{_}3305{_}Why, it is as cracked as Miss Quigley's voice!"
10111-8{_}3306{_}"My dear!"
10111-8{_}3307{_}says mamma.
10111-8{_}3308{_}The little languid boy bursts out into a jolly laugh.
10111-8{_}3309{_}"What funny pictures, mamma!
10111-8{_}3310{_}Action with Count de Grasse; the death of General Wolfe; a portrait of an officer, an old officer in blue, like grandpapa; Brasenose College, Oxford; what a funny name."
10111-8{_}3311{_}At the idea of Brasenose College, another laugh comes from the invalid.

10111-8{_}3314{_}A teaspoonful to be taken when the cough is troublesome."

10111-8{_}3316{_}the blue, the fresh, the ever free," sings the young lady, with a shake.
10111-8{_}3317{_}"How much better is this than going home and seeing those horrid factories and chimneys!
10111-8{_}3318{_}I love Dr. Goodenough for sending us here.
10111-8{_}3319{_}What a sweet house it is.
10111-8{_}3320{_}What nice rooms!"

10111-8{_}3322{_}She curtseys with dignity to her lodger, who vouchsafes a very slight inclination of the head, saying that the apartments will do very well.
10111-8{_}3323{_}"And they have such a beautiful view of the sea!"
10111-8{_}3324{_}cries Ethel.

10111-8{_}3337{_}Dr. Goodenough has ordered ass's milk.
10111-8{_}3338{_}Anything further I want I will communicate through the man who first spoke to you--and that will do."
10111-8{_}3339{_}A heavy shower of rain was descending at this moment, and little Miss Honeyman, looking at her lodger, who had sat down and taken up her book, said, "Have your ladyship's servants unpacked your trunks?"
10111-8{_}3340{_}"What on earth, madam, have you--has that to do with the question?"
10111-8{_}3341{_}"They will be put to the trouble of packing again, I fear.
10111-8{_}3342{_}I cannot provide--three times five are fifteen--fifteen separate meals for seven persons--besides those of my own family.
10111-8{_}3343{_}If your servants cannot eat with mine, or in my kitchen, they and their mistress must go elsewhere.
10111-8{_}3344{_}And the sooner the better, madam, the sooner the better!"

10111-8{_}3355{_}And with this the indignant little landlady sailed out of the room.
10111-8{_}3356{_}"Gracious goodness!

10111-8{_}3368{_}What is it, my blessed darling?

10111-8{_}3370{_}Give her all, Ethel.

10111-8{_}3372{_}Let her take my all.
10111-8{_}3373{_}The monster!
10111-8{_}3374{_}The child must live!
10111-8{_}3375{_}It can't go away in such a storm as this.

10111-8{_}3380{_}Alfred roared out, "No, it's not n--ice; it's n-a-a-sty!
10111-8{_}3381{_}I won't have syrup.
10111-8{_}3382{_}I _will_ have dinner."
10111-8{_}3383{_}The mother, whose embraces the child repelled with infantine kicks, plunged madly at the bells, rang them all four vehemently, and ran downstairs towards the parlour, whence Miss Honeyman was issuing.
10111-8{_}3384{_}The good lady had not at first known the names of her lodgers, until one of the nurses intrusted with the care of Master Alfred's dinner informed her that she was entertaining Lady Ann Newcome; and that the pretty girl was the fair Miss Ethel; the little sick boy, the little Alfred of whom his cousin spoke, and of whom Clive had made a hundred little drawings in his rude way, as he drew everybody.
10111-8{_}3385{_}Then bidding Sally run off to St. James Street for a chicken, she saw it put on the spit, and prepared a bread sauce, and composed a batter-pudding, as she only knew how to make batter puddings.
10111-8{_}3386{_}Then she went to array herself in her best clothes, as we have seen; then she came to wait upon Lady Ann, not a little flurried as to the result of that queer interview; then she whisked out of the drawing-room, as before has been shown; and, finding the chicken roasted to a turn, the napkin and tray ready spread by Hannah the neat-handed, she was bringing them up to the little patient when the frantic parent met her on the stair.
10111-8{_}3387{_}"Is it--is it for my child?"
10111-8{_}3388{_}cried Lady Ann, reeling against the bannister.
10111-8{_}3389{_}"Yes, it's for the child," says Miss Honeyman, tossing up her head.
10111-8{_}3390{_}"But nobody else has anything in the house."
10111-8{_}3391{_}"God bless you!
10111-8{_}3392{_}God bless you!
10111-8{_}3393{_}A mother's bl--l-ess-ings go with you," gurgled the lady, who was not, it must be confessed, a woman of strong moral character.
10111-8{_}3394{_}It was good to see the little man eating the fowl.
10111-8{_}3395{_}Ethel, who had never cut anything in her young existence, except her fingers now and then with her brother's and her governess's penknives, bethought her of asking Miss Honeyman to carve the chicken.
10111-8{_}3396{_}Lady Ann, with clasped hands and streaming eyes, sat looking on at the ravishing scene.
10111-8{_}3397{_}"Why did you not let us know you were Clive's aunt?"
10111-8{_}3398{_}Ethel asked, putting out her hand.
10111-8{_}3399{_}The old lady took hers very kindly, and said, "Because you didn't give me time,--and do you love Clive, my dear?"
10111-8{_}3400{_}The reconciliation between Miss Honeyman and her lodger was perfect, and for a brief season Lady Ann Newcome was in rapture with her new lodgings and every person and thing which they contained.
10111-8{_}3401{_}The drawing-rooms were fitted with the greatest taste; the dinner was exquisite; were there ever such delicious veal cutlets, such fresh French beans?
10111-8{_}3402{_}"Indeed they were very good," said Miss Ethel, "I am so glad you like the house, and Clive, and Miss Honeyman."
10111-8{_}3403{_}Ethel's mother was constantly falling in love with new acquaintances; so these raptures were no novelty to her daughter.
10111-8{_}3404{_}Ethel had had so many governesses, all darlings during the first week, and monsters afterwards, that the poor child possessed none of the accomplishments of her age.
10111-8{_}3405{_}She could not play on the piano; she could not speak French well; she could not tell you when gunpowder was invented; she had not the faintest idea of the date of the Norman Conquest, or whether the earth went round the sun, or vice versa.
10111-8{_}3406{_}She did not know the number of counties in England, Scotland and Wales, let alone Ireland; she did not know the difference between latitude and longitude.
10111-8{_}3407{_}She had had so many governesses; their accounts differed; poor Ethel was bewildered by a multiplicity of teachers, and thought herself a monster of ignorance.
10111-8{_}3408{_}They gave her a book at a Sunday school, and little girls of eight years old answered questions of which she knew nothing.
10111-8{_}3409{_}The place swam before her.
10111-8{_}3410{_}She could not see the sun shining on their fair flaxen heads and pretty faces.
10111-8{_}3411{_}The rosy little children, holding up their eager hands and crying the answer to this question and that, seemed mocking her.
10111-8{_}3412{_}She seemed to read in the book, "Oh, Ethel, you dunce, dunce, dunce!"
10111-8{_}3413{_}She went home silent in the carriage, and burst into bitter tears on her bed.
10111-8{_}3414{_}Naturally a haughty girl of the highest spirit, resolute and imperious, this little visit to the parish school taught Ethel lessons more valuable than ever so much arithmetic and geography.
10111-8{_}3415{_}When Ethel was thirteen years old she had grown to be such a tall girl that she overtopped her companions by a head or more, and morally perhaps, also, felt herself too tall for their society.
10111-8{_}3416{_}"Fancy myself," she thought, "dressing a doll like Lily Putland, or wearing a pinafore like Lucy Tucker!"
10111-8{_}3417{_}She did not care for their sports.
10111-8{_}3418{_}She could not walk with them; it seemed as if everyone stared; nor dance with them at the academy; nor attend the _Cours de Litterature Universelle et de Science Comprehensive_ of the professor then the mode.
10111-8{_}3419{_}The smallest girls took her up in the class.
10111-8{_}3420{_}She was bewildered by the multitude of things they bade her learn.
10111-8{_}3421{_}At the youthful little assemblies of her sex, when, under the guide of their respected governesses, the girls came to tea at six o'clock, dancing, charades, and so forth, Ethel herded not with the children of her own age, nor yet with the teachers who sat apart at these assemblies, imparting to each other their little wrongs.
10111-8{_}3422{_}But Ethel romped with the little children, the rosy little trots, and took them on her knees, and told them a thousand stories.
10111-8{_}3423{_}By these she was adored, and loved like a mother almost, for as such the hearty, kindly girl showed herself to them; but at home she was alone, and intractable, and did battle with the governesses, and overcame them one after another.
10111-8{_}3424{_}While Lady Ann Newcome and her children were at Brighton, Lady Kew, mother of Lady Ann, was also staying there, but refused to visit the house in which her daughter was stopping for fear that she herself might contract the disease from which her grandchildren were recovering.
