10111-8{_}3425{_}She received news of them, however, through her grandson, Lord Kew, and his friend Jack Belsize, who enjoyed dining with the old lady whenever they were given the opportunity.
10111-8{_}3426{_}Having met their cousins one day before dining with Lady Kew their news was most interesting and enthusiastic.
10111-8{_}3427{_}"That little chap who has just had the measles--he's a dear little brick," said Jack Belsize.
10111-8{_}3428{_}"And as for Miss Ethel--"  "Ethel is a trump, mam," says Lord Kew, slapping his hand on his knee.
10111-8{_}3429{_}"Ethel is a brick, and Alfred is a trump, I think you say," remarks Lady Kew, "and Barnes is a snob.
10111-8{_}3430{_}This is very satisfactory to know."
10111-8{_}3431{_}"We met the children out to-day," cries the enthusiastic Kew, "as I was driving Jack in the drag, and I got out and talked to 'em.
10111-8{_}3432{_}The little fellow wanted a drive and I said I would drive him and Ethel, too, if she would come.
10111-8{_}3433{_}Upon my word she's as pretty a girl as you can see on a summer's day.
10111-8{_}3434{_}And the governess said, no, of course; governesses always do.
10111-8{_}3435{_}But I said I was her uncle, and Jack paid her such a fine compliment that she finally let the children take their seats beside me, and Jack went behind.
10111-8{_}3436{_}We drove on to the Downs; my horses are young, and when they get on the grass they are as if they were mad.
10111-8{_}3437{_}They ran away, ever so far, and I thought the carriage must upset.
10111-8{_}3438{_}The poor little boy, who has lost his pluck in the fever, began to cry; but that young girl, though she was as white as a sheet, never gave up for a moment, and sat in her place like a man.
10111-8{_}3439{_}We met nothing, luckily; and I pulled the horses in after a mile or two, and I drove 'em into Brighton as quiet as if I had been driving a hearse.
10111-8{_}3440{_}And that little trump of an Ethel, what do you think she said?
10111-8{_}3441{_}She said: 'I was not frightened, but you must not tell mamma.'
10111-8{_}3442{_}My aunt, it appears, was in a dreadful commotion.
10111-8{_}3443{_}I ought to have thought of that."
10111-8{_}3444{_}There is a brother of Sir Brian Newcome's staying with them, Lord Kew perceives; an East India Colonel, a very fine-looking old boy.
10111-8{_}3445{_}He was on the lookout for them, and when they came in sight he despatched a boy who was with him, running like a lamplighter, back to their aunt to say all was well.
10111-8{_}3446{_}And he took little Alfred out of the carriage, and then helped out Ethel, and said, "My dear, you are too pretty to scold; but you have given us all a great fright."
10111-8{_}3447{_}And then he made Kew and Jack a low bow, and stalked into the lodgings.
10111-8{_}3448{_}Then they went up and made their peace and were presented in form to the Colonel and his youthful cub.
10111-8{_}3449{_}"As fine a fellow as I ever saw," cries Jack Belsize.
10111-8{_}3450{_}"The young chap is a great hand at drawing--upon my life the best drawings I ever saw.
10111-8{_}3451{_}And he was making a picture for little What-do-you-call-'im, and Miss Newcome was looking over them.
10111-8{_}3452{_}And Lady Ann pointed out the group to me, and said how pretty it was."
10111-8{_}3453{_}In consequence of this conversation, which aroused her curiosity, Lady Kew sent a letter that night to Lady Ann Newcome, desiring that Ethel should be sent to see her grandmother; Ethel, who was no weakling in character despite her youth, and who always rebelled against her grandmother and always fought on her Aunt Julia's side when that amiable invalid lady, who lived with her mother, was oppressed by the dominating older woman.
10111-8{_}3454{_}From the foregoing facts we gather that Thomas Newcome had not been many weeks in England before he favoured good little Miss Honeyman with a visit, to her great delight.
10111-8{_}3455{_}You may be sure that the visit was an event in her life.
10111-8{_}3456{_}And she was especially pleased that it should occur at the time when the Colonel's kinsfolk were staying under her roof.
10111-8{_}3457{_}On the day of the Colonel's arrival all the presents which Newcome had ever sent his sister-in-law from India had been taken out of the cotton and lavender in which the faithful creature kept them.
10111-8{_}3458{_}It was a fine hot day in June, but I promise you Miss Honeyman wore her blazing scarlet Cashmere shawl; her great brooch, representing the Taj of Agra, was in her collar; and her bracelets decorated the sleeves round her lean old hands, which trembled with pleasure as they received the kind grasp of the Colonel of colonels.
10111-8{_}3459{_}How busy those hands had been that morning!
10111-8{_}3460{_}What custards they had whipped!
10111-8{_}3461{_}What a triumph of pie-crusts they had achieved!
10111-8{_}3462{_}Before Colonel Newcome had been ten minutes in the house the celebrated veal-cutlets made their appearance.
10111-8{_}3463{_}Was not the whole house adorned in expectation of his coming?
10111-8{_}3464{_}The good woman's eyes twinkled, the kind old hand and voice shook, as, holding up a bright glass of Madeira, Miss Honeyman drank the Colonel's health.
10111-8{_}3465{_}"I promise you, my dear Colonel," says she, nodding her head, adorned with a bristling superstructure of lace and ribbons, "I promise you, that I can drink your health in good wine!"
10111-8{_}3466{_}The wine was of his own sending, and so were the China firescreens, and the sandal-wood work-box, and the ivory card case, and those magnificent pink and white chessmen, carved like little sepoys and mandarins, with the castles on elephants' backs, George the Third and his queen in pink ivory against the Emperor of China and lady in white--the delight of Clive's childhood, the chief ornament of the old spinster's sitting-room.
10111-8{_}3467{_}Miss Honeyman's little feast was pronounced to be the perfection of cookery; and when the meal was over, came a noise of little feet at the parlour door, which being opened, there appeared: first, a tall nurse with a dancing baby; second and third, two little girls with little frocks, little trowsers, long ringlets, blue eyes, and blue ribbons to match; fourth, Master Alfred, now quite recovered from his illness and holding by the hand, fifth, Miss Ethel Newcome, blushing like a rose.
10111-8{_}3468{_}Hannah, grinning, acted as mistress of the ceremonies, calling out the names of "Miss Newcome, Master Newcome, to see the Colonel, if you please, ma'am," bobbing a curtsey, and giving a knowing nod to Master Clive, as she smoothed her new silk apron.
10111-8{_}3469{_}Miss Ethel did not cease blushing as she advanced towards her uncle; and the honest campaigner started up, blushing too.
10111-8{_}3470{_}Mr. Clive rose also, as little Alfred, of whom he was a great friend, ran towards him.
10111-8{_}3471{_}Clive rose, laughed, nodded at Ethel, and ate ginger-bread nuts all at the same time.
10111-8{_}3472{_}As for Colonel Thomas Newcome and his niece, they fell in love with each other instantaneously, like Prince Camaralzaman and the Princess of China.
10111-8{_}3473{_}"Mamma has sent us to bid you welcome to England, uncle," says Miss Ethel, advancing, and never thinking for a moment of laying aside that fine blush which she brought into the room, and which was her pretty symbol of youth and modesty and beauty.
10111-8{_}3474{_}He took a little slim white hand and laid it down on his brown palm, where it looked all the whiter; he cleared the grizzled moustache from his mouth, and stooping down he kissed the little white hand with a great deal of grace and dignity, after which he was forever the humble and devoted admirer of that bright young girl.
10111-8{_}3475{_}Raising himself from his salute, he heard a pretty little infantile chorus.
10111-8{_}3476{_}"How do you do, uncle?"
10111-8{_}3477{_}said girls number two and three, while the dancing baby in the arms of the bobbing nurse babbled a welcome.
10111-8{_}3478{_}Alfred looked up for a while at his uncle in the white trousers, and then instantly proposed that Clive should make some drawings; and was on his knees at the next moment.
10111-8{_}3479{_}He was always climbing on somebody or something, or winding over chairs, curling through bannisters, standing on somebody's head, or his own head; as his convalescence advanced, his breakages were fearful.
10111-8{_}3480{_}Miss Honeyman and Hannah talked about his dilapidations for years after.
10111-8{_}3481{_}When he was a jolly young officer in the Guards, and came to see them at Brighton, they showed him the blue dragon Chayny jar on which he would sit, and over which he cried so fearfully upon breaking it.
10111-8{_}3482{_}When this little party had gone out smiling to take its walk on the sea shore, the Colonel from his balcony watched the slim figure of pretty Ethel, looked fondly after her, and as the smoke of his cigar floated in the air, formed a fine castle in it, whereof Clive was Lord, and Ethel Lady.
10111-8{_}3483{_}"What a frank, generous, bright young creature is yonder!"
10111-8{_}3484{_}thought he.
10111-8{_}3485{_}"How cheering and gay she is; how good to Miss Honeyman, to whom she behaved with just the respect that was the old lady's due.
10111-8{_}3486{_}How affectionate with her brothers and sisters!
10111-8{_}3487{_}What a sweet voice she had!
10111-8{_}3488{_}What a pretty little white hand it is!
10111-8{_}3489{_}When she gave it me, it looked like a little white bird lying in mine."
10111-8{_}3490{_}Thus mused the Colonel, upon the charms of the young girl who was henceforth to occupy the first place in his affection.
10111-8{_}3491{_}His admiration for her might have been still further heightened had he been at Lady Ann's breakfast table some four or five weeks later, when Lady Ann and her nursery had just returned to London, little Alfred being perfectly set up by a month of Brighton air.
10111-8{_}3492{_}Barnes Newcome had just discovered an article in the Newcome Independent commenting warmly upon a visit which Colonel Newcome and Clive had recently paid to Newcome, the object of that visit having been the Colonel's desire to gladden the eyes of his old nurse Sarah with a sight of him.
10111-8{_}3493{_}Inhabitants of Newcome, feeling that the same Sarah Mason, who was a much respected member of the community, was much neglected by her rich and influential relatives in London, took great delight in commenting upon the Colonel's attention to the aged woman.
10111-8{_}3494{_}The article in the Independent on that subject was anything but pleasing to the family pride of Mr. Barnes, who remarked in a sneering tone, "My uncle the Colonel, and his amiable son, have been paying a visit to Newcome.
10111-8{_}3495{_}That is the news which the paper announces triumphantly," said Mr. Barnes.
10111-8{_}3496{_}"You are always sneering about our uncle," broke in Ethel, impetuously, "and saying unkind things about Clive.
10111-8{_}3497{_}Our uncle is a dear, good, kind man, and I love him.
10111-8{_}3498{_}He came to Brighton to see us, and went out every day for hours and hours with Alfred; and Clive, too, drew pictures for him.
10111-8{_}3499{_}And he is good, and kind, and generous, and honest as his father.
10111-8{_}3500{_}Barnes is always speaking ill of him behind his back; and Miss Honeyman is a dear little old woman too.
10111-8{_}3501{_}Was not she kind to Alfred, mamma, and did not she make him nice jelly?"
10111-8{_}3502{_}"Did you bring some of Miss Honeyman's lodging-house cards with you, Ethel?"
10111-8{_}3503{_}sneered her brother, "and had we not better hang up one or two in Lombard Street; hers and our other relation's, Mrs.
10111-8{_}3504{_}Mason?"
10111-8{_}3505{_}"My darling love, who _is_ Mrs.
10111-8{_}3506{_}Mason?"
10111-8{_}3507{_}asks Lady Ann.
10111-8{_}3508{_}"Another member of the family, ma'am.
10111-8{_}3509{_}She was cousin--"  "She was no such thing, sir," roars Sir Brian.
10111-8{_}3510{_}"She was relative and housemaid of my grandfather during his first marriage.
10111-8{_}3511{_}She has retired into private life in her native town of Newcome.
10111-8{_}3512{_}The Colonel and young Clive have been spending a few days with their elderly relative.
10111-8{_}3513{_}It's all here in the paper, by Jove!"
10111-8{_}3514{_}Mr. Barnes clenched his fist and stamped upon the newspaper with much energy.
10111-8{_}3515{_}"And so they should go down and see her, and so the Colonel should love his nurse and not forget his relations if they are old and poor!"
10111-8{_}3516{_}cries Ethel, with a flush on her face, and tears starting in her eyes.
10111-8{_}3517{_}"The Colonel went to her like a kind, dear, good brave uncle as he is.
10111-8{_}3518{_}The very day I go to Newcome I'll go to see her."
10111-8{_}3519{_}She caught a look of negation in her father's eye.
10111-8{_}3520{_}"I will go--that is, if papa will give me leave," says Miss Ethel, adding simply, "if we had gone sooner there would not have been all this abuse of us in the papers."
10111-8{_}3521{_}To which statement her worldly father and brother perforce agreeing, we may congratulate good old nurse Sarah upon adding to the list of her friends such a frank, open-hearted, high-spirited young woman as Miss Ethel Newcome.
10111-8{_}3522{_}In spite of the notoriety given him in the newspapers by his visit to Nurse Sarah, at his native place, he still remained in high favour with Sir Brian Newcome's family, where he paid almost daily visits, and was received with affection at least by the ladies and children of the house.
10111-8{_}3523{_}Who was it that took the children to Astley's but Uncle Newcome?
10111-8{_}3524{_}I saw him there in the midst of a cluster of these little people, all children together, the little girls, Sir Brian's daughters, holding each by a finger of his hands, young Masters Alfred and Edward clapping and hurrahing by his side; while Mr. Clive and Miss Ethel sat in the back of the box enjoying the scene, but with that decorum which belonged to their superior age and gravity.
10111-8{_}3525{_}As for Clive, he was in these matters much older than the grizzled old warrior his father.
10111-8{_}3526{_}It did one good to hear the Colonel's honest laughs at Clown's jokes, and to see the tenderness and simplicity with which he watched over this happy brood of young ones.
10111-8{_}3527{_}How lavishly did he supply them with sweetmeats between the acts!
10111-8{_}3528{_}There he sat in the midst of them, and ate an orange himself with perfect satisfaction, and was eager to supply any luxury longed for by his young companions.
10111-8{_}3529{_}The Colonel's organ of benevolence was so large that he would have liked to administer bounties to the young folks his nephews and nieces in Brianstone Square, as well as to their cousins in Park Lane; but Mrs. Newcome was a great deal too virtuous to admit of such spoiling of children.
10111-8{_}3530{_}She took the poor gentleman to task for an attempt upon her boys when those lads came home for their holidays, and caused them ruefully to give back the shining gold sovereigns with which their uncle had thought to give them a treat.
10111-8{_}3531{_}So the Colonel was obliged to confine his benevolence to that branch of the family where it was graciously accepted.
10111-8{_}3532{_}Meanwhile the Colonel had a new interest to absorb his attention.
10111-8{_}3533{_}He had taken a new house at 120 Fitzroy Square in connection with that Indian friend of his, Mr. Binnie.
10111-8{_}3534{_}The house being taken, there was fine amusement for Clive, Mr. Binnie, and the Colonel, in frequenting sales, in inspection of upholsterers' shops, and the purchase of furniture for the new mansion.
10111-8{_}3535{_}There were three masters with four or five servants under them.
10111-8{_}3536{_}Irons for the Colonel and his son, a smart boy with boots for Mr. Binnie; Mrs.
10111-8{_}3537{_}Irons to cook and keep house, with a couple of maids under her.
10111-8{_}3538{_}The Colonel himself was great at making hash mutton, hotpot, and curry.
10111-8{_}3539{_}What cosy pipes did we not smoke in the dining-room, in the drawing-room, or where we would!
10111-8{_}3540{_}What pleasant evenings did we not have together.
10111-8{_}3541{_}Clive had a tutor--Grindley of Corpus--with whom the young gentleman did not fatigue his brains very much, his great talent lying decidedly in drawing.
10111-8{_}3542{_}He sketched the horses, he sketched the dogs, all the servants, from the bleer-eyed boot-boy to the rosy cheeked lass whom the housekeeper was always calling to come downstairs.
10111-8{_}3543{_}He drew his father in all postures, and jolly little Mr. Binnie too.
10111-8{_}3544{_}Young Ridley, known to his young companions as J.J., was his daily friend now, to the great joy of that young man, who considered Clive Newcome to be the most splendid, fortunate, beautiful, high-born and gifted youth in the world.
10111-8{_}3545{_}What generous boy in his time has not worshipped somebody?
10111-8{_}3546{_}Before the female enslaver makes her appearance, every lad has a friend of friends, a crony of cronies, to whom he writes immense letters in vacation, whom he cherishes in his hearts of hearts; whose sister he proposes to marry in after life; whose purse he shares; for whom he will take a thrashing if need be; who is his hero.
10111-8{_}3547{_}Clive was John James's youthful divinity; when he wanted to draw Thaddeus of Warsaw, a Prince, Ivanhoe, or some one splendid and egregious, it was Clive he took for a model.
10111-8{_}3548{_}His heart leapt when he saw the young fellow.
10111-8{_}3549{_}He would walk cheerfully to Grey Friars with a letter or message for C. on the chance of seeing him and getting a kind word from him or a shake of the hand.
10111-8{_}3550{_}The poor lad was known by the boys as Newcome's Punch.
10111-8{_}3551{_}He was all but hunchback, long and lean in the arm; sallow, with a great forehead and waving black hair, and large melancholy eyes.
10111-8{_}3552{_}But his genius for drawing was enormous, which fact Clive fully appreciated.
10111-8{_}3553{_}Because of J.
10111-8{_}3554{_}J.
10111-8{_}3555{_}'s admiration for Clive it was his joy to be with Clive constantly; and after Grindley's classics and mathematics in the morning, the young men would attend Gandish's Drawing Academy, together.
10111-8{_}3556{_}"Oh," says Clive, if you talk to him now about those early days, "it was a jolly time!
10111-8{_}3557{_}I do not believe there was any young fellow in London so happy."
10111-8{_}3558{_}Clive had many conversations with his father as to the profession which he should follow.
10111-8{_}3559{_}As regarded mathematical and classical learning, the elder Newcome was forced to admit that out of every hundred boys there were fifty as clever as his own, and at least fifty more industrious; the army in time of peace Colonel Newcome thought a bad trade for a young fellow so fond of ease and pleasure as his son.
10111-8{_}3560{_}His delight in the pencil was manifest to all.
10111-8{_}3561{_}Were not his school books full of caricatures of the masters?
10111-8{_}3562{_}While his tutor was lecturing him, did he not draw Grindley instinctively under his very nose?
10111-8{_}3563{_}A painter Clive was determined to be, and nothing else; and Clive, being then some sixteen years of age, began to study art under the eminent Mr. Gandish of Soho.
10111-8{_}3564{_}It was that well-known portrait painter, Andrew Smee, Esq., R.A., who recommended Gandish to Colonel Newcome one day when the two gentleman met at dinner at Lady Ann Newcome's.
10111-8{_}3565{_}Mr. Smee happened to examine some of Clive's drawings, which the young fellow had executed for his cousins.
10111-8{_}3566{_}Clive found no better amusement than in making pictures for them and would cheerfully pass evening after evening in that direction.
10111-8{_}3567{_}He had made a thousand sketches of Ethel before a year was over; a year every day of which seemed to increase the attractions of the fair young creature.
10111-8{_}3568{_}Also, of course Clive drew Alfred and the nursery in general, Aunt Ann and the Blenheim spaniels, the majestic John bringing in the coal-scuttle, and all persons or objects in that establishment with which he was familiar.
10111-8{_}3569{_}"What a genius the lad has," the complimentary Mr. Smee averred; "what a force and individuality there is in all his drawings!
10111-8{_}3570{_}Look at his horses!
10111-8{_}3571{_}Capital, by Jove, capital!
10111-8{_}3572{_}And Alfred on his pony, and Miss Ethel in her Spanish hat, with her hair flowing in the wind!
10111-8{_}3573{_}I must take this sketch, I positively must now, and show it to Landseer."
10111-8{_}3574{_}And the courtly artist daintily enveloped the drawing in a sheet of paper, put it away in his hat, and vowed subsequently that the great painter had been delighted with the young man's performance.
10111-8{_}3575{_}Smee was not only charmed with Clive's skill as an artist, but thought his head would be an admirable one to paint.
10111-8{_}3576{_}Such a rich complexion, such fine turns in his hair!
10111-8{_}3577{_}Such eyes!
10111-8{_}3578{_}To see real blue eyes was so rare now-a-days!
10111-8{_}3579{_}And the Colonel too, if the Colonel would but give him a few sittings, the grey uniform of the Bengal Cavalry, the silver lace, the little bit of red ribbon just to warm up the picture!
10111-8{_}3580{_}It was seldom, Mr. Smee declared, that an artist could get such an opportunity for colour.
10111-8{_}3581{_}But no cajoleries could induce the Colonel to sit to any artist save one.
10111-8{_}3582{_}There hangs in Clive's room now, a head, painted at one sitting, of a man rather bald, with hair touched with grey, with a large moustache and a sweet mouth half smiling beneath it, and melancholy eyes.
10111-8{_}3583{_}Clive shows that portrait of their grandfather to his children, and tells them that the whole world never saw a nobler gentleman.
10111-8{_}3584{_}Well, then; Clive having decided to become an artist, on a day marked with a white stone, Colonel Newcome with his son and Mr. Smee, R. A., walked to Gandish's and entered the would-be artist on the roll call of that famous academy, and of J. J. as well, for the Colonel had insisted upon paying his expenses as an art student together with his son.
10111-8{_}3585{_}Mr. Gandish was an excellent master and the two lads made great progress under his excellent training.
10111-8{_}3586{_}Clive used to give droll accounts of the young disciples at Gandish's, who were of various ages and conditions, and in whose company the young fellow took his place with that good temper and gaiety which seldom deserted him and put him at ease wherever his fate led him.
10111-8{_}3587{_}Not one of the Gandishites but liked Clive, and at that period of his existence he enjoyed himself in all kinds of ways, making himself popular with dancing folks and with drawing folks, and the jolly king of his company everywhere.
10111-8{_}3588{_}He gave entertainments in the rooms in Fitzroy Square which were devoted to his use, inviting his father and Mr. Binnie now and then, but the good Colonel did not often attend those parties.
10111-8{_}3589{_}He saw that his presence rather silenced the young men, and went away to play his rubber of whist at the club.
10111-8{_}3590{_}And although time hung a bit heavily on the good Colonel's hands, now that Clive's interests were separate from his own, yet of nights as he heard Clive's companions tramping by his bedchamber door, where he lay wakeful within, he was happy to think his son was happy.
10111-8{_}3591{_}As for Clive, those were glorious days for him.
10111-8{_}3592{_}If he was successful in the Academy, he was doubly victorious out of it.
10111-8{_}3593{_}His person was handsome, his courage high, his gaiety and frankness delightful and winning.
10111-8{_}3594{_}His money was plenty and he spent it like a young king.
10111-8{_}3595{_}He was not the most docile of Mr. Gandish's pupils, and if the truth must be told about him, though one of the most frank, generous and kind-hearted persons, was somewhat haughty and imperious.
10111-8{_}3596{_}He had been known to lament since that he was taken from school too early where a further course of thrashings would, he believed, have done him good.
10111-8{_}3597{_}He lamented that he was not sent to college, where if a young man receives no other discipline at least he meets his equals in society and assuredly finds his betters; whereas in Mr. Gandish's studio our young gentleman scarcely found a comrade that was not in one way or other his flatterer, his inferior, his honest or dishonest admirer.
10111-8{_}3598{_}The influence of his family's rank and wealth acted more or less on all these simple folks, who would run on his errands and vied with each other winning his favour.
10111-8{_}3599{_}His very goodness of heart rendered him a more easy prey to their flattery, and his kind and jovial disposition led him into company from which he had much better have been away.
10111-8{_}3600{_}In fact, as the Colonel did not attempt in any way to check him in his youthful career of extravagance and experiences which were the result of an excessive high spirit, our young gentleman at this time brought down upon himself much adverse criticism for his behaviour, especially from his uncles.
10111-8{_}3601{_}Because of this and other reasons there was not much friendliness exhibited by the several branches of the family for Clive and his father.
10111-8{_}3602{_}Colonel Newcome, in spite of coldness, felt it his duty to make constant attempts to remain on friendly terms at least with the wives of his stepbrothers.
10111-8{_}3603{_}But after he had called twice or thrice upon his sister-in-law in Brianstone Square, bringing as was his wont a present for this little niece or a book for that, Mrs. Newcome gave him to understand that the occupation of an English matron would not allow her to pass the mornings in idle gossip, and with curtseys and fine speeches actually bowed her brother out of doors; and the honest gentleman meekly left her, though with bewilderment as he thought of the different hospitality to which he had been accustomed in the East, where no friend's house was ever closed to him, where no neighbour was so busy but he had time to make Thomas Newcome welcome.
10111-8{_}3604{_}When Hobson Newcome's boys came home for the holidays, their kind uncle was for treating them to the sights of the town, but here Virtue again interposed, and laid his interdict upon pleasure.
10111-8{_}3605{_}"Thank you, very much, my dear Colonel," says Virtue; "there never was surely such a kind, affectionate, unselfish creature as you are, and so indulgent for children, but my boys and yours are brought up on a _very different plan_.
10111-8{_}3606{_}Excuse me for saying that I do not think it is advisable that they should even see too much of each other, Clive's company is not good for them."
10111-8{_}3607{_}"Great heavens, Maria!"
10111-8{_}3608{_}cries the Colonel, starting up, "do you mean that my boy's society is not good enough for any boy alive?"
10111-8{_}3609{_}Maria turned very red; she had said not more than she meant, but more than she meant to say.
10111-8{_}3610{_}"My dear Colonel, how hot we are!
10111-8{_}3611{_}how angry you Indian gentlemen become with us poor women!
10111-8{_}3612{_}Your boy is much older than mine.
10111-8{_}3613{_}He lives with artists, with all sorts of eccentric people.
10111-8{_}3614{_}Our children are bred on _quite a different plan_.
10111-8{_}3615{_}Hobson will succeed his father in the bank, and dear Samuel, I trust, will go into the church.
10111-8{_}3616{_}I told you before the views I had regarding the boys; but it was most kind of you to think of them--most generous and kind."
10111-8{_}3617{_}"That nabob of ours is a queer fish," Hobson Newcome remarked to his nephew Barnes.
10111-8{_}3618{_}"He is as proud as Lucifer; he is always taking huff about one thing or the other.
10111-8{_}3619{_}He went off in a fume the other night because your aunt objected to his taking the boys to the play.
10111-8{_}3620{_}And then he flew out about his boy, and said that my wife insulted him!
10111-8{_}3621{_}I used to like that boy.
10111-8{_}3622{_}Before his father came he was a good lad enough--a jolly, brave little fellow.
10111-8{_}3623{_}But since he has taken this madcap freak of turning painter there is no understanding the chap.
10111-8{_}3624{_}I don't care what a fellow is, if he is a good fellow, but a painter is no trade at all!
10111-8{_}3625{_}I don't like it, Barnes!"
10111-8{_}3626{_}To Lady Ann Newcome the Colonel's society was more welcome than to her sister-in-law, and the affectionate gentleman never tired of doing kindnesses for her children, and consoled himself as best he might for Clive's absences with his nephews and nieces, especially with Ethel, for whom his admiration conceived at first sight never diminished.
10111-8{_}3627{_}He found a fine occupation in breaking a pretty little horse for her, of which he made her a present, and there was no horse in the Park that was so handsome, and surely no girl who looked more beautiful than Ethel Newcome with her broad hat and red ribbon, with her thick black locks waving round her bright face, galloping along the ride on "Bhurtpore."
10111-8{_}3628{_}Occasionally Clive was at their riding-parties, but Ethel rallied him and treated him with such distance and dignity, at the same time looking fondly and archly at her uncle, that Clive set her down as a very haughty, spoiled, aristocratic young creature.
10111-8{_}3629{_}In fact, the two young people were too much alike in disposition to agree perfectly, and Ethel's parents were glad that it was so.
10111-8{_}3630{_}It was pleasant to watch the kind old face of Clive's father, that sweet young blushing lady by his side, as the two rode homewards at sunset talking happily together.
10111-8{_}3631{_}Ethel wanted to know about battles; about lover's lamps, which she had read of in "Lalla Rookh."
10111-8{_}3632{_}"Have you ever seen them, uncle, floating down the Ganges of a night?
10111-8{_}3633{_}About Indian widows, did you actually see one burning, and hear her scream as you rode up?"
10111-8{_}3634{_}She wonders whether he will tell her anything about Clive's mother; how she must have loved Uncle Newcome!
10111-8{_}3635{_}Rambling happily from one subject to another Ethel commands: "Next year, when I am presented at Court, you must come, too, sir!
10111-8{_}3636{_}I insist upon it, you must come, too!"
10111-8{_}3637{_}"I will order a new uniform, Ethel," says her uncle.
10111-8{_}3638{_}The girl laughs.
10111-8{_}3639{_}"When little Egbert took hold of your sword, and asked you how many people you had killed, do you know I had the same question in my mind?
10111-8{_}3640{_}I thought perhaps the King would knight you instead of that horrid little Sir Danby Jilks, and I won't have you knighted anymore!"
10111-8{_}3641{_}The Colonel, laughing, says he hopes Egbert won't ask Sir Danby Jilks how many men he has killed; then thinking the joke too severe upon Sir Danby, hastens to narrate some anecdotes about the courage of surgeons in general.
10111-8{_}3642{_}Ethel declares that her uncle always will talk of other people's courage, and never say a word about his own.
10111-8{_}3643{_}So the pair talked kindly on, riding homewards through the pleasant summer twilight.
10111-8{_}3644{_}Mamma had gone out to dinner and there were cards for three parties afterward.
10111-8{_}3645{_}"Oh, how I wish it was next year!"
10111-8{_}3646{_}says Miss Ethel.
10111-8{_}3647{_}Many a splendid assembly and many a brilliant next year will the young creature enjoy; but in the midst of her splendour and triumphs she will often think of that quiet happy season before the world began for her, and of that dear old friend on whose arm she leaned while she was yet a young girl.
10111-8{_}3648{_}On account of the ugly rumours spread abroad concerning young Clive's extravagant habits and gaiety of living, also on account of the profession he had chosen, Sir Bryan Newcome's family preferred to have young Clive see as little of his handsome Cousin Ethel as possible, and Ethel's brother, Barnes, whose hatred for Clive was not untinged by jealousy, was the most vigorous of the family in spreading disagreeable reports about his cousin, whom he spoke of as an impudent young puppy.
10111-8{_}3649{_}Even old Lady Kew was particularly rude to Colonel Newcome and Clive.
10111-8{_}3650{_}On Ethel's birthday she had a small party chiefly of girls of her own age who came and played and sang together and enjoyed such mild refreshments as sponge cake, jellies, tea, and the like.
10111-8{_}3651{_}The Colonel, who was invited to this little party, sent a fine present to his favourite Ethel; and Clive and his friend J. J. made a funny series of drawings, representing the life of a young lady as they imagined it, and drawing her progress from her cradle upwards: now engaged with her doll, then with her dancing master; now marching in her backboard; now crying over her German lessons; and dressed for her first ball finally, and bestowing her hand upon a dandy of preternatural ugliness, who was kneeling at her feet as the happy man.
10111-8{_}3652{_}This picture was the delight of the laughing, happy girls; except, perhaps, the little cousins from Brianstone Square, who were invited to Ethel's party, but were so overpowered by the prodigious new dresses in which their mamma had attired them that they could admire nothing but their rustling pink frocks, their enormous sashes, their lovely new silk stockings.
10111-8{_}3653{_}Lady Kew, coming to London, attended on the party, and presented her granddaughter with a sixpenny pincushion.
10111-8{_}3654{_}The Colonel had sent Ethel a beautiful little gold watch and chain.
10111-8{_}3655{_}Her aunt had complimented her with that refreshing work, "Allison's History of Europe," richly bound.
10111-8{_}3656{_}Lady Kew's pincushion made rather a poor figure among the gifts, whence probably arose her ladyship's ill-humour.
10111-8{_}3657{_}Ethel's grandmother became exceedingly testy, when, the Colonel arriving, Ethel ran up to him and thanked him for the beautiful watch, in return for which she gave him a kiss, which I daresay amply repaid Colonel Newcome; and shortly after him Mr. Clive arrived.
10111-8{_}3658{_}As he entered, all the girls who had been admiring his pictures began to clap their hands.
10111-8{_}3659{_}Mr. Clive Newcome blushed, and looked none the worse for that indication of modesty.
10111-8{_}3660{_}Lady Kew had met Colonel Newcome a half-dozen times at her daughter's house; but on this occasion she had quite forgotten him, for when the Colonel made a bow, her ladyship regarded him steadily, and beckoning her daughter to her, asked who the gentleman was who had just kissed Ethel.
10111-8{_}3661{_}With the clapping of hands that greeted Clive's arrival, the Countess was by no means more good-humoured.
10111-8{_}3662{_}Not aware of her wrath, the young fellow, who had also previously been presented to her, came forward presently to make her his compliments.
10111-8{_}3663{_}"Pray, who are you?"
10111-8{_}3664{_}she said, looking at him very earnestly in the face.
10111-8{_}3665{_}He told her his name.
10111-8{_}3666{_}"H'm," said Lady Kew, "I have heard of you, and I have heard very little good of you."
10111-8{_}3667{_}"Will your ladyship please to give me your informant?"
10111-8{_}3668{_}cried out Colonel Newcome.
10111-8{_}3669{_}Barnes Newcome, who had condescended to attend his sister's little party, and had been languidly watching the frolics of the young people, looked very much alarmed, and hastened to soften the incident by a change of conversation.
10111-8{_}3670{_}But the attitude of Lady Kew and young Barnes was only a reflection of the attitude of Ethel's parents concerning Clive, and Ethel, who was really friendly towards him, found it difficult to deny the charges which were constantly brought against the boy.
10111-8{_}3671{_}The truth was the young fellow enjoyed life, as one of his age and spirit might be expected to do; but he did very little harm and meant less; and was quite unconscious of the reputation which he was gaining.
10111-8{_}3672{_}There had been a long-standing promise that Clive and his father were to go to Newcome at Christmas; and I daresay Ethel proposed to reform the young prodigal, if prodigal he was, for she busied herself delightedly in preparing the apartments for their guests and putting off her visit to this pleasant neighbour, or that pretty scene in the vicinity, until her uncle should come and they might enjoy the excursion together.
10111-8{_}3673{_}And before the arrival of her relatives, Ethel, with one of her young brothers, went to see Mrs. Mason and introduced herself as Colonel Newcome's niece, and came back charmed with the old lady and eager once more in defence of Clive, for had she not seen the kindest letter which Clive had written to old Mrs. Mason, and the beautiful drawing of his father on horseback, and in regimentals, waving his sword in front of the gallant Bengal Cavalry, which the lad had sent down to the good old woman?
10111-8{_}3674{_}He could not be very bad, Ethel thought, who was so kind and thoughtful for the poor.
10111-8{_}3675{_}And the young lady went home quite fired with enthusiasm for her cousin, but encountered Barnes, who was more than usually bitter and sarcastic on the subject.
10111-8{_}3676{_}Ethel lost her temper, and then her firmness, while bursting into tears she taxed Barnes with cruelty for uttering stories to his cousin's disadvantage and for pursuing with constant slander one of the very best of men.
10111-8{_}3677{_}But notwithstanding her defence of the Colonel and Clive, when they came to Newcome for the Christmas holidays, there was no Ethel there.
10111-8{_}3678{_}She had gone on a visit to her sick aunt.
10111-8{_}3679{_}Colonel Newcome passed the holidays sadly without her, and Clive consoled himself by knocking down pheasants with Sir Brian's keepers; and increased his cousin's attachment for him by breaking the knees of Barnes's favourite mare out hunting.
10111-8{_}3680{_}It was a dreary holiday; father and son were glad enough to get away from it, and to return to their own humbler quarters in London.
10111-8{_}3681{_}Thomas Newcome had now been for three years in the possession of that joy which his soul longed after, and yet in spite of his happiness, his honest face grew more melancholy, his loose clothes hung only the looser on his lean limbs; he ate his meals without appetite; his nights were restless and he would sit for hours silent, and was constantly finding business which took him to distant quarters of England.
10111-8{_}3682{_}Notwithstanding this change in him the Colonel insisted that he was perfectly happy and contented, but the truth was, his heart was aching with the knowledge that Clive had occupations, ideas, associates, in which the elder could take no interest.
10111-8{_}3683{_}Sitting in his blank, cheerless bedroom, Newcome could hear the lad and his friends making merry and breaking out in roars of laughter from time to time.
10111-8{_}3684{_}The Colonel longed to share in the merriment, but he knew that the party would be hushed if he joined it, that the younger men were happier and freer without him, and without laying any blame upon them for this natural state of affairs, it saddened the days and nights of our genial Colonel.
10111-8{_}3685{_}Clive, meanwhile, passed through the course of study prescribed by Mr. Gandish and drew every cast and statue in that gentleman's studio.
10111-8{_}3686{_}Grindley, his tutor, getting a curacy, Clive did not replace him, but took a course of modern languages, which he learned with great rapidity.
10111-8{_}3687{_}And now, being strong enough to paint without a master, Mr. Clive must needs have a studio, as there was no good light in the house in Fitzroy Square.
10111-8{_}3688{_}If his kind father felt any pang even at this temporary parting, he was greatly soothed and pleased by a little mark of attention on Clive's part.
10111-8{_}3689{_}He walked over with Colonel Newcome to see the new studio, with its tall centre window, and its curtains and hard wardrobes, china jars, pieces of armour, and other artistic properties, and with a very sweet smile of kindness and affection lighting up his honest face, took out a house-key and gave it to his father: "That's _your_ key, sir," he said to the Colonel; "and you must be my first sitter, please, father; for, though I am to be a historical painter, I shall condescend to do a few portraits, you know."
10111-8{_}3690{_}The Colonel grasped his son's hand as Clive fondly put the other hand on his father's shoulder.
10111-8{_}3691{_}Then Colonel Newcome walked away for a minute or two, and came back wiping his moustache with his handkerchief, and still holding the key in the other hand.
10111-8{_}3692{_}He spoke about some trivial subject when he returned; but his voice quite trembled, his face glowed with love and pleasure, and the little act of affection compensated him for many weary hours of solitude.
10111-8{_}3693{_}It is certain that Clive worked much better after he had this apartment of his own, and meals at home were gayer; and the rides with his father more frequent and agreeable.
10111-8{_}3694{_}The Colonel used his key not infrequently, and found Clive and his friend J. J. as a general thing absorbed in executing historical subjects on the largest possible canvases.
10111-8{_}3695{_}Meanwhile Colonel Newcome was preparing his mind to leave his idol, who he knew would be happy without as with him.
10111-8{_}3696{_}During the three years since he had come from India the Colonel had spent money lavishly and had also been obliged to pay dearly for some of Clive's boyish extravagances.
10111-8{_}3697{_}At first, the Colonel had thought he might retire from the army altogether, but experience showed him that he could not live upon his income.
10111-8{_}3698{_}He proposed now to return to India to get his promotion as full Colonel when the thousand a year to which that would entitle him, together with his other investments, would be ample for Clive and himself to live on.
10111-8{_}3699{_}While the Colonel's thoughts were absorbed in this matter his favourite Ethel was constantly away with her grandmother.
10111-8{_}3700{_}The Colonel went to see her at Brighton, and once, twice, thrice, Lady Kew's door was denied to him.
10111-8{_}3701{_}Once when the Colonel encountered his pretty Ethel with her riding master she greeted him affectionately, but when he rode up to her she looked so constrained, when he talked about Clive she was so reserved, when he left her, so sad, he could only feel pain and regret.
10111-8{_}3702{_}Back he went to London, having in a week only caught this single glance of his darling, but filled with determination to have a frank talk with his sister-in-law, Lady Ann, and if possible to mend the family disagreement and turn the tide of Lady Ann's affection again towards his son.
10111-8{_}3703{_}This he attempted to do, and would have succeeded had not Barnes Newcome been the head of the house.
10111-8{_}3704{_}As we know, his opinion of Clive was not to that young man's advantage.
10111-8{_}3705{_}These opinions were imparted to his Uncle Hobson at the bank, and Uncle Hobson carried them home to his wife, who took an early opportunity of repeating them to the Colonel, and the Colonel was brought to see that Barnes was his boy's enemy, and words very likely passed between them, for Thomas Newcome took a new banker at this time, and was very angry because Hobson Brothers wrote to him to say that he had overdrawn his account.
10111-8{_}3706{_}"I am sure there is some screw loose," remarked Clive to a friend, "and that my father and the people in Park Lane have disagreed, because he goes there very little now; and he promised to go to Court when Ethel was presented and he didn't go."
10111-8{_}3707{_}This state of affairs between the members of the Newcome family continued for some months.
10111-8{_}3708{_}Then, happily, a truce was declared, the quarrel between the Newcome brothers came to an end--for that time at least--and was followed by a rather showy reconciliation and a family dinner at Brianstone Square.
10111-8{_}3709{_}Everybody was bent upon being happy and gracious.
10111-8{_}3710{_}It was "My dear brother, how do you do?"
10111-8{_}3711{_}from Sir Brian.
10111-8{_}3712{_}"My dear Colonel, how glad we are to see you!
10111-8{_}3713{_}How well you look!"
10111-8{_}3714{_}from Lady Ann.
10111-8{_}3715{_}Ethel Newcome ran to him with both hands out, an eager welcome on her beautiful face.
10111-8{_}3716{_}And even Lady Kew held out her hand to Colonel Newcome, saying briskly: "Colonel, it is an age since we met," and turning to Clive with equal graciousness to say, "Mr. Clive, let me shake hands with you; I have heard all sorts of good of you, that you have been painting the most beautiful things, that you are going to be quite famous."
10111-8{_}3717{_}There was no doubt about it,--it was an evening of reconciliation on every side.
10111-8{_}3718{_}Ethel was so happy to see her dear uncle that she had no eyes for any one else, until Clive advancing, those bright eyes became brighter still as she saw him; and as she looked she saw a very handsome fellow, for Clive at that time was of the ornamental class of mankind--a customer to tailors, a wearer of handsome rings, shirt studs, long hair, and the like; nor could he help, in his costume or his nature, being picturesque, generous, and splendid.
10111-8{_}3719{_}Silver dressing cases and brocade morning gowns were in him a sort of propriety at this season of his youth.
10111-8{_}3720{_}It was a pleasure to persons of colder temperament to sun themselves in the warmth of his bright looks and generous humour.
10111-8{_}3721{_}His laughter cheered one like wine.
10111-8{_}3722{_}I do not know that he was very witty; but he was pleasant.
10111-8{_}3723{_}He was prone to blush; the history of a generous trait moistened his eyes instantly.
10111-8{_}3724{_}He was instinctively fond of children and of the other sex from one year old to eighty.
10111-8{_}3725{_}Coming from the Derby once and being stopped on the road in a lock of carriages during which the people in a carriage ahead saluted us with many insulting epithets, and seized the heads of our leaders, Clive in a twinkling jumped off the box, and the next minute we saw him engaged with a half dozen of the enemy: his hat gone, his fair hair falling off his face, his blue eyes flashing fire, his lips and nostrils quivering with wrath.
10111-8{_}3726{_}His father sat back in the carriage looking on with delight and wonder while a policeman separated the warriors.
10111-8{_}3727{_}Clive ascended the box again, with his coat gashed from waist to shoulder.
10111-8{_}3728{_}I hardly ever saw the elder Newcome in such a state of triumph.
10111-8{_}3729{_}While we have been making this sketch of Clive, Ethel was standing looking at him, and the blushing youth cast down his eyes before hers while her face assumed a look of arch humour.
10111-8{_}3730{_}And now let us have a likeness of Ethel.
10111-8{_}3731{_}She was seventeen years old; rather taller than the majority of girls; her face somewhat grave and haughty, but on occasion brightening with humour or beaming with kindliness and affection.
10111-8{_}3732{_}Too quick to detect affectation or insincerity in others, too impatient of dulness or pomposity, she was more sarcastic now than she became when after-years of suffering had softened her nature.
10111-8{_}3733{_}Truth looked out of her bright eyes, and rose up armed and flashed scorn or denial when she encountered flattery or meanness or imposture.
10111-8{_}3734{_}But those who had no cause to fear her keenness or her coldness admired her beauty; nor could the famous Parisian model whom Clive said she resembled be more perfect in form than this young lady.
10111-8{_}3735{_}Her hair and eyebrows were jet black, but her complexion was dazzlingly fair and her cheeks as red as those belonging by right to a blonde.
10111-8{_}3736{_}In her black hair there was a slight natural ripple.
10111-8{_}3737{_}Her eyes were grey; her mouth rather large; her teeth were regular and white, her voice was low and sweet; and her smile, when it lighted up her face and eyes, as beautiful as spring sunshine; also her eyes could lighten and flash often, and sometimes, though rarely, rain.
10111-8{_}3738{_}As for her figure, the tall, slender form clad in a simple white muslin robe in which her fair arms were enveloped, and which was caught at her slim waist by a blue ribbon, let us make a respectful bow to that fair image of youth, health, and modesty, and fancy it as pretty as we will.
10111-8{_}3739{_}Not yet overshadowed by the cloud of Colonel Newcome's departure, light-hearted in the joy of reconciliation and meeting, once again full of high spirits and mindful of no moment beyond the present, the two cousins never looked brighter or happier, and as Colonel Newcome gazed upon them in the freshness of their youth and vigour his heart was filled with delight.
10111-8{_}3740{_}Not many days after the dinner the good Colonel found it necessary to break the news of his intended departure to Clive.
10111-8{_}3741{_}His resolution to go being taken, and having been obliged to dip somewhat deeply into the little purse he had set aside for European expenses to help a kinsman in distress, the Colonel's departure came somewhat sooner than he had expected.
10111-8{_}3742{_}But, as he said, "A year sooner or later, what does it matter?
10111-8{_}3743{_}Clive will go away and work at his art, and see the great schools of painting while I am absent.
10111-8{_}3744{_}I thought at one time how pleasant it would be to accompany him.
10111-8{_}3745{_}I fancy now a lad is not the better for being always tied to his parents' apron-strings.
10111-8{_}3746{_}You young fellows are too clever for me.
10111-8{_}3747{_}I haven't learned your ideas or read your books.
10111-8{_}3748{_}I feel myself very often an old damper in your company.
10111-8{_}3749{_}I will go back, sir, where I have some friends, and where I am somebody still.
10111-8{_}3750{_}I know an honest face or two, white and brown, that will lighten up in the old regiment when they see Tom Newcome again."
10111-8{_}3751{_}With this resolution taken, the Colonel began saying farewell to his friends.
10111-8{_}3752{_}He and Clive made a pilgrimage to Grey Friars; and the Colonel ran down to Newcome to give Mrs. Mason a parting benediction; went to all the boys' and girls' schools where his little protégés were, so as to be able to take the very latest account of the young folks to their parents in India; and thence proceeded to Brighton to pass a little time with good Miss Honeyman.
10111-8{_}3753{_}With Sir Brian's family he parted on very good terms.
10111-8{_}3754{_}I believe Sir Brian even accompanied him downstairs from the drawing-room in Park Lane, and actually saw his brother into his cab, but as for Ethel, _she_ was not going to be put off with this sort of parting; and the next morning a cab dashed up to Fitzroy Square and she was closeted with Colonel Newcome for five minutes, and when he led her back to the carriage there were tears in his eyes.
10111-8{_}3755{_}Then came the day when Clive and his father travelled together to Southampton, where a group of the Colonel's faithful friends were assembled to say a "God bless you" to their dear old friend, and see the vessel sail.
10111-8{_}3756{_}To the end Clive remained with his father and went below with him, and when the last bell was ringing, came from below looking very pale.
10111-8{_}3757{_}The plank was drawn after him almost as soon as he stepped on land, and the vessel had sailed.
10111-8{_}3758{_}Although Thomas Newcome had gone back to India in search of more money, he was nevertheless rather a wealthy man and was able to leave a hundred a year in England to be transferred to his boy as soon as he came of age.
10111-8{_}3759{_}He also left a considerable annual sum to be paid to the boy, and so as soon as the parting was over and his affairs were settled, Clive was free to start on his travels, to study art in new lands, accompanied by his faithful friend J.J.
10111-8{_}3760{_}They went first to Antwerp; thence to Brussels, and next Clive's correspondents received a letter from Bonn: in which Master Clive said, "And whom should I find here but Aunt Ann, Ethel, Miss Quigley and the little ones.
10111-8{_}3761{_}Uncle Brian is staying at Aix, and, upon my conscience, I think my pretty cousin looks prettier every day.
10111-8{_}3762{_}J.J. and I were climbing a little hill which leads to a ruin, when I heard a little voice cry, 'Hello!
10111-8{_}3763{_}it's Clive!
10111-8{_}3764{_}Hooray, Clive,' and an ass came down the incline with a little pair of white trousers at an immensely wide angle over the donkey's back, and there was little Alfred grinning with all his might.
10111-8{_}3765{_}"He turned his beast and was for galloping up the hill again, I suppose to inform his relations; but the donkey refused with many kicks, one of which sent Alfred plunging amongst the stones, and we were rubbing him down just as the rest of the party came upon us.
10111-8{_}3766{_}Miss Quigley looked very grim on an old white pony; my aunt was on a black horse that might have turned grey, he is so old.
10111-8{_}3767{_}Then came two donkeys-full of children, with Kuhn as supercargo; then Ethel on donkey back, too, with a bunch of wild flowers in her hand, a great straw hat with a crimson ribbon, a white muslin jacket, you know, bound at the waist with a ribbon of the first, and a dark skirt, with a shawl round her feet, which Kuhn had arranged.
10111-8{_}3768{_}As she stopped, the donkey fell to cropping greens in the hedge; the trees there chequered her white dress and face with shadow.
10111-8{_}3769{_}Her eyes, hair, and forehead were in shadow, too, but the light was all upon her right cheek.
10111-8{_}3770{_}Upon her shoulder down to her arm, which was of a warmer white, and on the bunch of flowers which she held, blue, yellow, and red poppies, and so forth.
10111-8{_}3771{_}"J. J. says, 'I think the birds began to sing louder when she came.'
10111-8{_}3772{_}We have both agreed that she is the handsomest woman in England.
10111-8{_}3773{_}It's not her form merely, which is certainly as yet too thin and a little angular; it is her colour.
10111-8{_}3774{_}I do not care for women or pictures without colour.
10111-8{_}3775{_}Oh, ye carnations!
10111-8{_}3776{_}Oh, such black hair and solemn eyebrows.
10111-8{_}3777{_}It seems to me the roses and carnations have bloomed again since we saw them last in London, when they were drooping from the exposure to night air, candle light, and heated ballrooms.
10111-8{_}3778{_}"Here I was in the midst of a regiment of donkeys bearing a crowd of relations; J. J. standing modestly in the background, beggars completing the group.
10111-8{_}3779{_}Throw in the Rhine in the distance flashing by the Seven Mountains--but mind and make Ethel the principal figure: if you make her like she certainly _will_ be, and other lights will be only minor fires.
10111-8{_}3780{_}You may paint her form, but can't paint her colour."
10111-8{_}3781{_}Thus wrote Clive from Bonn, and now that the old Countess and Barnes were away, the barrier between Clive and this family was withdrawn.
10111-8{_}3782{_}The young folks who loved him were free to see him as often as he would come.
10111-8{_}3783{_}They were going to Baden: would he come, too?
10111-8{_}3784{_}He was glad enough to go with them, and to travel in the orbit of such a lovely girl as Ethel Newcome, whose beauty made all the passengers on all the steamers look round and admire.
10111-8{_}3785{_}The journey was all sunshine and pleasure and novelty; and I like to think of the pretty girl and the gallant young fellow enjoying this holiday.
10111-8{_}3786{_}Few sights are more pleasant than to watch a happy, manly English youth, freehanded and generous-hearted, content and good-humour shining in his honest face, pleased and pleasing, eager, active, and thankful for services, and exercising bravely his noble youthful privilege to be happy and to enjoy.
10111-8{_}3787{_}As for J. J., he, too, had his share of enjoyment.
10111-8{_}3788{_}Clive was still his hero as ever, his patron, his splendid young prince and chieftain.
10111-8{_}3789{_}Who was so brave, who was so handsome, generous, witty as Clive?
10111-8{_}3790{_}To hear Clive sing, as the lad would whilst they were seated at their work, or driving along on this happy journey, through fair landscapes in the sunshine, gave J. J. the keenest pleasure; his wit was a little slow, but he would laugh with his eyes at Clive's sallies, or ponder over them and explode with laughter presently, giving a new source of amusement to these merry travellers, and little Alfred would laugh at J.J.'s laughing; and so, with a hundred harmless jokes to enliven, and the ever-changing, ever-charming smiles of Nature to cheer and accompany it, the happy day's journey would come to an end.
10111-8{_}3791{_}So they travelled by the accustomed route to the prettiest town of all places where Pleasure has set up her tents, and there enjoyed themselves to the fullest extent.
10111-8{_}3792{_}Among Colonel Newcome's papers to which the family biographer has had access, there are a couple of letters from Clive, dated Baden this time, and full of happiness, gaiety, and affection.
10111-8{_}3793{_}Letter No.
10111-8{_}3794{_}1 says: "Ethel is the prettiest girl here.
10111-8{_}3795{_}At the Assemblies all the princes, counts, dukes, etc., are dying to dance with her.
10111-8{_}3796{_}She sends her dearest love to her uncle."
10111-8{_}3797{_}By the side of the words "Prettiest girl" are written in a frank female hand the monosyllable "_stuff_"; and as a note to the expression "dearest love," with a star to mark the text and the note, are squeezed in the same feminine characters at the bottom of Clive's page the words "_that I do_.
10111-8{_}3798{_}E. N."  In letter No.
10111-8{_}3799{_}2, Clive, after giving amusing details of life at Baden and the company whom he met there, concludes with this: "Ethel is looking over my shoulder.
10111-8{_}3800{_}She thinks me such a delightful creature that she is never easy without me.
10111-8{_}3801{_}She bids me to say that I am the best of sons and cousins, and am, in a word, a darling du--" The rest of this important word is not given, but "_goose_" is added in the female hand.
10111-8{_}3802{_}Ethel takes up the pen.
10111-8{_}3803{_}"My dear uncle," she says, "while Clive is sketching out of the window, let me write to you a line or two on his paper, _though I know you like to hear no one speak_ but him.
10111-8{_}3804{_}I wish I could draw him for you as he stands yonder looking the picture of good health, good spirits, and good-humour.
10111-8{_}3805{_}Everybody likes him.
10111-8{_}3806{_}He is quite unaffected; always gay, always pleased, and he draws more beautifully every day."
10111-8{_}3807{_}When these letters were received by the good Colonel in India we can well imagine the joy that warmed his fond heart.
10111-8{_}3808{_}He, himself, was comfortably settled in the only place which would ever be home to him,--his son, the idol of his heart, was with Ethel, his darling.
10111-8{_}3809{_}The objects of his tenderest affection were gay, happy, together, and, best of all, thinking of him.
10111-8{_}3810{_}That he was not with them gave him no regrets; his love was too great for that.
10111-8{_}3811{_}That their youth was soon to give place to the soberer experiences of life, gave him no pang of fear for them.
10111-8{_}3812{_}Reading their letters, the Colonel was filled with quiet contentment; their future he could trust to the care of that Guiding Hand to whom he had entrusted his boy in childhood's earliest days.
10111-8{_}3813{_}ARTHUR PENDENNIS   [Illustration: ARTHUR PENDENNIS AT FAIR-OAKS.]
10111-8{_}3814{_}Early in the Regency of George the Magnificent there lived in a small town in the west of England, called Clavering, a gentleman whose name was Pendennis.
10111-8{_}3815{_}At an earlier date Mr. Pendennis had exercised the profession of apothecary and surgeon, and had even condescended to sell a plaster across the counter of his humble shop, or to vend tooth-brushes, hair-powder, and London perfumery.
10111-8{_}3816{_}And yet that little apothecary was a gentleman with good education, and of as old a family as any in the county of Somerset.
10111-8{_}3817{_}He had a Cornish pedigree which carried the Pendennises back to the time of the Druids.
10111-8{_}3818{_}He had had a piece of University education, and might have pursued that career with honour, but in his second year at Oxford his father died insolvent, and he was obliged to betake himself to the trade which he always detested.
10111-8{_}3819{_}For some time he had a hard struggle with poverty, but his manners were so gentleman-like and soothing that he was called in to prescribe for some of the ladies in the best families of Bath.
10111-8{_}3820{_}Then his humble little shop became a smart one; then he shut it up altogether; then he had a gig with a man to drive in; and before she died his poor old mother had the happiness of seeing her beloved son step into a close carriage of his own; with the arms of the family of Pendennis handsomely emblazoned on the panels.
10111-8{_}3821{_}He married Miss Helen Thistlewood, a very distant relative of the noble family of Bareacres, having met that young lady under Lady Pentypool's roof.
10111-8{_}3822{_}The secret ambition of Mr. Pendennis had always been to be a gentleman.
10111-8{_}3823{_}By prudence and economy, his income was largely increased, and finally he sold his business for a handsome sum, and retired forever from handling of the mortar and pestle, having purchased as a home the house of Fair-Oaks, nearly a mile out of Clavering.
10111-8{_}3824{_}The estate was a beautiful one, and Arthur Pendennis, his son, being then but eight years of age, dated his earliest recollections from that place.
10111-8{_}3825{_}Fair-Oaks lawn comes down to the little river Brawl, and on the other side were the plantations and woods of Clavering Park.
10111-8{_}3826{_}The park was let out in pasture when the Pendennises came first to live at Fair-Oaks.
10111-8{_}3827{_}Shutters were up in the house; a splendid free stone palace, with great stairs, statues and porticos.
10111-8{_}3828{_}Sir Richard Clavering, Sir Francis's grandfather, had commenced the ruin of the family by the building of this palace: his successor had achieved the ruin by living in it.
10111-8{_}3829{_}The present Sir Francis was abroad somewhere, and until now nobody could be found rich enough to rent that enormous mansion; through the deserted rooms, mouldy, clanking halls, and dismal galleries of which Arthur Pendennis many a time walked trembling when he was a boy.
10111-8{_}3830{_}At sunset from the lawn of Fair-Oaks there was a pretty sight: it and the opposite park of Clavering were in the habit of putting on a rich golden tinge, which became them both wonderfully.
10111-8{_}3831{_}The upper windows of the great house flamed so as to make your eyes wink; the little river ran off noisily westward and was lost in sombre wood, behind which the towers of the old abbey church of Clavering (whereby that town is called Clavering St. Mary's to the present day) rose up in purple splendour.
10111-8{_}3832{_}Little Arthur's figure and his mother's cast long blue shadows over the grass: and he would repeat in a low voice (for a scene of great natural beauty always moved the boy, who inherited this sensibility from his mother) certain lines beginning, "These are thy glorious works.
10111-8{_}3833{_}Parent of Good; Almighty!
10111-8{_}3834{_}thine this universal frame," greatly to Mrs. Pendennis's delight.
10111-8{_}3835{_}Such walks and conversation generally ended in a profusion of filial and maternal embraces; for to love and to pray were the main occupations of this dear woman's life; and I have often heard Pendennis say in his wild way, that he felt that he was sure of going to heaven, for his mother never could be happy there without him.
10111-8{_}3836{_}As for John Pendennis, as the father of the family, and that sort of thing, everybody had the greatest respect for him: and his orders were obeyed like those of the Medes and Persians.
10111-8{_}3837{_}His hat was as well brushed perhaps as that of any man in this empire.
10111-8{_}3838{_}His meals were served at the same minute every day, and woe to those who came late, as little Pen, a disorderly little rascal, sometimes did.
10111-8{_}3839{_}Prayers were recited, his letters were read, his business despatched, his stables and garden inspected, his hen-houses and kennel, his barn and pig-sty visited, always at regular hours.
10111-8{_}3840{_}After dinner he always had a nap with the Globe newspaper on his knee, and his yellow bandanna handkerchief on his face.
10111-8{_}3841{_}And so, as his dinner took place at six o'clock to a minute, and the sunset business alluded to may be supposed to have occurred at half-past seven, it is probable that he did not much care for the view in front of his lawn windows, or take any share in the poetry and caresses which were taking place there.
10111-8{_}3842{_}They seldom occurred in his presence.
10111-8{_}3843{_}However frisky they were before, mother and child were hushed and quiet when Mr. Pendennis walked into the drawing-room, his newspaper under his arm.
10111-8{_}3844{_}And here, while little Pen, buried in a great chair, read all the books on which he could lay hold, the Squire perused his own articles in the Gardener's Gazette, or took a solemn hand at piquet with Mrs. Pendennis, or an occasional friend from the village.
10111-8{_}3845{_}As for Mrs. Pendennis, she was conspicuous for her tranquil beauty, her natural sweetness and kindness, and that simplicity and dignity which purity and innocence are sure to bestow upon a handsome woman, and during her son's childhood and youth the boy thought of her as little less than an angel, a supernatural being, all wisdom, love and beauty.
10111-8{_}3846{_}But Mrs. Pendennis had one weakness,--pride of family.
10111-8{_}3847{_}She spoke of Mr. Pendennis as if he had been the Pope of Rome on his throne, and she a cardinal kneeling at his feet, and giving him incense.
10111-8{_}3848{_}Mr. Pendennis's brother, the Major, she held to be a sort of Bayard among Majors, and as for her son Arthur, she worshipped that youth with an ardour which the young scapegrace accepted almost as coolly as the statue of the saint in St. Peter's receives the rapturous kisses which the faithful deliver on his toe.
10111-8{_}3849{_}Notwithstanding his mother's worship of him, Arthur Pendennis's school-fellows at the Grey Friars School state that as a boy he was in no way remarkable either as a dunce or as a scholar.
10111-8{_}3850{_}He never read to improve himself out of school-hours, but on the contrary devoured all the novels, plays and poetry he could get hold of.
10111-8{_}3851{_}He never was flogged, but it was a wonder how he escaped the whippingpost.
10111-8{_}3852{_}When he had money he spent it royally in tarts for himself and his friends, and had been known to disburse nine and sixpence out of ten shillings awarded to him in a single day.
10111-8{_}3853{_}When he had no funds he went on tick.
10111-8{_}3854{_}When he could get no credit he went without, and was almost as happy.
10111-8{_}3855{_}He had been known to take a thrashing for a crony without saying a word; but a blow ever so slight from a friend would make him roar.
10111-8{_}3856{_}To fighting he was averse from his earliest youth, and indeed to physic, the Greek Grammar, or any other exertion, and would engage in none of them, except at the last extremity.
10111-8{_}3857{_}He seldom if ever told lies, and never bullied little boys.
10111-8{_}3858{_}Those masters or seniors who were kind to him, he loved with boyish ardour.
10111-8{_}3859{_}And though the Doctor, when he did not know his Horace, or could not construe his Greek play, said that that boy Pendennis was a disgrace to the school, a candidate for ruin in this world, and perdition in the next; a profligate who would most likely bring his venerable father to ruin and his mother to a dishonoured grave, and the like--yet as the Doctor made use of these compliments to most of the boys in the place, little Pen, at first uneasy and terrified by these charges, became gradually accustomed to hear them; and he has not, in fact, either murdered his parents or committed any act worthy of transportation or hanging up to the present day.
10111-8{_}3860{_}Thus with various diversions and occupations his school days passed until he was about sixteen years old, when he was suddenly called away from his academic studies.
10111-8{_}3861{_}It was at the close of the forenoon school, and Pen had been unnoticed all the previous part of the morning till now, when the Doctor put him on to construe in a Greek play.
10111-8{_}3862{_}He did not know a word of it, though little Timmins, his form-fellow, was prompting him with all his might.
10111-8{_}3863{_}Pen had made a sad blunder or two, when the awful chief broke out upon him.
10111-8{_}3864{_}"Pendennis, sir," he said, "your idleness is incorrigible and your stupidity beyond example.
10111-8{_}3865{_}You are a disgrace to your school, and to your family, and I have no doubt will prove so in after-life to your country.
10111-8{_}3866{_}If that vice, sir, which is described to us as the root of all evil, be really what moralists have represented, what a prodigious quantity of future crime and wickedness are you, unhappy boy, laying the seed!
10111-8{_}3867{_}Miserable trifler!
10111-8{_}3868{_}A boy, sir, who does not learn his Greek play cheats the parent who spends money for his education.
10111-8{_}3869{_}A boy who cheats his parent is not very far from robbing or forging upon his neighbour.
10111-8{_}3870{_}A man who forges on his neighbour pays the penalty of his crime at the gallows.
10111-8{_}3871{_}And it is not such a one that I pity, for he will be deservedly cut off, but his maddened and heartbroken parents, who are driven to a premature grave by his crimes, or, if they live, drag on a wretched and dishonoured old age.
10111-8{_}3872{_}Go on, sir, and I warn you that the very next mistake that you make shall subject you to the punishment of the rod.
10111-8{_}3873{_}Who's that laughing?
10111-8{_}3874{_}What ill-conditioned boy is there that dares to laugh?"
10111-8{_}3875{_}shouted the Doctor.
10111-8{_}3876{_}Indeed, while the master was making this oration, there was a general titter behind him in the schoolroom.
10111-8{_}3877{_}The orator had his back to the door of this ancient apartment, which was open, and a gentleman who was quite familiar with the place (for both Major Arthur, Pen's uncle, and Mr. John Pendennis had been at the school) was asking the fifth-form boy who sat by the door for Pendennis.
10111-8{_}3878{_}The lad, grinning, pointed to the culprit against whom the Doctor was pouring out the thunders of his just wrath.
10111-8{_}3879{_}Major Pendennis could not help laughing.
10111-8{_}3880{_}He remembered having stood under that very pillar where Pen the younger now stood, and having been assaulted by the Doctor's predecessor years and years ago.
10111-8{_}3881{_}The intelligence was "passed round" in an instant that it was Pendennis's uncle, and a hundred young faces, wondering and giggling, between terror and laughter, turned now to the newcomer and then to the awful Doctor.
10111-8{_}3882{_}The Major asked the fifth-form boy to carry his card up to the Doctor, which the lad did with an arch look.
10111-8{_}3883{_}Major Pendennis had written on the card: "I must take A.P.
10111-8{_}3884{_}home; his father is very ill."  As the Doctor received the card, and stopped his harangue with rather a scared look, the laughter of the boys, half constrained until then, burst out in a general shout.
10111-8{_}3885{_}"Silence!"
10111-8{_}3886{_}roared out the Doctor, stamping with his foot.
10111-8{_}3887{_}Pen looked up and saw who was his deliverer; the Major beckoned to him gravely, and, tumbling down his books, Pen went across.
10111-8{_}3888{_}The Doctor took out his watch.
10111-8{_}3889{_}It was two minutes to one.
10111-8{_}3890{_}"We will take the Juvenal at afternoon school," he said, nodding to the Captain, and all the boys, understanding the signal, gathered up their books and poured out of the hall.
10111-8{_}3891{_}Young Pen saw by his uncle's face that something had happened at home.
10111-8{_}3892{_}"Is there anything the matter with--my mother?"
10111-8{_}3893{_}he said.
10111-8{_}3894{_}He could hardly speak for emotion and the tears which were ready to start.
10111-8{_}3895{_}"No," said the Major, "but your father's very ill. Go and pack your trunk directly; I have got a post-chaise at the gate."
10111-8{_}3896{_}Pen went off quickly to his boarding-house to do as his uncle bade him; and the Doctor, now left alone in the schoolroom, came out to shake hands with the Major.
10111-8{_}3897{_}"There is nothing serious, I hope," said the Doctor.
10111-8{_}3898{_}"It is a pity to take the boy otherwise.
10111-8{_}3899{_}He is a good boy, rather idle and unenergetic, but an honest, gentleman-like little fellow, though I can't get him to construe as I wish.
10111-8{_}3900{_}Won't you come in and have some luncheon?
10111-8{_}3901{_}My wife will be very happy to see you."
10111-8{_}3902{_}But Major Pendennis declined the luncheon.
10111-8{_}3903{_}He said his brother was very ill, and had had a fit the day before, and it was a great question if they should see him alive.
10111-8{_}3904{_}"There's no other son, is there?"
10111-8{_}3905{_}said the Doctor.
10111-8{_}3906{_}The Major answered "No."
10111-8{_}3907{_}"And there's a good eh--a good eh--property, I believe?"
10111-8{_}3908{_}asked the other in an off-hand way.
10111-8{_}3909{_}"H'm--so-so," said the Major.
10111-8{_}3910{_}Whereupon this colloquy came to an end.
10111-8{_}3911{_}And Arthur Pendennis got into a post-chaise with his uncle, never to come back to school any more.
10111-8{_}3912{_}As the chaise drove through Clavering, the ostler standing whistling under the archway of the Clavering Arms winked to the postilion ominously, as much as to say all was over.
10111-8{_}3913{_}The gardener's wife came and opened the lodge-gates and let the travellers through with a silent shake of the head.
10111-8{_}3914{_}All the blinds were down at Fair-Oaks; and the face of the old footman was as blank when he let them in.
10111-8{_}3915{_}Arthur's face was white, too, with terror more than with grief.
10111-8{_}3916{_}Whatever of warmth and love the deceased man might have had, and he adored his wife, and loved and admired his son with all his heart, he had shut them up within himself; nor had the boy ever been able to penetrate that frigid outward barrier.
10111-8{_}3917{_}A little girl, who was Mrs. Pendennis's adopted daughter, the child of a dear old friend, peered for a moment under the blinds as the chaise came up, opened the door from the stairs into the hall, and there taking Arthur's hand silently as he stooped down to kiss her, led him upstairs to his mother.
10111-8{_}3918{_}What passed between that lady and the boy is not of import; a veil should be thrown over those sacred emotions of love and grief.
10111-8{_}3919{_}As for Arthur Pendennis, after that awful shock which the sight of his dead father must have produced on him, and the pity and feeling which such an event no doubt occasioned, I am not sure that in the very moment of the grief, and as he embraced his mother and tenderly consoled her and promised to love her forever, there was not springing up in his breast a sort of secret triumph and exultation.
10111-8{_}3920{_}He was the chief now and lord.
10111-8{_}3921{_}He was Pendennis; and all round about him were his servants and handmaids.
10111-8{_}3922{_}"You'll never send me away," little Laura said, tripping by him and holding his hand.
10111-8{_}3923{_}"You won't send me to school, will you, Arthur?"
10111-8{_}3924{_}Arthur kissed her and patted her head.
10111-8{_}3925{_}No, she shouldn't go to school.
10111-8{_}3926{_}As for going himself that was quite out of the question.
10111-8{_}3927{_}He had determined that his life should be all holidays for the future; that he wouldn't get up till he liked, or stand the bullying of the Doctor any more; and made a hundred such day-dreams and resolves for the future.
10111-8{_}3928{_}Then in due time they buried John Pendennis, Esquire, in the Abbey Church of Clavering St. Mary's, and Arthur Pendennis reigned in his stead.
10111-8{_}3929{_}Arthur was about sixteen years old when he began to reign; in person he had what his friends would call a dumpy, but his mamma styled, a neat little figure.
10111-8{_}3930{_}His hair was of a healthy brown colour, which looked like gold in the sunshine.
10111-8{_}3931{_}His face was round, rosy, freckled, and good-humoured.
10111-8{_}3932{_}In fact, without being a beauty, he had such a frank, good-natured, kind face and laughed so merrily at you out of his honest blue eyes that no wonder Mrs. Pendennis thought him the pride of the whole country.
10111-8{_}3933{_}You may be certain he never went back to school; the discipline of the establishment did not suit him, and he liked being at home much better.
10111-8{_}3934{_}The question of his return was debated, and his uncle was for his going back.
10111-8{_}3935{_}The Doctor wrote his opinion that it was most important for Arthur's success in after life that he should know a Greek play thoroughly, but Pen adroitly managed to hint to his mother what a dangerous place Grey Friars was, and what sad wild fellows some of the chaps there were, and the timid soul, taking alarm at once, acceded to his desire to stay at home.
10111-8{_}3936{_}Then Pen's uncle offered to use his influence with his Royal Highness, the Commander-in-Chief, to get Pen a commission in the Foot Guards.
10111-8{_}3937{_}Pen's heart leaped at this: he had been to hear the band at St. James's play on a Sunday, when he went out to his uncle.
10111-8{_}3938{_}He had seen Tom Ricketts, of the fourth form, who used to wear a jacket and trousers so ludicrously tight that the elder boys could not forbear using him in the quality of a butt or "cockshy"--he had seen this very Ricketts arrayed in crimson and gold, with an immense bearskin cap on his head, staggering under the colours of the regiment.
10111-8{_}3939{_}Tom had recognised him and gave him a patronising nod--Tom, a little wretch whom he had cut over the back with a hockey-stick last quarter, and there he was in the centre of the square, rallying round the flag of his county, surrounded by bayonets, cross-belts, and scarlet, the band blowing trumpets and banging cymbals--talking familiarly to immense warriors with tufts to their chins and Waterloo medals.
10111-8{_}3940{_}What would not Pen have given to enter such a service?
